[1] Chapter 1: Aroo's Christmas
[2] The story begins at a crossroads, half a mile outside the small West Virginia village of Aroo, where one road leads from New Kensington to Pietown [1], and another leads to Aroo.
[3] Ellery Queen immediately realized that the terrain was important. His first glance revealed many other things, but only found evidence conflicting and confusing. Everything seemed out of place, and it seemed necessary to leave the crossroads and think things over more carefully.
[4] Ellery Queen, the erudite and cosmopolitan man, found himself standing in the cold and gloomy weather of the West Virginia handle region [2] at two o'clock in the afternoon on December 26th, next to an old Duesenberg [3] race car. This unusual phenomenon was caused by a combination of factors, with the most important being his father, Inspector Quin, who advocated working on holidays. The elderly man was deeply engrossed in his daily work, the so-called "police customs," and worked tirelessly. The crime situation in Chicago was always dire, and the police commissioner invited the top police officers from major cities to his jurisdiction to commiserate over the terrible state of affairs.
[5] It was during the trip with his father, the Inspector, from the hotel to the Chicago Police Headquarters that Ellery learned about the perplexing case in Aroo, which was dubbed the "T-shaped murder" by the United Press. There were many elements in the report that piqued Ellery's interest - such as the fact that Andrew Van was decapitated and nailed to a cross on Christmas morning - that prompted him to pull his father out of the smoky Chicago meeting and drive off in a secondhand, fast Duesenberg towards the east.
[6] Although the Inspector complied with his son, his good mood was gone. From Chicago, passing through Toledo, Sandusky, Cleveland, Lorain, and Licking, they finally arrived at Chester in West Virginia. Along the way, the old man maintained a threatening silence, only occasionally interrupted by Ellery's witty remarks and the exhaust from the Duesenberg.
[7] Unknowingly, they passed Aroo, a small town of about two hundred people, and then...
[8] Before the car came to a stop, they saw the bare silhouette of the signpost at a distance. The end of Aroo Road was there, intersecting at a right angle with the New Kensington-Pietown Road. So the signpost faced the exit to Aroo Peak, with one arm pointing northeast to Pietown and the other arm pointing southwest to New Kensington.
[9] The Inspector growled softly, "Go on, you fool, you're making a fool of yourself! Dragging me here... for another crazy murder... I won't do it."
[10] Ellery turned off the engine and stepped out of the car, walking along the road with no one in sight. The mountains of West Virginia stood majestically, and the earth underfoot was dry and cracked. The weather was cold, and a sharp wind blew Ellery's coat fluttering. The signpost stood in front of him, with the cranky Aroo school principal, Andrew Van, nailed to it in a cross shape.
[11] The sign used to be white, but now it was dirty gray, with mud stains intersecting each other. It was six feet tall, and its top was level with Ellery's head. Ellery stood a few steps away, feeling that anyone looking at it would see a giant letter T. It was only then that he understood why the reporter from the United Press gave this crime the name "T-shaped murder" – first, the sign was in the shape of a T, second, the sign was located at a T-shaped intersection, and finally, there was a strange T-shaped mark on the door of the victim's house, which Ellery saw when he drove past the T-shaped crossroads a few hundred feet away.
[12] Ellery sighed and took off his hat. This was not a gesture of respect, but rather a sign that despite the cold and strong wind, he was sweating. He wiped his forehead with a handkerchief, wondering what kind of psychopath would commit such a cruel, senseless, and completely baffling crime. Even the corpse… he vividly recalled a report on the discovery of the body in a famous Chicago newspaper, written by a reporter who was very adept at describing violence:
[13] The most pitiful Christmas story of the year was revealed today: At dawn on Christmas morning, the headless body of Andrew Van, the 46-year-old principal of a small village in West Virginia, was found nailed to a lonely crossroads sign in the village.
[14] Four-inch nails were driven into the victim's open palms, piercing through the palms and into the weather-beaten arms of the sign. Another two nails pierced the victim's ankles, bringing them together and fixing them to the bottom of the signpost. Two more nails were driven into the armpits, supporting the victim's weight. The victim's head was cut off, and it looked like nothing more than a giant letter T.
[15] The sign formed a T; the intersection formed a T; and a T-shaped mark was hastily drawn on the door of the victim's house, not far from the intersection. It was a crazy idea to form a T with a person.
[16] The local police were at a loss. Van was eccentric but a quiet, harmless man. He had no enemies and no friends. His only friend was a simple man named Clinton, who had been playing the role of Van's servant. Clinton had disappeared, and according to the evidence that was not made public, the district attorney, Crummett of Hampshire County, believed that this servant was another victim of the bloodthirsty maniac who had written a chapter in the modern American crime chronicle.
[17] There were more similar reports, including details of the unfortunate principal's rural life in Arroyo, bits of information gathered by the police about Van and Clinton's last known whereabouts, and the district attorney's exaggerated announcement.
[18] Ellery took off his glasses, wiped them, and put them back on, scanning the terrible relics left at the scene with sharp eyes.
[19] Near the top of the signpost's arms, there were many small holes left by the police who had dug out the nails. Each hole was surrounded by an irregular rust-brown stain, and thin brown streaks slowly seeped out of the hole, which were the blood that had flowed from the victim's injured hands. There were two more holes on the signpost's arms that extended out, with edges that were not clearly visible, left by the nails that had been driven in to support the body in the armpits. The entire signpost was covered in dried blood, forming a crisscross pattern, very dirty. This blood flowed down from the victim's neck, which was cracked near the top of the signpost.
[20] Near the bottom of the signpost, there were two holes that were no more than four inches apart, with edges that were also stained brown with blood. These holes were left by the victim's ankles being nailed to the wood, and the blood flowed down to the ground where the signpost was standing.
[21] Ellery returned to the car with a serious expression. The officer sat slumped in the passenger seat, leaning against the leather chair with a familiar air of frustration and anger. The old man wrapped his neck in an old wool scarf and raised his sharp red nose, as if to send a dangerous signal. "Oh," he snapped, "hurry up, I'm freezing."
[22] "You're not curious at all?" Ellery asked, sliding into the driver's seat.
[23] "Of course!"
[24] "You're not like a normal person," Ellery started the engine. He smiled, and the car leaped forward like a greyhound, its wheels spinning, pressing into the ground and then bouncing up, and the car spinning around and then speeding back along the road to Arroyo.
[25] The officer clung tightly to the seat edge, gripped by fear of death.
[26] "What an odd idea," Ellery shouted over the engine's roar, "nailing people to crosses at Christmas!"
[27] "Hey!" the officer said.
[28] "I think," Ellery yelled, "I'm going to like this case!"
[29] "Drive carefully, you idiot!" the old man shrieked, and the car straightened out. "You're not going to like anything," he added, his face flushed with anger. "You're coming back to New York with me."
[30] They sped into Arroyo.
[31] "You know," as Ellery abruptly stopped the Duesenberg in front of a low-lying structure, "they do things here in a disgraceful way," Quinlan muttered. He shook his head, his sharp gray head tilted to one side, "where are you going now?"
[32] "I thought you weren't interested," Ellery said, jumping off the sidewalk. "Hey, come here!" he called to a country bumpkin wrapped in a blue work suit, sweeping the sidewalk with a worn-out broom. "Is the sheriff of Arroyo here?" The man stared at him in disbelief. "Of course, it's written on the sign… come on, you crook."
[33] It was a dreary residential area with few buildings. The Duesenberg stopped in front of a building whose skeletal structure was made of shoddy wood, like a box for growing mushrooms in the past. Next to it was a general store with an old gas pump in front, and then a car repair shop. A handwritten sign hung proudly in front of the mushroom box-shaped building: Arroyo Village Hall. They found the gentleman they were looking for sitting at a desk at the back of the building, asleep. The sign on the door read "Town Marshal."
[34] Quinlan snorted, and the town marshal lifted his heavy eyelids, scratched his head, and said in a hoarse whisper, "If you're looking for Matt Hollis, he's out."
[35] Ellery smiled. "We're looking for Arroyo's Sheriff Redden."
[36] "Oh, I am," he said. "What do you want?"
[37] "Sheriff," Ellery said menacingly, "allow me to introduce Richard Quin, the head of the New York Police Department's Homicide Division—he's here in person."
[38] "Who?" The sheriff stared blankly, "New York?"
[39] "Absolutely," Ellery said, stepping on his father's toes, "Now, Sheriff, we want—"
[40] "Sit," said Sheriff Luddon, kicking a chair over to Quin. Quin grunted and sat down elegantly. "This Mr. Van's business, huh? I don't see how the New Yorkers would be interested. What's going on?"
[41] Ellery took out a cigarette case and offered it to the sheriff, who grumbled and stuffed a large wad of chewing tobacco into his mouth.
[42] "Tell me everything, Sheriff."
[43] "There's not much to tell. People from Chicago and Pittsburgh have been poking around the village, and I'm sick of it."
[44] Detective Quinlan snorted. "I understand, Sheriff."
[45] Ellery pulled a wallet from his chest pocket and opened it thoughtfully, gazing at the bills inside. Sheriff Rudden's tired eyes brightened. "Well," he said hastily, "maybe I'm not quite that fed up yet, I can tell you another time."
[46] "Who found the body first?"
[47] "Old Pete. You won't know him, he has a shack in the woods somewhere."
[48] "Oh, I know that. Isn't there a farmer involved too?"
[49] "Mike O'Keefe, he has a few acres on Pigeon Mountain. It seems that O'Keefe was driving his Ford to Arroyo - let me think, it was Monday - yes, it was Friday morning... Christmas morning, quite early. Old Pete was going to Arroyo too - he often comes down, so O'Keefe gave him a ride. Well, they came to the crossroads, where O'Keefe was going to turn off to Arroyo, and that's when they found this. Andrew Van's body was hanging stiffly on the signpost, like a frozen calf in a meat locker."
[50] "We saw that signpost," Elicker encouraged.
[51] "I guess there were hundreds of people driving up here to see it over the past few days," the sheriff complained. "I keep running into traffic problems every so often. Anyway, Owens and Old Pete were terrified, they were both about to faint..."
[52] "Hmm," the officer said.
[53] "Did they touch the body?" Ellery asked.
[54] The sheriff shook his head firmly. "They didn't dare touch it! They came barreling into Arroyo and woke me up from my bed."
[55] "What time was that, sheriff?"
[56] Sheriff Redden blushed. "At eight o'clock. But I stayed up all night playing at Matt Hulley's house the night before, so I overslept a bit."
[57] "I guess you and Mr. Hulley went to the crossroads right away, didn't you?"
[58] "Yes. Matt – he's our mayor, you know – Matt and I, we took four boys with us in a car. A mess, he – I mean, Van. I've never seen anything like it, and it's still Christmas. I call it blasphemy. As for Van, he's an atheist."
[59] "Huh?" the officer asked quickly, his red nose poking out from the folds of the scarf, "Atheist? What does that mean?"
[60] "Oh, maybe not an atheist," Sheriff Redden mumbled uneasily, "I'm not a regular churchgoer myself, but Van never goes. The parish priest – well, I'd better not talk about that."
[61] "Quite extraordinary," Ellery turned to his father and said. "Indeed, it's quite extraordinary. Dad, it's like something a religious fanatic would do."
[62] "Yes, they all say so," Judge Redden said. "I—I don't know. I'm just a country judge, you know? This jail hasn't held a vagrant in three years. But I dare say, gentlemen, it's not just about religion."
[63] "I don't think anyone in town would be a suspect," Ellery frowned and asserted.
[64] "Nobody would be so crazy, I tell you, it must be someone connected to Range's past."
[65] "Has there been any strangers in town lately?"
[66] "None... So Matt and I, and the boys, checked the height, build, clothing, and credentials of the body, and then identified him, and put the body down. On the way back to the village, we stopped at Van's house -"
[67] "Well," Ellery said eagerly, "What did you find?"
[68] "It was like hell," Sheriff Redden said, chewing on his tobacco, "There were signs of a violent struggle, chairs were overturned, almost everything had blood on it. The door had a big T painted in blood, as the newspaper said, and poor old Clark disappeared without a trace."
[69] "Ah," said the officer, "Is that the servant who just walked away, huh? Did he take his clothes?"
[70] "Oh," said the sheriff, rubbing his head, "Not sure, the coroner took over from me. I know they're looking for Clark - I also guess,” he slowly closed one eye, “I also guess they're looking for someone else, but I don't know anything about it." He added hastily.
[71] "Any clues on Clark?" Ellery asked.
[72] "As far as I know, none. The alert has been lifted. The body was sent to the county, Wilton County – about 11 to 12 miles away – where the coroner is in charge. The coroner has sealed off Van's house. The state police are on the case, and the Hanover County district attorney is busy."
[73] Ellery pondered, the officer fidgeted in his chair, and Sheriff Rudden stared intently at Ellery's spectacles.
[74] "The head was cut off," Ellery finally muttered, "that's suspicious. I guess it was done with an axe?"
[75] "Yes, we found the axe in the house, it belonged to Clark, and there were no fingerprints on it."
[76] "Where's the head?"
[77] Sheriff Luden shook his head, "No trace, I guess the crazy killer took it as a souvenir, huh!"
[78] "I think," Ellery said as he put on his hat, "we should be going, Dad. Thank you, Sheriff." He extended his hand, and the other man weakly shook it. When the sheriff felt something slip into his palm, he couldn't help but smile. He gladly gave up his afternoon nap and escorted the Quinns to the street.
[79] * *
[80] Newtown and Piedmont are both in Pennsylvania.
[81] A narrow strip of eastern or northern West Virginia.
[82] An American luxury car manufacturing company founded by August and Frederick Duesenberg in Iowa.
[83] Chapter Two: Weldon's New Year
[84] Ellery Quin had a lasting interest in the case of the principal nailed to the cross. There were plenty of logical reasons for it. He should have been in New York. Inspector Quin had been ordered to shorten his vacation and return to Center Street, and where the inspector went, Ellery usually followed. But there was something in the air of the West Virginia town that held him there. The whole town of Weldon was buzzing with a suppressed excitement that was communicated through whispered words, and the whole town was filled with it.
[85] "Well," as Ellery tucked him into the sleeping car, the old man asked, "what do you think you can make of it? Come on, tell me, I think you've solved it, haven't you?"
[86] "All right, Inspector," Ellery said in a soothing voice, "take it easy on your blood pressure. I'm just interested. I've never encountered such raw violence. I intend to wait for the autopsy and hear that evidence Rudin mentioned."
[87] "You'll slink back to New York with your tail between your legs," the inspector gloomily predicted.
[88] "Oh, undoubtedly," Ellery grinned. "Meanwhile, I'm all out of ideas, and there are so many possibilities…"
[89] They left it at that, and the train departed, leaving Ellery standing on the platform at the starting station, no longer under constraint but feeling uneasy. He drove back to Weldon that day.
[90] It was Tuesday. Until Saturday, the day after New Year's, he had time to sweet-talk the district attorney of Hampshire County into giving him information. District Attorney Crummett was a dour old man, shrewd and ambitious, and self-important. Ellery stood at the door of his reception room, but neither pleading nor cajoling could get him any further. The district attorney was busy and not receiving anyone. Come back tomorrow. The district attorney would see no one. From New York - the son of Inspector Queen? I'm sorry…
[91] Ellery paced the streets of Weldon, listening with tireless ears to the chatter of the townspeople. Weldon, with its glistening Christmas trees of holly and metal, was reveling in festivity to distract from fear. There were few women and no children about. The men met briefly, their lips stiff, discussing ways to punish the killer. Some talked about lynching – a valuable idea, but it failed because no one knew who to hang. The police of Weldon prowled the streets uneasily, and state police rushed through the city. When District Attorney Crummett's car sped by, his lean face occasionally flashed past, with a face of steel-cold and resentful.
[92] Despite the hubbub around him, Ellery remained calm and inquisitive. On Wednesday, he tried to see County Coroner Stapleton. The man was a plump young fellow who perspired constantly but was also sharp-witted, and Ellery learned nothing from him.
[93] So Ellery spent the remaining three days doing his best to gather information about the victim, Andrew Van. It was astounding how little there was about the man. Few had seen him before his death. He was a gentleman who lived a reclusive life, accustomed to solitude and rarely ventured into Weldon. There were rumors that the villagers of Arroyo considered him a model teacher: kind but not lenient, and his contributions were deemed satisfactory by the Arroyo Village Board. Moreover, although he did not often attend church services, he was a teetotaler, which further cemented his standing in a God-fearing, temperance village.
[94] On Thursday, the editors of Weldon's major newspapers became eloquent. The next day was New Year's, a good opportunity to expose lies. Six ministers who contributed to the spiritual needs of Weldon's people preached on the front page, saying that Andrew Van was an ungodly man. The ungodly would die for their ungodliness, but violent acts… The editor did not stop there. An editorial filled with vulgarity was titled in ten-point black letters, quoting French Bluebeard Landru, the Dusseldorf vampire, the British Jack the Ripper, and many other monsters from real life and fiction, providing a tasty dish for the New Year's dinner of Weldon's good people.
[95] Saturday morning, before the scheduled autopsy time, the county courthouse was already full. Ellery cleverly became one of the first to arrive, his seat in the front row behind the railing. At nine minutes before nine, Coroner Stapleton himself appeared; Ellery quickly caught him and presented a telegram signed by the Chief of Police of New York, which granted him access to the front hall where Andrew Van's body was placed.
[96] "The condition of the body is a bit rough," Stapleton panted, "after all, we couldn't conduct the autopsy during the Christmas week, so it's been eight days… The body has been stored in the living room of the local undertaker all this time."
[97] Ellery hardened his heart and removed the sheet covering the body. It was a repulsive sight, so he quickly covered it again. It was a large body, headless – only a gaping hole remained.
[98] On a nearby table, there were men's clothes: a plain dark gray suit, black shoes, a shirt, short socks, underwear – all stained with diluted blood, dry and hard. From the clothes of the dead man, a pencil, a pen, a wallet, a keychain, a crumpled pack of cigarettes, some coins, a cheap watch, and an old letter were found – all of which seemed unremarkable to Ellery; except for a few objects with the initials AV on them, and a letter from a bookstore in Pittsburgh, addressed to Mr. Andrew Van, nothing seemed important for the investigation.
[99] Stapleton turned to introduce a tall, thin old man who had just entered, looking at Ellery with suspicion: "This is Mr. Quin – this is District Attorney Crummett."
[100] "Who?" Crummett asked sternly.
[101] Ellery nodded with a smile and returned to the interrogation room.
[102] Five minutes later, the coroner Stapleton tapped his gavel a few times, and the crowded courtroom fell silent. After quickly arranging the usual preparations, the coroner called Michael O'Keefe to the witness stand.
[103] O'Keefe lumbered down the aisle, followed by murmurs and glances from the audience. He was an old, hunched farmer with skin tanned by the sun. He sat down nervously, his large hands clasped together.
[104] "Mr. O'Keefe," the coroner puffed, "tell us how you discovered the body."
[105] The farmer licked his lips and said, "Okay, sir. Last Friday morning, I drove my own Ford to Arroyo. As I approached Arroyo Peak, I saw old Pete walking heavily from the mountains. I picked him up, and then we drove to the turnoff, where... you could see the body hanging on the signpost. It was nailed, from the hands and feet." O'Keefe's voice trailed off, "We... we quickly rushed to town."
[106] A person in the audience snickered, and the coroner rapped the gavel for silence. "Did you touch the body?"
[107] "No, sir! We didn't even get out of the car."
[108] "Very well, Mr. Oakins."
[109] The farmer suddenly sighed and leisurely walked back to the aisle, then wiped the sweat from his forehead with a large red handkerchief.
[110] "Hmm... old Pete?"
[111] A commotion erupted in the back of the courtroom, and an odd figure stood up. The old man stood tall, with a thick patch of gray whiskers and long, drooping eyebrows. He was wrapped in a tattered, stained, and patched-up collection of old clothes. He stumbled down the aisle, looking uncertain, and then shook his head before settling into the witness stand.
[112] The coroner seemed irritated. "What is your full name?"
[113] "Huh?" The old man blinked his bright eyes and stared blankly to the side.
[114] "Your name! What...what is it? Pete something?"
[115] Old Pete shook his head. "No name," he said. "Old Pete, that's me. I'm dead, been dead for twenty years."
[116] There was a terrifying silence around, and Mr. Stribling looked puzzled as he glanced around. A short, middle-aged man with a wary look on his face stood up near the coroner's bench. "Well, coroner sir."
[117] "What is it, Mr. Hollis?"
[118] "Well," the man repeated loudly, "Old Peter has been crazy for years - ever since he suddenly appeared from the mountains. He has a shabby little house near Arroyo, where he comes every two or three months, probably to set some bird or animal traps. He is very familiar with the daily operations of Arroyo and is a regular visitor here, coroner sir."
[119] "I see, thank you, Mr. Hollis."
[120] The coroner wiped his fat face, and the mayor of Arroyo sat down amidst a murmur of approval. Old Peter's face lit up, and he waved his dirty hand at Mr. Matt Hollis. The coroner continued to question him gruffly. Old Peter's answers were vague, but they were enough to confirm Michael O'Keefe's experience, so the old mountaineer was allowed to leave the witness box. He slowly walked back to his seat, blinking his eyes.
[121] Mayor Hollis and Sheriff Rudd recounted their experiences - how they were woken by O'Keefe and Old Pete, how they went to the crossroads to identify the body, removed the nails, and took the body away in a wagon, how they saw the butchery scene at the Fanns' and the T-shaped mark on the door...
[122] A stout, robust German man was called up. "Luther Bernheim."
[123] He smiled, showing his gold teeth, patted his belly, and sat down in the witness stand.
[124] "You run the general store in Arroyo?" asked the coroner.
[125] "Yes, sir."
[126] "Do you know Andrew Van?"
[127] "Yes, sir, he has bought things from my store."
[128] "How long have you known him?"
[129] "Ah, for many years. He's a good customer, always pays in cash."
[130] "Did he come to buy groceries himself?"
[131] "Occasionally. Mostly it's his helper, Cling, who comes, but he always comes to pay the bill himself."
[132] "Is he friendly?"
[133] "Burnheim squints his eyes, "Hmm...yes, not."
[134] "You mean he's never been friendly, just acting nice?"
[135] "Yes."
[136] "Do you think Van is an odd person?"
[137] "Huh? Oh, yes, yes. For example, he always orders caviar."
[138] "Caviar?"
[139] "Yes, I have only one customer who buys it. I used to have to order all sorts of caviar for him - sturgeon, salmon, but mostly blackfish, the best kind."
[140] "Mr. Burnheim, and Mayor Hollis and Sheriff Redden, please come to the next room to formally identify the body."
[141] The coroner left the podium, and the three residents of Arroyo followed behind. During this time, the room was filled with a buzzing sound, like an intermission. The grocer's red face turned gray, and fear filled his eyes.
[142] Ellery Queen sighed. A principal, living in a village of only two hundred people, ordering caviar! Perhaps Sheriff Redden is more astute than he appears. From Fang's work and living environment, his past is clearly more luxurious.
[143] The tall, thin figure of the district attorney, Krümmit, slowly appeared on the witness stand, and the audience was immediately excited. Everything that had happened before was insignificant, and now was the time to reveal the truth.
[144] "Mr. Prosecutor," the coroner Stapleton nervously leaned forward and said, "have you investigated the background of the deceased?"
[145] "Yes!"
[146] Ellery slumped in his chair, disliking the energetic and imposing district attorney, but Krümmit's cold gaze held a certain ominousness.
[147] "Please recount what you have discovered."
[148] The district attorney of Hancock County gripped the witness chair's armrests. "Andrew Van appeared in Arroyo nine years ago, applying for the position of principal at the village school. His credentials and preliminary work were satisfactory, so he was hired by the town committee. He brought along his servant, a man named Klein, and rented a house on Arroyo Street, where they lived until his death. His performance as a teacher was satisfactory, and his behavior in Arroyo was without reproach."
[149] "What happened before that?"
[150] "There were no leads, but he was an American citizen who gained citizenship in Pittsburgh thirteen years ago. His documents in Pittsburgh record him as an Armenian born in 1885 before becoming an American."
[151] Armenians! Elijah rubbed his chin behind the railing, thinking, not far from Galilee... Some strange ideas crossed his mind, but he impatiently discarded them.
[152] "Have you also investigated the background of Andrew's servant, Clinton, Mr. Prosecutor?"
[153] "Yes. He is an orphan, raised and cared for by St. Vincent's Orphanage in Pittsburgh. Upon reaching adulthood, he was employed by the orphanage to perform various tasks. After Andrew resigned from his position at the Pittsburgh public school and accepted the appointment in Arroyo, he visited the orphanage, expressing his desire to hire a servant. Seeing that Clinton seemed suitable, Andrew carefully investigated his background and expressed satisfaction. Thus, the two arrived in Arroyo and stayed there until Andrew's death."
[154] Elijah lazily thought, What motive could drive a person to leave a big city like Pittsburgh for a small village like Arroyo? A criminal record, wanting to evade the police? Impossible, it would be easier to hide in a big city, not a small village. No, he was sure it was something deeper, more obscure, perhaps deeply rooted in the mind of the deceased. Some people seek solitude after experiencing setbacks in life, and this situation is likely to be consistent with that of the Arroyo principal who loved caviar.
[155] "What kind of person is Clinton?" Stapleton asked.
[156] The district attorney looked annoyed. "The orphanage reported him to be a rather simple-minded person – I believe they considered him psychologically retarded, a harmless guy."
[157] "Did he show any signs of a murderous inclination, Mr. Krümmit?"
[158] "No. He was considered a gentle, slow-witted person at St. Vincent's Orphanage and very gentle with the other children. He was humble and easy to please, respectful to his superiors."
[159] The district attorney moistened his lips, seemingly ready to reveal the truth as agreed. But the coroner Stapleton hastily interrupted him, calling the grocer over.
[160] "Do you know Clinton, Mr. Bernheim?"
[161] "Yes, sir."
[162] "What kind of a person is he?"
[163] "Quiet, naturally good-natured, and silent as a bull."
[164] "Is it true that Clint was known for his strength in Arroyo, Mr. Bernheim?"
[165] Eleanor smiled. The coroner was a simple man.
[166] Bernheim made a gurgling sound in his throat. "Oh, yes, Clinton is very strong. He can lift a whole barrel of sugar! But he wouldn't harm a fly, coroner, I remember once... "
[167] "All right, Mr. Stapleton," he said impatiently. "Mayor Hollis, please come to the witness stand again."
[168] Matt Hollis's face lit up, and Elyot decided he was a slick little man.
[169] "Are you the head of the town council, Mayor Hollis?"
[170] "Yes!"
[171] "Tell the jury everything you know about Andrew Vance."
[172] "He was always satisfactory, kept to himself, a hard-working fellow. After school he lived in the nice little house I rented him, some people thought he had been robbed, others that he was a foreigner, but I didn't think so. The mayor spoke briefly, "Quiet, that's all there is to it. No trouble with neighbors? Well, that's his business. If he didn't want to go fishing with me and Constable Redden, that was his business. Mayor Hollar smiled and nodded. "Besides, he spoke perfect English, just like you and me, Mr. Coroner."
[173] "Did you know of any visitors who came to see him?"
[174] "No, of course I can't be sure. But he was an odd character," the mayor continued thoughtfully, "a few times I went to Pittsburgh on business and he asked me to buy him some books – strange books, things to make him look the part. Philosophical, historical, about the stars, and so on."
[175] "Yes, yes, very interesting, Mr. Hollar. By the way, you're the banker of Arroyo, aren't you?"
[176] "Yes, I am." The mayor blushes, lowering his eyes modestly. Ellery deduces from his expression that he holds sway over the town of Arroyo.
[177] "Did Andrew Van have an account with you at the bank?"
[178] "No, he usually takes his salary in cash, but I don't think he's deposited any money elsewhere, as I've asked a few times - you know, business is business - he said he keeps it at home." Mr. Hollis shrugs, "I don't trust banks. Oh, everyone has their own preferences, and I won't argue with him…"
[179] "Is this common knowledge in Arroyo?"
[180] Mr. Hollis hesitates. "Well... well, maybe I mentioned it somewhere, I think almost everyone in town knows about the strange behavior of the school principal."
[181] The coroner waved the mayor off the witness stand and called the sheriff up. The sheriff walked up stiffly, as if he knew exactly how to handle such matters.
[182] "Did you search Andrew Van's house on Friday morning, December 25th, sheriff?"
[183] "Yes."
[184] "Did you find any money?"
[185] "No."
[186] The spectators gasped. Robbery! Ellery frowned. The case had started out with religious fanaticism, and now it was turning into a simple theft. He leaned forward... a man took something to the lectern, a flattened, cheap green tin box with a badly deformed clasp and a tiny, weak lock. The coroner took the box from the assistant and opened and turned it over, finding it empty.
[187] "Marshal, do you recognize this box?"
[188] The marshal sniffed. "I was about to get to that." He spoke in his own raspy voice.
[189] "It was found like this in Van's house. It's the box he used to keep his money in, without a doubt."
[190] The coroner held up the box for the jury to see. "Jury, please take note of this exhibit... All right, Marshal, call the postmaster of Arroyo to the stand."
[191] "Did Andrew receive many letters?" asked the coroner.
[192] "Not many," said the postmaster shrilly. "He almost never received any other mail besides advertisements."
[193] "Did he receive any letters or packages the week before he died?"
[194] "No!"
[195]
[196] "Did he often send letters?"
[197] "No, he only sent a couple of them occasionally, and not a single one in the last three or four months."
[198] Dr. Strong, the coroner's pathologist, was called in. As soon as his name was mentioned, the audience began to whisper frantically. He looked tired and sad, and he dragged his feet along the aisle as if he had an eternity ahead of him.
[199] After he sat down, the coroner asked, "Dr. Strong, when was your first examination of the deceased's body?"
[200] "Two hours after the body was found."
[201] "Can you give the jury an approximate time of death?"
[202] "Yes. I believe the person was already dead for six to eight hours when they were found at the crossroads."
[203] "So, we can confirm that the murder took place around midnight on Christmas Eve?"
[204] "Yes."
[205] "Can you provide the jury with any further details about the body that are relevant to this inquiry?"
[206] Erlie chuckled. The coroner, Stapleton, had done his job meticulously, his words were formal and official, and the audience could see they were deeply affected by his words.
[207] Dr. Strong crossed his legs and spoke in a tired voice: "There are no other marks on the body except for the wound on the neck where the head was cut off and the nail holes in the hands and feet."
[208] The coroner leaned forward, his belly bumping against the edge of the table. "Dr. Strong," he croaked, "what conclusions did you draw from this fact?"
[209] "The victim was most likely killed by a blow to the head or a gunshot wound, as there are no other signs of violence on the body."
[210] Erlie nodded, impressed by the thoughtful country doctor.
[211] "In my opinion," continued the coroner's assistant, "the victim was already dead when his head was cut off. The condition of the wound at the neck indicates that a very sharp tool was used."
[212] The coroner picked up an object that had been carefully placed on the table in front of him and held it up. It was a long-handled, evil-looking axe, with a gleaming blade that had no blood on it. "Dr. Strong, can this weapon be used to cut the victim's head off from his body?"
[213] "Yes."
[214] The coroner turned to the jury: "This exhibit was found on the ground in the kitchen of Andrew Van's house, which is where the murder took place. I would remind you, gentlemen, that there are no fingerprints on this weapon, which suggests that the killer either wore gloves or wiped the axe clean after using it. This axe has been identified as belonging to the deceased and is usually kept in the kitchen, where it is used by the missing Clark to split wood… that's all, Dr. Strong. Colonel Pickett, please come to the witness stand."
[215] The West Virginia State Police Commissioner, a tall, military-looking man, agreed. "Colonel Pickett, what do you have to report?"
[216] "We conducted a thorough search of the surrounding area of Arroyo," Colonel Picket said in a machine-gun-like voice, "but we couldn't find the victim's head or the missing servant, Clinton. I have sent out descriptions of Clinton to neighboring states, and we are searching for him."
[217] "The investigation of the last sighting of the deceased and the missing person, I assume, is your responsibility, Colonel. Did you find anything?"
[218] "The last time Andrew Van was seen alive was on Thursday, December 24th, at 4 pm, when he visited Mrs. Rebecca Traub of Arroyo and reminded her that her son William was falling behind in his studies. He was a student at William's school. Since then, no one has seen him alive."
[219] "What about Clinton?"
[220] "Clinton was last seen by a farmer named Timothy Trainer between Arroyo and Pudding, at a few minutes past 4 pm on the same day. He bought a bushel of potatoes with cash and walked away with them on his shoulder."
[221] "Did the bushel of potatoes turn up in Van's house? In determining whether Clinton arrived home, Colonel, this may be important."
[222] "Yes. The potatoes were found untouched, and Timothy Trainer confirmed that they were the same bushel he sold that afternoon."
[223] "Do you have anything else to report?"
[224] Before answering, Colonel Pickert looked around the courtroom. As he spoke in a cold voice, his open mouth resembled a trap: "I certainly do!"
[225] The courtroom fell silent. Ellery yawned, knowing that the revelation was about to begin. Colonel Pickert leaned over and whispered something to the coroner, who blinked, smiled, wiped his sweaty face, and nodded. The audience sensed that something was about to happen and shifted in their seats. Colonel Pickert quietly gestured to someone at the back of the courtroom.
[226] A tall police officer walked out, holding the arm of a short, elderly man. The man was in shock, with unkempt brown hair and a scruffy brown beard, and had piercing little eyes - the eyes of a fanatic. His skin was a dirty yellow-brown, weathered and wrinkled from years of exposure to the elements, as if he had spent his entire life outdoors. Ellery squinted and saw him wearing a pair of baggy yellow-brown shorts covered in mud and a faded gray high-collared sweater. His brown feet, exposed, had grayish-white veins like ropes winding around them, and he wore a pair of strange sandals. He held a striking object in his hand - a staff-like rod with a crudely carved snake-shaped statue on top, obviously made by a clumsy craftsman.
[227] There was an immediate commotion, and the audience burst into laughter. The coroner pounded his table, trying to restore order.
[228] A young man with an oily face and wearing overalls lagged behind the police officer and the accused. It was clear that most of the audience knew him, as many hands reached out to give him a reassuring pat as he passed, and the rest of the audience began openly pointing at his retreating figure.
[229] The three of them sat down behind the railing. The elderly man with the brown beard was clearly terrified. His eyes darted back and forth, his thin brown hands clenched around the strange staff he held, twitching and unclenching.
[230] "Casper Crocker to the witness stand!"
[231] The white-faced young man swallowed a large gulp of saliva and stood up to take the witness stand.
[232] "You run an auto repair shop and a gas station on Main Street in Wilton, Maine, right?" the coroner asked.
[233] "Oh, yes, you know me, sir…"
[234] "Answer my question," Stapleton said sternly. "Tell the jury what happened around eleven o'clock on Christmas Eve night."
[235] Crocker took a deep breath, glancing around the room as if seeking a friendly gaze, and said, "On Christmas Eve, I closed my auto repair shop and planned to celebrate. I live in a house behind the shop. At around eleven that night, my wife and I were sitting in the front room when we heard a terrible thumping and shouting coming from somewhere outside, like it was coming from my shop. It was pitch black outside," he swallowed again quickly, "so, there was a man standing there pounding on the shop's door. As soon as he saw me…"
[236] "Wait a moment, Mr. Crocker, what was he wearing?"
[237] The auto repair shop owner shrugged. "It was too dark to see, and there was no reason to pay special attention."
[238] "Did you get a good look at the man's appearance?"
[239] "Yes, sir, he was standing in front of my night light. He was dressed up—it was cold outside—but to me, he didn't want to be recognized. In short, I saw that he had a clean-shaven face, dark skin, and looked a bit like a foreigner, but he spoke in perfect old-fashioned American English."
[240] "How old do you think he was?"
[241] "Oh, around thirty-five, maybe a little older or younger, it's hard to say."
[242] "What did he want to do?"
[243] "He wanted to rent a small car to take him to Arroyo."
[244] The courtroom was very quiet, and Ellery could hear a short, fat man sitting behind him panting. The audience was tense, sitting on the edge of their seats.
[245] "What happened next?" the coroner asked.
[246] "Oh, I didn't like the deal - it was already eleven o'clock on Christmas Eve, and my wife had to be alone at home. But he pulled out a wallet and said, 'If you agree to drive, I'll give you ten dollars.' Oh, sir, that was a lot of money for a poor man like me, so I said, 'Sure, stranger, I'll take you.'"
[247] "You drove him there?"
[248] "Yes, sir, I drove him there. I went home to get my coat and told my wife I'd be gone for about half an hour. Then I came back, got my old car, and he got in. I asked him where he was going in Arroyo, and he said, 'There's an intersection with New Kensington-Bridgeton Road, isn't there?' I said, 'Yes, there is.' He said, 'Well, that's where I'm going.' I drove him there, he got out, and then he gave me ten dollars. Then I turned around and rushed home, feeling quite strange and scary."
[249] "Did you see him do anything when you left?"
[250] Crocker nodded vigorously, "I turned my head over my shoulder and almost drove the car into the ditch. He was walking away with a shovel in his hand, limping quite badly, sir."
[251] The man with the brown beard sitting next to the sheriff let out a gasp, his eyes darting around as if searching for an escape route.
[252] "Which leg, Crocker?"
[253] "Well, he seemed to favor his left leg, putting most of his weight on his right leg."
[254] "Is that the last time you saw him?"
[255] "Yes, sir, that was the first and last time I saw him that night before then."
[256] "All right."
[257] Mr. Crocker left the witness stand, grateful, and hurried down the aisle towards the door.
[258] "Hey," the coroner, Mr. Stapleton, suddenly spoke, startling the small man with the brown beard who was cowering in a chair with wary eyes, "you, over there, come up to the witness stand."
[259] The constable stood up, pulling the man with the brown beard to his feet and pushing him forward. The little man walked docilely, but his wild eyes were filled with fear, and he kept trying to shrink back. The constable roughly sat him down in the witness chair and returned to his seat.
[260] "What is your name?" the coroner, Mr. Stapleton, asked.
[261] The man sat in the witness chair, suddenly the focus of attention, and the audience erupted in laughter again at his strange attire and appearance. It took a long time for the court to regain order. During this time, the witness licked his lips, swaying from side to side, and muttering to himself. Ellery was surprised to find that the man was praying, praying to the wooden snake on the top of the staff, which was truly surprising.
[262] Stribling repeated the question nervously. The man grabbed the middle of the staff, about an arm's length from one end, and shrugged his bony shoulders back, as if gathering strength and dignity from this posture. He looked straight into Stribling's eyes and spoke in a clear, sharp voice: "I am called Harakhti, the god of the midday sun. Ra-Harakti, which means the hawk!"
[263] The whole court fell into a stunned silence. The coroner Stribling blinked his eyes and shrank back, as if someone had suddenly made an incomprehensible threat in front of him. The audience opened their mouths wide, and then burst out laughing again - this time not out of mockery, but out of inexplicable fear. There was something terrible and bizarre about this man, and he exuded a sense of solemnity and unimaginable fanaticism from his whole body.
[264] "Who are you?" the coroner asked in a weak voice.
[265] The man named Harakti crossed his arms over his emaciated chest and held the staff tightly in front of him, unwilling to answer.
[266] Stapleton rubbed his cheek, looking puzzled and unsure of how to proceed. "Uh...what is your occupation, uh...Mr. Horus?"
[267] Ellery sank deeper into his seat. He was embarrassed for the coroner, and the scene was becoming increasingly uncomfortable.
[268] Horus's stiff lips spat out stern words: "I am a healer of the weak, restoring health to sickly bodies. I am the one who sets sail on the dawn boat Mensekt, and the one who returns on the evening boat Mesektet. I am called Horus, the god of the horizon. I am the son of Nut; Nut is the sky goddess, wife of Geb, and mother of Isis and Osiris. I am the supreme god of Memphis. I am Atum—"
[269] "Stop!" the coroner shouted. "Captain Pickford, for God's sake, what is going on here? I...I thought you said this madman was an important figure and would be helpful for the interrogation!"
[270] The state police chief quickly stood up. The man named Horus calmly waited, his initial fear dissipating, as if in his twisted mind, he realized he was in control of the situation.
[271] "I apologize, coroner sir," the colonel said quickly. "I should have reminded you that this man's mind is not normal. I think it would be best if I tell you and the jury what he does, and then you can ask him more directly. He runs a kind of medicine show - things like that, putting sun, stars, and the moon on everything, as well as strange portraits of Egyptian pharaohs. It seems he believes he is the sun or something like that, but it is harmless. He travels around in a four-wheeled wagon pulled by an old horse, going from town to town. He has been to Illinois, Indiana, Ohio, and West Virginia, promoting and selling a remedy that he claims cures everything by rubbing it on the hair..."
[272] "This is the universal remedy for young people," Harlock said solemnly. "It is a bottle of sunlight. I am the one chosen to preach the truth of the sun, I am Menti, Atum, and..."
[273] "As far as I know, it's just regular cod liver oil," Colonel Pickert smiled. "No one knows his real name, and I think he's forgotten it himself."
[274] "Thank you, Colonel," the coroner said with respect.
[275] Ellery sat rigidly in his stiff seat, thrilled by a sudden discovery that extended even to his spine. He recognized the crude emblem on the madman's hand as Uraeus [18], the staff representing the godhood and divine pharaohs of the ancient Egyptians. At first, from the design of the snake, he was inclined to think it was a poorly made staff of the god Mercury [19], but Mercury's symbol always includes wings; and this, he strained his eyes wide open, could only see a rough sun circle around the head of the snake or snakes... the land of the Pharaohs of Egypt! From the mouth of this interesting little madman came a whole string of familiar names: Horus, Nut, Isis, Osiris; other names sounded strange but were full of Egyptian flavor... Ellery sat up straight.
[276] "Eh - Harleckt, or whatever you call yourself," the coroner said, "did you hear Mr. Crocker's testimony about a black-skinned, clean-shaven, limping man?"
[277] The big-bearded man's eyes flashed a more rational glance, and then the hidden fear reappeared. "That… that limping man," he stammered, "I heard."
[278] "Based on this description, do you recognize him?"
[279] He hesitated for a moment, then - "I recognize him."
[280] "Ah!" the coroner sighed, "Well, Harleckt, we have made progress. Who is he, and how do you know it's him?"
[281] "He's my priest."
[282] "Priest!" murmurs were heard in the crowd, and Elyot heard the short, fat man behind him say, "Oh, how blasphemous!"
[283] "Do you mean he's your... assistant?"
[284] "He's my disciple, my priest, the Great Priest of Horus."
[285] "Yes, yes," Stepon quickly said, "What's his name?"
[286] "Werger Crocass."
[287] "Hmm," the coroner frowned, "A foreign name, eh? Armenian, perhaps? He asked the brown-bearded little man.
[288] "There's no other country but Egypt," Harakht quietly said.
[289] "Well!" Stapleton said, squinting, "How do you spell that name?"
[290] Colonel Pickett said, "We've got it all figured out, Stapleton, V-e-l-j-a, K-r-o-s-a-c. We found it on some papers in the man's compartment."
[291] "Where is this Verga Krossak?" asked the coroner.
[292] Harlech shrugged, "He left." But Ellery saw a flicker of fear in his eyes.
[293] "When did he leave?"
[294] He shrugged again.
[295] Captain Pickert broke the silence again. "Stapleton, it might be best if I explain, to speed up the investigation. Krossak was always in the background before we found him, he had been following Harlech for years, a mysterious character. He played some kind of business manager and advertising agent role, letting Harlech talk nonsense here. Harlech met him somewhere in the west, the last time they were together was on Christmas Eve, camping near Holly Bay [20].” It was several miles from Wilton, and Ellery remembered some of the landmarks along the way. "Krossak left around 10 o'clock, the same time the unknown witness claimed to have seen him last, which matches the time perfectly."
[296] "You haven't found any leads on Crocker?"
[297] The colonel looked annoyed. "Not yet," he said sharply. "He just disappeared, swallowed up by the Earth. But we will find him, he won't get away, we've circulated his description all over the place."
[298] "Harrington," the coroner said, "have you ever been to Arroyo?"
[299] "Arroyo? No."
[300] "They've never gone that far north in West Virginia," the colonel explained.
[301] "What do you know about Crosack?"
[302] "He's a devout believer," Harleckt said cautiously, "He worships at the altar with reverence; he attends Cooper's Church, eagerly listening to the Bible, he is our pride and glory…"
[303] "Well, enough of that," the coroner said wearily, "Take him away, deputy."
[304] The deputy grinned and stood up, grabbing the skinny arm of the brown-bearded man and dragging him off the witness stand. As the two disappeared into the crowd, the coroner made a disgusted face and sighed with relief.
[305] Ellery sighed again. His father was right; even if he didn't slink back to New York with his tail between his legs, he would at least look crestfallen. The whole thing was so madcap, so inexplicable, so lacking in logic, it had become a farce. However—there was a body that had been brutally decapitated and nailed to a cross…
[306] Nailed to a cross! He shuddered and drew in a visible breath. Nailed to a cross—Egypt. Where had he encountered such a bizarre thing?
[307] The questioning proceeded quickly. Colonel Pickert produced many items that he had found in Croscius' carriage, which Croscius claimed belonged to Croscius. There was no connection between them, and they held no intrinsic value as clues to the man's background or identity. As the coroner pointed out to the jury, the fact that they could not find a photograph of Croscius made it quite difficult to arrest the man if they wanted to. Adding to the difficulty was the fact that there was no valid sample of Croscius' handwriting.
[308] Other witnesses were called to the stand, and small clues emerged that were known to everyone: no suspicious persons were found to have been watching Andrew Van's house on Christmas Eve; after Crocker left Croscius at the crossroads, no one else saw him. Van's house was the only one near the crossroads, and no one passed by that night… The iron nails found in Van's crucified corpse came from his own toolbox, which he usually kept in the kitchen's utensil room. It was a well-established fact that some time ago, Van had bought those nails from Mr. Bernheim, the grocer, and most of them had been used to build a woodshed.
[309] It was only when the coroner, Mr. Swipe, stood up that Ellery realized where he was. "Ladies and gentlemen of the jury," the coroner said, "you have listened to the entire trial—"
[310] Ellery jumped up. Mr. Swipe stopped and looked around, annoyed at the interruption. "What is it, Mr. Quine? You're interrupting the trial…"
[311] "Wait a minute, Mr. Stapleton," Ellery said quickly. "After you finish your statement to the jury, I have a fact that I believe is relevant to your trial."
[312] "What?" the district attorney, Krutmet, exclaimed, jumping up from his seat. "A new fact?"
[313] "Not a new fact, Mr. Prosecutor," Ellery smiled as he answered. "An ancient fact, older than Christianity."
[314] "What?" the coroner, Stapleton, called out. The audience leaned forward, murmuring quietly, and the jury members stood up and stared at the unexpected witness. "What did you find out, Mr. Quine? What does Christianity have to do with this?"
[315] "Nothing—I hope," Ellery pushed his spectacles up his nose and addressed the coroner. "The most significant feature of this terrible crime, if I may say so, has not been mentioned in this trial. I refer to: the killer, whoever he is, went to great lengths to smear the letter T or symbol around the scene. The T-shaped crossroads, the T-shaped road sign, the T-shaped corpse, the T-shaped bloodstain on the victim's door, all of which have been appropriately commented on in newspapers and magazines."
[316] "Yes, yes," the district attorney, Crimmett, interjected with a cold smile. "We all know that. But what is your fact?"
[317] "Look here." Ellery stared intently at Crimmett, who sat down, blushing. "I admit I couldn't find a connection, and I was utterly perplexed. But do you know, that symbol T may not have anything to do with the letter itself."
[318] "What do you mean, Mr. Quine?" The coroner, Stapleton, asked anxiously.
[319] "I mean, the symbol T has a religious significance."
[320] "Religious significance?" Stapleton repeated. An obese old gentleman in a clerical collar stood up from the dense audience. "Allow me to interject," he said in a sharp tone. "As a minister of the Gospel of Truth, I have never heard of such a thing as a religious significance in the symbol T!"
[321] Someone called out, "Let him speak, the minister!" So the minister blushed and sat down.
[322] Ellery smiled. "Allow me to refute this erudite minister. Its meaning is as follows: In many religious symbols, there is a T-shaped cross called a Tau cross or a Tau cross. "
[323] The minister jumped up from his seat. "Yes," he called, "that's right, but it's not the original Christian cross, sir. It's a pagan symbol!"
[324] Ellery smiled lightly. "Exactly, sir. Before the advent of Christianity, people used the Greek cross, didn't they? The Tau cross is several hundred years older than the Greek cross that people are familiar with. Some people believe it was originally a symbol of the phallus... but the point is this..."
[325] He paused to take a deep breath, and people waited in tense silence. Then he pushed his spectacles up his nose and faced the coroner clearly. "Tau or the Tau cross is not its only name. It is sometimes called," he paused, concluding quietly, "the Egyptian cross!"

[354][1] Danilo Rieka.
[355] Chapter Three: Professor Yadle
[356] That was all there was to the case. Unusual, unbelievable – but that was it. Erlie's hint about the mysterious connection only served to deepen the mystery. He couldn't make sense of it. One couldn't analyze the wild thoughts of a madman with logic. He had to console himself with that.
[357] If it was too difficult for him, it was too difficult for the coroner, Mr. Stapleton, the district attorney, Mr. Crummett, Colonel Pickert, the jury, Arroyo, and the residents of Weldon, as well as the numerous journalists who gathered in the city on the day of the inquest. The coroner firmly resisted the temptation to make a hasty, unsupported conclusion. The jury scratched their heads in confusion, making a decision of "unknown cause of death by one or more persons" under the coroner's guidance. The journalists wandered around for a day or two. Colonel Pickert and District Attorney Crummett were busy but made no progress, and gradually relaxed. In the end, the case died down in the newspapers.
[358] Erlie returned to New York with a calm shrug, and the longer he thought about the problem, the more he tended to believe that the final explanation would be simple. He saw no reason to doubt the overwhelming implications, which were indeed circumstantial evidence but clear in meaning. There was a man named Vilga Crocok who spoke English and had a somewhat con artist style. For his own mysterious purposes, he plotted and searched in secret and ultimately took the life of a foreign-born village schoolteacher. The method of murder, from a criminal standpoint, was interesting but not particularly important. It was a terrible but understandable expression of a twisted emotion, driven by mania and oddity, hidden behind – whether false error, religious fanaticism, or a sordid story of bloodthirsty revenge – might never be known. Crocok would naturally disappear after completing his terrible mission, perhaps returning to his own country across the turbulent sea.
[359] Erlie returned to New York, facing the keen gaze of Inspector Quin.
[360] "I'm not going to say 'I told you so,' " Erlie said on the evening he returned, smiling at the dinner table, "but I want to point out a moral lesson."
[361] "Speak," Ellery grumbled, gnawing on a bone.
[362] "The moral lesson is: murder is murder. I tell you, you stupid idiot, that on Earth, almost any crime committed in any place can be explained very easily, without any need for wild imagination, and you must understand it." The inspector's face glowed, "I don't know what you expected to accomplish in that God-forsaken countryside, but even a patrol officer can tell you the answer."
[363] Ellery put down his fork. "But logic…"
[364] "Don't give me that gibberish!" The inspector snorted, "Go to bed."
[365] Six months passed, during which Ellery completely forgot about the mysterious murder in Arroyo, as there were many other things to do. New York is not like Pennsylvania, where it is not a city of love and friendship, and there are many murder cases. The inspector was busy investigating, and Ellery followed behind, contributing his special abilities to the cases that aroused his interest.
[366] It was not until six months after Andrew Van's crucifixion in West Virginia that the Arroyo murder case resurfaced in Ellery's mind.

[518] * * *
[519] A legendary name for an ancient continent, nation, or city-state, said to have had a highly developed civilization and to have been destroyed by a prehistoric flood around 10,000 BCE.
[520] The earliest description of Atlantis appeared in the writings of the ancient Greek philosopher Plato in his work "Dialogues."
[521] [3] A utopian novel by the British philosopher Francis Bacon, published posthumously in 1627 after the author's death.
[522] [4] A writing medium widely used by the ancient Egyptians, made from papyrus reeds grown in the Nile Delta.
[523] [5] A gulf in the Atlantic Ocean, located to the north of Long Island.
[524] [6] A two-player board game where the pieces move diagonally and can jump over and capture an opponent's piece. The board is the same as that of chess.
[525] [7] An ancient form of religious belief involving carving patterns of certain animals (armadillos, badgers, bears, moles, crows, etc.) onto objects such as wood, stones, or shells, in the belief that this would bring power to a person or group.
[526][8]The Roman god of the sea, also worshipped as a horse god in Rome, usually depicted holding a trident.
[527][9]This actually means "This analysis is not very enlightening," but to match the professor's pun, the literal meaning is retained.
[528][10]The term totem in English is "totem."

[751] Ellery chewed loudly, his mind whirling with unusual ideas, but he kept them to himself. Eschew once complained of sciatica. Otherwise, the room was quiet.
[752] At two o'clock, they left the dining room and returned to the house's right wing. The library was a special feature, a man's study. It was square, with a polished hardwood floor except for the three feet around the edges, which were covered with a Chinese-style thick carpet. Built-in bookshelves lined both walls, filled from floor to ceiling with books. In a small alcove between two walls, there was a small piano with a soft-rounded keyboard, the lid propped open – obviously Thomas Brad had left it there the night before. In the center of the room was a low round reading table covered with magazines and smoking paraphernalia. A sofa chair was placed against one wall, with the front legs on the carpet. Across from it was a desk with a fold-down flap. Ellery looked at the flap, and he could clearly see the two ink bottles, red and black, on top of it, and, after a habitual glance, noticed that both bottles were almost full.
[753] "I've examined that desk with a magnifying glass," Eschew said, sinking onto the sofa, "Needless to say, it was one of the first things we did. Of course, if it's Brad's private desk, it might contain some files that would be valuable to our investigation." He shrugged, "But nothing came of it. As for the rest of the room – well, you can see for yourself, there's nothing else here with any personal touch, and the murder took place in the gazebo. Now, the only thing left to study is the chess."
[754] "Oh," Vane added, "we found the red pawn near the totem pole."
[755] "I assume you've checked the rest of the house?" Ellery said, pacing around the room.
[756] "Oh, yes, it's just routine. We checked Brad's bedroom, and there was nothing remarkable there."
[757] Eleri turned his attention to the round reading table. He took out the glass paper envelope from his pocket, which contained tobacco ash, found in the pipe on the ground in the gazebo. He opened the lid of a large cigar box on the table, and took out a hand of tobacco, the same color and cut as the ones found in the pipe - all cut in an unusual way.
[758] He smiled, "Well, at least this dirty tobacco is not a problem, another clue lies in the pipe. If this cigar box belonged to Brad, then the tobacco was his."
[759] "It did belong to Brad," Isam said.
[760] Eleri tried to open a small drawer, the outline of which could be seen from the underside of the round table. He found it to be a real smoker's collection, a mess, and all the items were of excellent quality and had been used multiple times, although they were all ordinary-looking pipes - with straight or curved stems - made of various materials such as sepiolite, heather, and celluloid, and two long ones with a British earthenware style.
[761] "Well," he said, "Mr. Brad has his own inner sanctum, chess and pipes have always been with him. I'm surprised there's no dog by the fireplace, hmm, nothing here."

[763] Ellery shook his head, "You won't find another one exactly like it, will you? No one would have two of those things, absolutely impossible. I think he'd get lockjaw if he kept chewing on that strange stuff. The pipe must have been a gift."
[764] Ellery turned his attention to the main exhibit - the object located on the left side of the desk, opposite the sofa, on the same wall.
[765] It was a delicate device, a foldable chess table hinged together, obviously foldable and able to be stored in a shallow niche in the rear wall. A sliding door, currently resting on top of the niche, could be lowered to cover the entire device. Two wall chairs, one on each side of the table, could also be rotated into the wall.
[766] "Mr. Bred must have had quite the checkers addiction," Ellery said, "to have a wall-embedded device like this. Hmm... I think it's still in the state he left it, hasn't been touched, has it?"
[767] "At least not by us," Escham coldly said, "See what you can get out of that."
[768] The desktop was a gleaming work of art, inlaid with sixty-four alternating black and white squares, all bordered by an expensive mother-of-pearl edge, designed in the common manner of a chessboard. Each player had a wide edge for placing their discarded pieces. Nine red pieces captured by black were scattered along the edge near the desk; three black pieces captured by red were on the opposite edge. On the board, there were three black kings (made by placing one black piece on top of another) and three single black pieces, as well as two single red pieces, one of which was in the black's first row, or starting row.
[769] Ellery thoughtfully examined the chessboard and its edges, "Where are the boxes for these?"
[770] Escham kicked his foot towards the desk. A cheap cardboard box lay open on the activity flap, empty.
[771] "There are eleven red pieces here," Ellery said, staring at the wall, "There should have been twelve, but another identical red piece was found near the totem pole."
[772] "Yeah," Eschew sighed, "We've checked the rest of the house, and there are no other complete sets of chess, so the red piece we found must have come from here."
[773] "Exactly," Ellery said, "This is quite interesting, really interesting." He looked back down at the pieces.
[774] "You think so?" Eschew said with a frown, "You won't think that in a while. I know what you're thinking, but that's not the case. I'll bring Breda's butler here."
[775] He walked over to the door and told the detective, "Call that Stollings guy back here, the butler."
[776] Ellery raised his expressive eyebrows but said nothing. He walked to the desk and picked up the empty cardboard chess box. Isam grinned, watching him with a complex expression.

[779] "You'll understand in a moment, there's nothing surprising about it, I assure you."

Then, just recently, Mr. Temple praised this set, so Mr. Bred - he told me one day - decided to get him a set exactly like it, as a surprise. Two weeks ago, he had a private carver in Brooklyn make copies of the twenty-four pieces, but they haven't come back yet. So, for the time being, he had to make do with these cheap ones."

He said he needed to be alone all evening, and he told Fox he could use the car, so we all left."

[835] "In fact," the district attorney said, "I think you've been doing a lot of unnecessary work, Vance. No one with criminal intent would get off at the nearest station; they'd get off a station or two earlier or later and walk the rest of the way."

[840] Ellery grinned. "Trying to escape my relentless pursuit, is it, officer?"
[841] "Huh?"

[844][1] From the Latin poet Ovid's (43 BC – 17 AD) long poem "The Art of Love," the original two lines are: "Follow me, far away, and flee from my relentless pursuit."
[845] Chapter 7: Fox and the British Couple
[846] They were in a fog, seeing no light.

[915] Chapter Eight: Oyster Island
[916] The Ketcham Bay can be roughly considered a half-circle, separated from the shore where Thomas Bred's property is located. In the center of the crescent-shaped beach is a dock, where several motorboats and a large boat are moored. Ellery and his two companions returned to the western road and walked along the path towards the water, only to find himself standing on a smaller dock a few hundred yards away from the main dock. On the other side of the water, less than a mile away, lay Oyster Island. Its coastline looked like the island had twisted its body and separated from the mainland, slightly expanding in the process. Ellery couldn't see the other side of the island, but he judged that the inspiration for its name came from its silhouette.
[917] Oyster Island was like a green gem, set against the background of the Long Island Sound, with a patchwork of primitive forests covering the entire island, extending almost to the water's edge. No...there was a small dock. Ellery squinted his eyes and could make out its gray outline, which seemed to be falling apart, but there were no other man-made structures in sight.
[918] Escham strode onto the dock and called out to a patrol boat that was idly drifting between the mainland and Oyster Island. As Ellery watched through the narrow strait to the west, he noticed another patrol boat's stern disappearing behind the island. The first patrol boat left the land and quickly approached the dock.
[919] "Alright, let's go," Vance said, sounding quite tense, as he stepped into the boat. "Quickly, Mr. Quine, maybe the island can tell us the answer."
[920] "Well, let's go," Vance said, sounding quite tense, as he stepped into the boat. "Quickly, Mr. Quine, maybe the island can tell us the answer."
[921] Ellery and Escham jump in, and the patrol boat turns sharply, heading straight towards the center of Oyster Island.
[922] As they cross the bay, they gradually see the island and the mainland. Near the dock where they boarded, they see another dock that leads west, clearly meant for the Reins. A rowboat is tied to a mooring post, looking a bit faded in the sunlight. On the opposite side of the bay, to the east, a replica of the Reins' dock appears.
[923] "Is Temple living over there?" Ellery asks.
[924] "Yes, that's where he must have landed." The eastern dock is empty of boats.
[925] The patrol boat turns around in the water. As they approach the small dock on Oyster Island, the island's details come into view. They sit silently, watching the island grow larger.
[926] Suddenly, Officer Vaughan jumped up, his face flushed with excitement, exclaiming, "What's happening over there!"
[927] They stared at the dock, where a man's silhouette emerged from the shrubs, carrying a woman who struggled and made faint cries, as he clumsily jumped into a motorboat tied up at the dock on the west side. He tossed the woman onto the seatboard of the boat, started the engine, and drove the boat away from the dock, heading straight for the approaching police boat. The woman lay motionless, as if unconscious. When the man turned to look at the island, they could see his dark face.
[928] Just seconds after the escape - if that indeed was an escape - a startling apparition emerged from the same path the escapee had taken, running from the woods.
[929] It was a naked man, a tall, broad-shouldered, dark-skinned, muscular fellow with black hair that swayed in the wind as he ran. Ellery thought of Tarzan, and he was almost ready to see Tarzan's bulky, incredible companions emerge from the woods. But where was the loincloth...? ...When this Tarzan briefly stopped on the dock and stared at the departing boat, they could hear his disappointed curses. He stood there for a moment, his muscular arms hanging loosely, completely unaware that he was naked. His eyes were only on the departing boat, and the man on the boat looked back nervously, clearly unaware of what lay ahead.
[930] Then, in the blink of an eye, the naked man disappeared. He plunged into the water from the dock's edge, splitting the water like a harpoon. He reappeared almost immediately, quickly swimming towards the escapee, closing the distance quickly.
[931] "A complete fool!" Escham exclaimed. "Does he want to catch up to a boat?"
[932] "The boat has stopped," Ellery said coldly.
[933] Escham was startled, staring at the boat. It lay motionless in the water a hundred yards from shore, the driver frantically fiddling with the engine at the stern.
