Remember, we didn't find any Crosetti fingerprints in the investigation of the Van case."
[1117] "Did Crosetti show up on March 1st?"
[1118] "No, sir. No one by that name came, but an old man by the name of Banesh [1] - Ha! Harlock, was it? - he came, along with a man named Roman, and they paid the remainder of the rent in cash and stayed."
[1119] Vance and Eames agreed not to ask any more about Crosetti. It was clear that the old man couldn't provide any more information on that front. The officer put the letter in his pocket and began asking about the argument between Braid and Harlock. He had always suspected that Braid was a nudist, and when it became apparent that the group was indeed a nudist colony, Braid had come to the island to announce the general opposition to it from the mainland society. However, it seemed that Harlock was impervious to soft or hard tactics, and Roman was belligerent. In desperation, Braid offered them double compensation; he even suggested paying them a large sum of money to give up their lease.
[1120] "By the way, who signed the lease?"
[1121] "That old scoundrel," Quetcham answered.
[1122] Harakat and Roman rejected Bread's proposal. Later, Bread threatened to take legal action, claiming that their claims were indecent. Roman countered that they had not harmed anyone and that the island was remote from the main roads, effectively belonging to them during their tenancy. Therefore, Bread tried to persuade Quetcham to take legal action to evict them for the same reason.
[1123] "But they didn't harm me and my wife," the old man said. "Mr. Bread offered me a thousand dollars if I would do it. No, sir, I, Quetcham, won't; I, Quetcham, don't know how to sue."
[1124] Quetcham continued, recounting the last and most intense argument, which took place just three days ago, on Sunday. Bread crossed the bay, like Menelaus going to Troy, and encountered Striker in the woods, where they engaged in a heated argument, with the little brown-bearded man going mad. "I think he had a fit," Quetcham said calmly. "Roman, the beast of strength, intervened and ordered Mr. Bread to leave the island. I watched from the woods; it wasn't my business. Mr. Bread refused to leave, and Roman grabbed Mr. Bread's collar and said, 'Hey, you, get out of here, or I'll beat you up so badly that your mother won't recognize you!' Mr. Bread had to leave, shouting that he would settle the score with them, even if it meant bankrupting himself."
[1125] Vane wiped his hands again. "Well, Mr. Quetcham. I wish there were more people like you around. Tell me, have any other mainlanders had a spat with Harakat and Roman?"
[1126] "Of course," the old Ketcham said, smiling slyly. "Jonah Lincoln, the guy who lives at Bread's place. He got into a fight with Roman last week, right here."
[1127] "Ah, ah?"
[1128] Old Ketcham grew more animated, his eyes shining. "Beautiful, that girl. She walked up, stripped off her clothes, and stood right in front of them! She was pissed off at her brother for meddling and told him that ever since she was a kid, he had been bossing her around and controlling her, and now she was going to do whatever she wanted. ... Let me tell you, that's what happened. I saw it through the trees..."
[1129] "Ketcham, you bull!" a woman's voice called out from inside the cabin. "How can you be so shameless!"
[1130] "Well," Ketcham said, suddenly serious. "Anyway, when Lincoln learned that his sister didn't want to go back and saw her standing there naked, right in front of Roman—he didn't like that—he raised his hand and slapped Roman, and they started fighting. Lincoln got a beating, but he was stubborn; he was—like a man." Lincoln, took a hard hit, but he held up well. Roman threw him into the bay, really. Strong guy, Roman."
[1131] When they couldn't get any more information from the chatty old man, they returned to the police boat. Professor Adley was quietly smoking, and Dr. Temple was pacing on the deck, his purple face agitated.
[1132] "Did you find out anything?" Professor Adley asked mildly.
[1133] "A little."
[1134] As the police boat sped towards the mainland, they all fell into deep thought.
[1135] * * *
[1136] [1] In Irish and Scottish folklore, a banshee is a female spirit whose appearance or wailing foretells the death of someone in the family of the person who sees or hears her.
[1137] [2] In Greek mythology, King Menelaus of Sparta. His wife, Helen, was abducted by a Trojan prince, leading to the Trojan War.
[1138] Chapter 10: Dr. Temple's Adventure
[1139] The afternoon dragged on. The Essex County prosecutor left. Inspector Vance issued orders and received a series of illogical reports. Oyster Island remained quiet. Mrs. Bradstreet shut herself in her room, reportedly ill, while her daughter Helen attended to her. Jonah Lincoln paced nervously in the garden. Police and detectives yawned throughout Bradwood. Reporters came and went, and the evening air was filled with flashes of light.
[1140] Ellery was not tired and followed Professor Yardley across the street, through a tall stone gate, and along a gravel path to Professor Yardley's house. Both of them were in a melancholy mood, lost in their own thoughts.
[1141] The dusk came, followed by a starless night. As darkness fell, Oyster Island seemed to sink into the bay.
[1142] In silence, Ellery and his master did not discuss the strange problem they were trying to solve. They talked about happier times - the days at the university, the stubborn college president, Ellery's first foray into crime investigation, and Yarley's quiet life since they had parted. At eleven o'clock, Ellery put on a flannel nightgown and smiled, falling asleep. The professor spent an hour smoking and writing letters in his study before retiring.
[1143] Around midnight, there was a commotion at the doorstep of Dr. Temple's stone house. The doctor, dressed in black pants, a black shirt, and black buckled shoes, extinguished his pipe and silently stepped out of the doorstep, disappearing into the dark woods between his house and Bredwood.
[1144] The countryside seemed to have fallen asleep, except for the chirping of crickets.
[1145] Against the backdrop of trees and shrubs, he appeared as a dark silhouette, his skin tone indiscernible. A few feet from the roadside, he stood motionless behind a tree. Someone was walking heavily towards him along the path. Dr. Temple recognized the figure of a county police officer in uniform, clearly patrolling. The policeman walked past him, heading towards Chatham Bay.
[1146] Once the sound of the patrolman's footsteps faded away, Dr. Temple quietly crossed the road, disappearing into the wooded area of Bradwood, and silently walked westward. He made his way through Bradwood in half an hour without arousing suspicion from the occasional dark figures passing by. He passed the gazebo and totem pole, the high wire fence enclosing the tennis court, the main house, and the central walkway leading to Bradwood's wharf, and the Fox cottage, heading towards the westward road that separated Bradwood from the Lein residence.
[1147] Here, his muscles tensed, and Dr. Temple moved cautiously, like a ghost, through the woods of Lein, until he reached the front of the dark, looming house. He approached it from the front; now, he walked northward, where the trees were dense and almost touched the house itself. He crouched behind an old fig tree. Less than five feet away, a light was on in the window closest to him. The curtains were drawn. He could hear the sound of footsteps dragging in a bedroom. Mrs. Lein's plump figure occasionally passed by the curtain.
[1148] Dr. Temple crawled on all fours, feeling his way along the ground, inch by inch, until he reached the window's exact location below.
[1149] He heard a door close, and Mrs. Lein's voice, louder than usual, "Percy! Did you bury it?" Dr. Temple gritted his teeth, sweat trickling down his cheeks, but made no sound.
[1150] "Yes, yes. Please, Beth, don't be so loud!" Percy's voice was tense. "There are police everywhere in this damn place!"
[1151] Dr. Temple crept up to the window, pressing himself against the wall base and holding his breath. The curtains suddenly slid open, and Mrs. Reins looked out. Then the curtains were drawn closed again.
[1152] "Where is it?" Elizabeth Reins whispered.
[1153] Dr. Temple strained every muscle, listening intently, trembling with tension. Despite his best efforts, he could not hear Mrs. Reins' whispered response...
[1154] "They'll never find it," Reins said later, in a more normal tone of voice. "If we just stay still, we'll be safe."
[1155] "But Dr. Temple—I'm scared, Percy!"
[1156] Reine cursed under his breath, "I remember, it's okay. That was in post-war Budapest. The Bundy Reine incident... he should be damned! It's the same person, I swear."
[1157] "He didn't say anything," Reine's wife whispered, "maybe he forgot."
[1158] "He wouldn't! Last week, in Brest... he kept staring at me. Be careful, Beth. Our situation is dire—"
[1159] The lights flickered and went out; a mattress spring creaked; voices lowered to inaudible murmurs.
[1160] Dr. Temple remained in that position for a long time, but there were no more sounds. The Reine couple had fallen asleep.
[1161] He stood up, listening tensely for a few seconds, then sneaked back into the woods. A shadow slipped from one tree to another… As he made his way through the woods surrounding Ketcham's semi-circular bay, he could hear the sound of water hitting the pier.
[1162] He stood behind a tree again; faint voices came from the pier. He crept cautiously towards the coast. Suddenly, the black water gurgled almost at his feet. He widened his eyes: about ten feet from the shore, near the dark pier, a rowboat rocked. He could see two vague figures sitting in the middle of the boat, a man and a woman. The woman's arms were around the man, and she was fervently pleading with him.
[1163] "Why are you so heartless? Take me to the island. We'll be safer there—under the trees…"
[1164] The man's voice was low and cautious: "You're acting like a fool. It's dangerous there. Why did you choose tonight of all nights! Have you gone mad? Someone will be watching you, and this will have serious consequences. I told you we should split up, at least until this crisis is over!"
[1165] The woman took her arms off his neck and sobbed, "I know! You don't love me anymore. Oh, this is—"
[1166] He slapped her palm across her mouth, hissing fiercely: "Shut up! There are cops around!"
[1167] She relaxed in his arms. Then she pushed him away and slowly sat up. "You won't get her. I promise."
[1168] The man remained silent. He picked up an oar and rowed the boat to the shore. The woman stood up and was roughly shoved off the boat. Then, the man hastily pushed the boat away, rowing towards the island.
[1169] As the moon rose, Dr. Temple saw that it was Paul Roman who had rowed away.
[1170] The woman standing on the shore, pale and trembling, was Mrs. Bradwood.
[1171] Dr. Temple glared forward and disappeared into the bushes.
[1172] Chapter Eleven: Hullo, Mr. Fox!
[1173] The next morning, as Ellery walked along the gravel path of the Braidwoods, he saw the district attorney's car parked at the entrance. The faces of the police officers standing around were grim with anticipation. He suspected something important had happened and hurried up the steps of the colonial porch and into the house.
[1174] He brushed past Stollings and made his way to the living room, where he found a grinning Escham and a furious Detective Vance confronting Fox, who stood silently in front of Vance, his hands clenched; only his eyes betrayed his unease. Mrs. Braid, Helen, and Jonathan Lincoln stood by, like the Fates.
[1175] "Come in, Mr. Quin," Escham said cheerfully. "You're just in time. Fox, you're caught red-handed. What's the matter? You don't say anything?"
[1176] Ellery slipped quietly into the room. Fox stood motionless, even his lips taut. "I don't understand," he said, but it was clear he understood very well, and was steeling himself for the attack.
[1177] Vorn bared his teeth. "Don't play dumb here. You met Patsy Malone Tuesday night - the night Brad was murdered!"
[1178] "That night," Escham added, "you left Stollings and Mrs. Baxter. At eight o'clock, Fox."
[1179] Fox stood like a statue. His lips turned pale.
[1180] "Well?" the officer growled. "What do you have to say, you fool? Why did an innocent driver visit the headquarters of a New York gang?"
[1181] Fox blinked again but didn't answer.
[1182] "Don't want to talk, huh?" The officer moved towards the door. "Mike, bring the ink here!"
[1183] A plainclothes policeman immediately appeared with ink and paper. Fox let out a suppressed cry and lunged towards the door. The plainclothes policeman quickly dropped the ink and paper, grabbed Fox's arms, and the officer tightly gripped his legs, throwing the struggling Fox to the ground. Fox was subdued and, without resistance, allowed Vance to pull him up.
[1184] Helen Brad watched in terror, while Mrs. Brad looked indifferent. Lincoln stood up and turned his back.
[1185] "Take his fingerprints," the officer said sternly. The plainclothes policeman grabbed Fox's right hand, pressed his fingers into the ink, and then skillfully pressed them onto the paper; he repeated the process with Fox's left hand. Fox looked pained.
[1186] "Check it immediately," the fingerprint expert rushed to obey the order. "Hey, my boy, Fox - if that's your name, I'm clear that it's not - you better be clear, answer my questions. Why did you go see Malone?"
[1187] There was no answer.
[1188] "What's your real name? Where are you from?"
[1189] There was still no answer. The officer walked back to the door and beckoned two detectives standing in the hall. "Take him back to his hut and lock him up. We'll take care of him later."
[1190] Fox stumbled out between the two detectives, his eyes blazing. He avoided looking at Mrs. Bradshaw and Helen.
[1191] "Well!" The officer wiped his forehead. "I'm sorry, Mrs. Bradshaw, for all the commotion in your living room. But this guy is obviously a bad actor."
[1192] Mrs. Bradshaw shook her head. "I can't understand. He's always been a good young man, polite, efficient. You don't think he's –"
[1193] "If he is, then only God can help him."
[1194] "I'm sure he's not," Helen said sternly, her eyes full of pity. "Fox couldn't be a murderer or a thief. He doesn't socialize, that's true, but he's never been drunk or done anything else wrong. He's also a well-cultured man, I often see him reading good books and poetry."
[1195] "These guys can be quite cunning, Miss Bradshaw," Isam said. "As far as we know, he's been pretending to be someone else since he got here. We checked his credentials, and they're real – but he's only been working for that guy for a few months."
[1196] "Maybe he took the job just to get the credentials," said Vance. "They'll do anything."
[1197] He turned to Ellery. "You can give your father a high score for this one, Mr. Quin: we got this tip from Quin officer, and his informants always seem to know more than any cop in New York."
[1198] "I knew Dad couldn't resist getting involved," Ellery grumbled. "Is your information so special?"
[1199] "The informant saw Fox go into Malone's headquarters. That's all it took. But it was enough."
[1200] Ellery shrugged. Helen said, "You people's problem is, you always assume the worst about everyone." Lincoln sat down and lit a cigarette. "Maybe, Helen, we shouldn't get involved in this."
[1201] "Maybe, Jonah, you should mind your own business!"
[1202] "Children," Mrs. Brad weakly said.
[1203] Ellery sighed. "Any news, Mr. Escham? I'm eager for new information."
[1204] The officer smiled. "Here, take a stab at this." He pulled a stack of printed papers from his pocket and handed them to Ellery. "If you can find something in there, you're a genius. But...," he said sharply, turning to Lincoln who was getting up to leave the room, "Don't go, Mr. Lincoln. I want to ask you something."
[1205] Timing was perfect, and Ellery admired the officer's flexible and cautious strategy. Lincoln stopped immediately, his face flushed. The two women in the chairs looked uncomfortable. Suddenly, the air in the room went from gentle to tense.
[1206] "What is it?" Lincoln asked laboriously.
[1207] "Why," Vance asked gently, "did you lie to me yesterday and tell me that Miss Braid and her mother came back together on Monday night?"
[1208] "I... well, what do you mean?"
[1209] Essex said, "It seems you people are doing everything in your power to hinder, rather than help, the investigation of your husband's murder, Mrs. Braid. The officer's men, from the taxi driver who brought you two from the station to Braidwood on Monday night, found out—"
[1210] "Two?" Ellery asked, drawing out the word.
[1211] "—only Lincoln and Miss Brad were in the taxi, Mrs. Brad!"
[1212] Helen jumped up; Mrs. Brad was speechless with shock. "Don't say anything, Mom. It's humiliating! Are you suggesting that one of us is involved in this murder, Mr. Esham?"
[1213] Lincoln muttered, "Listen, Helen, maybe we should—"
[1214] "Jonah!" She turned to him, trembling, "If you dare say anything, I will—I will never speak to you again!"
[1215] He bit his lip, avoiding her gaze, and left the room. Mrs. Brad let out a soft sob, and Helen stood in front of her, as if to protect her from harm.
[1216] "Oh," Essex said, raising his hands, "What do you think, Mr. Quin? That's what official investigators face. Well, Miss Breda. But you should know, from now on, everyone—I mean everyone—will be suspected of murdering Thomas!"

[1220] Chapter Twelve: The Professor's Speech
[1221] Ellery Queen, the somewhat bewildered special investigator, like a dog with a bone, eagerly, with a progress report in hand, crossed the street to his master's house. The midday sun was too hot to go to the store, so Ellery looked for coolness indoors. He found Professor Yardley in a "thousand and one nights" room with a courtyard inlaid with marble and decorated with Arabesque motifs. It looked like an inner courtyard of a harem, and the most pleasant thing was the pool overflowing with water.
[1222] "Wow!" said Ellery. "I'm grateful for your little harem, Professor."
[1223] "As usual," said the professor seriously, "you choose your words too hastily. Don't you know that a man's home is called an outer room? ...Take off your clothes, Quine, and come here. What do you have?"
[1224] "A message from Garcia. Don't move, let's study this together. I'll be back in a minute."
[1225] Soon he reappeared in swimming trunks, his upper body smooth and shiny with sweat. He jumped into the pool, splashing water and getting the professor wet, extinguishing his pipe. He splashed energetically around.
[1226] "Another of your achievements," Yardley roared, "swimming is always so clumsy. Get out of here and don't soak me through."
[1227] Ellery grinned, climbed out, and stretched out on the marble, reaching for Vance's stack of reports.
[1228] "What do we have here?" His gaze fell on the top one, "Hmm. Seems like there's not much. The commendable officer didn't laze around. He checked with Hancock County."
[1229] "Oh," the professor said, laboriously relighting his pipe, "so they checked, did they? What happened there?"
[1230] "First, the findings of the autopsy of Andrew Van. Nothing worth mentioning. If you've read as many autopsy reports as I have, you'll appreciate... A complete account of the preliminary investigation. Nothing beyond the known, nothing you couldn't read in the newspapers at the time... Ah, what's this? 'According to,' let's think about this; it sounds just like Crosetto - 'according to the investigation by the district attorney of Arroyo Elementary School's Andrew Van and the recently murdered Long Island millionaire Thomas Bride, we regretfully state that there is no such relationship; at least, we can determine from a careful study of the deceased Van's past correspondence, etc.' Quite succinct, isn't it?"
[1231] "A rhetorical masterpiece," the professor grinned.
[1232] "But that's all. Alors[1], let's leave Arroyo and go back to the Gulf of Chiquem.” Ellery glanced at the fourth sheet. "Dr. Rumsen's report on the body of Thomas Braid. Nothing we didn't know, really. The body itself showed no signs of violence, the internal organs showed no signs of poisoning, etc., etc., ad nauseam[2]. Commonplace stuff."
[1233] "I remember you asked Dr. Rumsen if Braid could have been strangled. What did he say?"
[1234] "He said there were no signs of asphyxiation. Ergo[3], he wasn't strangled."
[1235] "But why did you ask that question first?"
[1236] Ellery waved a wet arm. "No great reason. But since there were no signs of violence in the rest of the body, it might be important to find out how he was killed. You know, it had to be the head first; it made me think of strangulation. But Dr. Rumsen said in his report that it could only have been a blunt object hitting the skull or a gunshot to the head. All things considered, I had to say that first."
[1237] The professor kicked up a spray of water. "I see. Anything else?"
[1238] "They also investigated to find out the route the killer took to escape. Worthless, very worthless."
[1239] Ellery shook his head. "It's impossible to get a list of people who traveled by train in the Gulf area during the time of the crime. The police on the roads and the residents nearby and on the roads couldn't provide any information. The police tried to find out who was in the Gulf of Ketchum or near it on Tuesday evening, but they failed... The captain and other people on the fast boats that sailed in the channel on Tuesday afternoon and evening reported no suspicious or mysterious activities, and there were no strange boats that could have landed the killer by water in the Gulf."
[1240] "As you say, all for nothing." The professor sighed. "He could have come by train, by car, by ship, and I think we'll never know for sure. By way of contradiction [4], he might have even come by seaplane." "I have an idea," Ellery smiled. "Don't make the mistake of calling the impossible ridiculous, professor. I've seen some strange things happen... Let's take a look at this." He quickly scanned another sheet. "Nothing again. The ropes used to tie Braid's arms and legs to the totem pole..." [1241] "I guess," Yardley muttered, "you'll say the investigation of the totem pole is useless."
[1241] "I guess," Adler mumbled, "you'll say that investigating the totem pole is also useless."
[1242] "The totem pole," Ellery continued diligently, "the police found that the ropes were ordinary clotheslines that could be bought at any grocery or hardware store. No merchant within ten miles of Bradwood could provide anything that could serve as a clue. However, Escham reported that Vane's people would conduct a wider investigation."
[1243] "How meticulous these people are," the professor said.
[1244] "Although I don't like to admit it," Ellery grinned, "it is often the strictness of the routine that can unravel the process of the crime… the knot, Vane's favorite point of attention. The result - zero. According to Vane's experts, the knot was clumsy but effective. It was just the kind of knot that you and I could tie."
[1245] "I'm different," Adler said. "You know, I'm a sailor. Halyards, half hitches, and so on, I can tie them all."
[1246] "Your habit of misusing words," the professor said, "really should be regrettable."
[1247] "Vorn's boys say that the man's background is unknown. As he said himself, all we know is that he joined our Egyptology group in Pittsburgh in February. We found nothing else about him. His previous track is a blank."
[1248] "The Rein couple?"
[1249] Ellery put the file aside for a moment. "Ah, the Rein couple," he whispered, "do you know anything about them?"
[1250] The professor stroked his beard. "You're suspicious, aren't you, old boy? I knew I couldn't escape your scrutiny. There are some suspicious things about them. Although they are very respectable, as far as I know, they are blameless."
[1251] Ellery picked up the papers again. "Well, Scotland Yard, despite the lack of details, I'm sure they don't think so. Esham sent a telegram to Scotland Yard, and they replied, according to this report, that they couldn't find a couple named Percy and Elizabeth Reins who match the description. Their passports were also checked, of course, and there were no problems, which was to be expected. Perhaps we have been a bit ungrateful... Scotland Yard says they are continuing to search the resident records - and the criminal records - in the hope of finding material about the Reins couple, since they claim to be British citizens."
[1252] "Oh, dear, what a mess!"
[1253] Ellery frowned. "You just discovered it? I have handled many complicated cases in my short and brilliant career, but I have never encountered such a mess... Of course, you haven't heard about the latest developments regarding Mr. Fox, the driver friend, and Mrs. Breda."
[1254] "It's like the water in the Ganges," Yadley muttered. "I'm beginning to suspect the whole thing."
[1255] "What do you suspect?"
[1256] The professor shrugged. "I shouldn't jump to conclusions. What else does your 'encyclopedia' reveal?"
[1257] "About work on the Wain side. The doorman at the Park Theatre confirmed that a woman resembling Mrs. Brad left the theatre during the first act of the play on Tuesday night – around nine o'clock."
[1258] "Alone?"

[1261] Ellery sighs. "Jumping to conclusions? I thought you'd be more careful in this regard. The name was printed, and the address was simply Pekoria – obviously, the wanderer who distributed manna everywhere had stopped there and done some small business with the locals... another good deed for the locals. The accountant is checking the accounts of the Brad-Megal Company. Naturally, it's a normal investigative step. So far, everything looks above board; the company has a good reputation and is thriving; the financial situation is normal... incidentally, our traveler Stephen Megalos is idling somewhere at sea, uninterested in business affairs for five years. Brad has been responsible for overseeing the company, but young Jonathan Lincoln has practically taken over the place. I don't know what's bothering him."

[1264] Adler's pipe still hung in mid-air. "What?"

Apparently, the district attorney, Krutmit, held it back in his first response and later decided to completely withdraw, dumping the whole thing on poor Esam... six months of investigation. The material is rich... Vilga Krossak is a Black Mountain man!"
[1266] "Montenegro? You mean the birthplace? Because you know, Montenegro no longer exists as a country today,” Adler said interestedly, "It has become a region of the present-day Yugoslavia[10] - formally merged in 1918 by Serbia, Croatia, and Slovenia."
[1267] "Well. Krulmit's investigation revealed that Crosack was one of the first immigrants from Montenegro to America after the peace was declared in 1918. His passport only stated that he was born in Montenegro, with no other value. The Tutankhamun's tomb incident[11] occurred at that time!”
[1268] "Did Krulmit find anything about his life in America?"
[1269] "Slightly but sufficiently. He seemed to travel from one city to another, probably to get acquainted with the new country and learn the language. For years he did small business, apparently all legally. He sold fashionable embroidery, small woven mats, and so on.”
[1270] "They all do that," the professor said.
[1271] Erlie pondered the next passage. "Four years ago in Chattanooga, Tennessee [12], he met his friend Harkness, or Stray, and the two teamed up. Stray was selling 'sun pills' - with his own brand of cod liver oil - and Crocetta became his business manager and, for mutual benefit, his 'disciple', helping the poor old man build up the sun cult and preach health teachings on the road."
[1272] "Is there any information about Crocetta after the Alamo murder?"
[1273] Erlie's face fell. "No. He disappeared without a trace, doing a good job of it."
[1274] "What about Klint, Van's servant?"
[1275] "There's no trace of him. It's like the earth swallowed them both. Klint's complicated situation has been bothering me. Where is he? If Crocetta sent his soul to the afterlife, where is his body - where did Crocetta bury it? I dare say, professor, we won't solve this case until we know Klint's true fate... Crocetta made great efforts to find the relationship between Klint and Crocetta, assuming that they might have an alliance. But he found nothing."
[1276] "That doesn't necessarily mean there is no connection."
[1277] "Of course not. As for Crosse, we can't be sure if he had any communication with Strach."
[1278] "Strach…just a case of divine retribution," Adler muttered. "Poor devil!"
[1279] Ellery grinned. "Strengthen your resolve, sir; this is a murder. By the way, from this last report, the West Virginian tracked Strach to his residence. That means they found out he was Alva Strach, as Krummy said, a famous Egyptologist. As you said, many years ago in the Valley of the Kings he went mad from heatstroke. It has been established that he has no kin, and he appears to be a completely harmless madman. Look here - Krummy's notes: 'The Hanover County District Attorney believes that Alva Strach, who claims to be Harlatch or La-Harlatch, is innocent of the murder of Andrew Van, but has been a victim of shameless opportunists for years who have exploited his strange appearance and slight mental instability, as well as his bizarre and harmful obsession with confused pagan worship. We also believe that a person of this type, with an unknown motive for killing Van, is responsible for the victim's death. All evidence points to Wilga Crosse as this person.' Neat, isn't it?"
[1280] "A case with a situation evidence [13] flavor against Crosse, is it?" the professor asked.
[1281] Ellery shook his head. "Whether it's circumstantial evidence or not, Crummett hit the nail on the head by choosing Crosetti as the likely killer of Vane."
[1282] "Why do you think so?" asked the professor.
[1283] "It's the facts," Ellery leaned forward. "But Crosetti killing Andrew Vane is not the main point we are trying to crack this case. The key question is, who is Crosetti?" asked Ellery eagerly.
[1284] "What do you mean?" asked Professor Adler.
[1285] "I mean that only one person knows the true identity of Wilga Crosetti in this case," Ellery replied eagerly, "and that's Strick, but we can't get any reliable testimony from him. So I'll say it again: who is Crosetti? Who is Crosetti now? He could be anyone around us!"
[1286] "Nonsense," the professor said uneasily. "A black man, probably with a Croatian accent, and with a limp on his left leg…"
[1287] "Not entirely nonsense, professor. People of different nationalities mix freely in this country, so of course, when Krosok talked to Mr. Crocker, the owner of Wilton's Garage, he spoke flawless English. As for the fact that Krosok might be among us, I don't think you've fully analyzed all the factors of the Brad case."
[1288] "Oh, have I not?" Professor Yardley said hastily. "Maybe not. But let me tell you, young man, you're rushing things."
[1289] "I've analyzed it before," Ellery stood up and jumped back into the pool. As his head emerged, dripping wet, he grinned at the professor. "I don't even need to mention the fact that Krosok placed the Cult of the Sun in the vicinity of Bradwood! Before the murder of Van, please note. Significant, isn't it? He might have been around there… Come on!"
[1290] The professor muttered, lighting his pipe with two matches. Ellery stretched out on the heated stones, lit a cigarette, and closed his eyes. "Now consider the situation from the perspective of the motive. Central Europe? The Balkan Peninsula? The center of superstition and violence; almost cliché. What does it mean to you?"
[1291] "I'm completely ignorant of the Balkan countries," said the professor coldly. "The only association I have with the word is that it's been the source of spells and absurd legends for centuries. I think it's the result of intellectual backwardness and mountainous isolation."
[1292] "Ha! An idea," said Ellery, grinning. "Vampire. You're recalling Dracula [14] - the evil element added by Bram Stoker to the nightmare of innocent citizens - isn't it? A human vampire story, set in Central Europe. Also decapitation!"
[1293] "Nonsense," said Yardley with an anxious look forward.
[1294] "Yes," said Ellery quickly. "That's nonsense, as long as Brad and Range's hearts aren't being driven into stakes [15]. No self-respecting vampire believer would skip that delightful little ceremony. If we find stakes, I can almost be sure we're dealing with someone who's gone crazy with superstition, trying to get rid of what he thinks is a vampire."
[1295] "You're not serious," protested Yardley.
[1296] Ellery took a puff of his cigarette. "I don't know if it's serious or not. You know, professor, we can use our cultural contempt to disdain the belief in vampires and such primitive fears, but if Crocetta believes in vampires and goes around decapitating people, you can't just close your eyes to that reality. It's almost a utilitarian philosophy. If he exists…"
[1297] "How about your Egyptian cross?" the professor asked seriously; he sat up straighter to be more comfortable, as if preparing for a long conversation.
[1298] Ellery sat up, hugging his brown knees. "Oh, what's wrong? You have something to say; you hinted at it yesterday. Did I make a foolish mistake?"
[1299] The professor calmly tapped his empty pipe and put it on the edge of the pool beside him, then ruffled his black beard and assumed a professorial air. "My child," he said solemnly, "you have done a foolish thing."
[1300] Ellery furrowed his brow. "Do you mean that the T-shaped cross is not an Egyptian cross?"
[1301] "That's exactly what I mean."
[1302] Ellery shook his body lightly. "A voice of authority… Well. Would you like to make a small bet, professor?"
[1303] "I'm not a gambler; I don't have that kind of income… Where did you get the idea that the crux commissa was called an Egyptian cross?"
[1304] "The Encyclopaedia Britannica. About a year ago, I had the opportunity to do some research on the topic of crosses; I was writing a novel. According to what I remember now, the T-shaped cross is said to be a common object in Egypt, often called an Egyptian cross or similar terms. Anyway, I remember that the article definitely linked it to the cross. Would you like to check it out?"
[1305] The professor chuckled. "I believe you. I don't know who wrote that article—as far as I know, it could be someone very erudite. But the Encyclopedia, like any other man-made thing, has its errors; it's not the final word. I'm not an authority on Egyptian art, please understand, but it's part of my work, and I can tell you without equivocation that I have never encountered the term 'Egyptian cross'; I'm sure it's a misnomer. Indeed, there is something in Egyptian that is described as T-shaped…"
[1306] Ellery was confused. "Then why do you say that the T-cross is not... "
[1307] "Because it isn't," Adler smiled. "In ancient Egyptian artifacts, there is a symbol shaped like the Greek letter T. It appears frequently in hieroglyphics. But that doesn't make it an Egyptian cross; the cross is an ancient Christian symbol. There are many such coincidences. For example, the cross of Saint Anthony [17] also uses the name of the T-cross because it resembles the T-shaped staff that Saint Anthony is often depicted holding. Strictly speaking, it is not the Saint Anthony cross any more than our T-shaped staff. "
[1308] "So, that T doesn't necessarily mean an Egyptian cross," Ellery muttered. "Damn, I've mixed them all up."
[1309] "If you want to call it that, I can't stop you," the professor said. "Yes, the cross has seemed to be a sufficiently familiar symbol for a long time, and its uses have varied greatly and widely from the primitive era. I can give you many examples of the changes in the symbolism of the cross, such as the cross used by the Native Americans before the arrival of the Spaniards. But that has nothing to do with it. The point is, "the professor furrowed his brow, "if there is a cross-shaped symbol that you can loosely call an Egyptian cross, it is the ankh [18]."
[1310] "Ankh?" Ellery looked thoughtful. "Maybe that's what I was really thinking about. Is it a T-cross with a circle on top?"
[1311] Yadley shook his head. "Not a circle, my child, but a waterdrop or pear-shaped little figure. The ankh is essentially like a key, called the Crux Ansata [19], which appears frequently in Egyptian inscriptions. It represents god or royalty, able to give the owner the identity of a life-giver."
[1312] "Life-giver?" Something was brewing in Elyot's eyes. "Wow! That's it! It's the Egyptian cross! Something told me we were on the right track!"
[1313] "Explain yourself, young man."
[1314] "You don't see it? It's plain as day!" Elyot exclaimed loudly. "The ankh—the symbol of life. The horizontal stroke of the T—the arms; the vertical stroke—the body; the pear-shaped thing on top—the head. And the head being cut off! That means—Croesus deliberately changed the symbol of life into the symbol of death!"
[1315] The professor looked at him for a moment, then burst out into a long mocking laugh. "Excellent, my child, very good, but a million miles from the truth."
[1316] Ellery's excitement subsided. "Where did I go wrong?"
[1317] "If the Ankh, or the Crux Ansata, is considered a symbol of a statue, your inspired interpretation of Crooks cutting off the victim's head may be correct. But it's not, Quine. It has a much more ordinary source."
[1318] The professor sighed. "Do you remember the slippers that Striker wore? They were a replica of a typical Egyptian shoe... I don't want to be quoted on this - after all, I'm just an anthropologist, not an Egyptologist - but the Ankh is usually considered to represent a shoelace, like the one Striker used, the knot at the top being part of the band that goes around the ankle. The vertical part of the band below the knot is the part that goes over the shoe and down to the big toe and the other toes of the sole. Shorter, parallel ones go down from the sides of the foot to the sole."
[1319] Ellery looked dejected. "But I still don't understand how, if its origin is a shoe, the symbol could represent the creation of life, even metaphorically."
[1320] The professor shrugged. "The origin of a word or concept can sometimes be incomprehensible to modern psychology. The entire evolutionary process from a scientific perspective is not very clear. But since the Ankh symbol, as a stem of the word 'life', is frequently used in writing various words, it eventually becomes a symbol of life or life. Although the material it comes from is soft - naturally, shoes are usually made of processed papyrus - the Egyptians eventually used this symbol in a hard form - wooden amulets, etc. But the symbol itself has never indicated a statue."
[1321] The professor chuckled. "You're not giving up, are you?... No, as far as I know."
[1322] Ellery firmly put his glasses back on. "So we abandon the Egyptian theory altogether! At least that's what I'm doing. The most recent worrying sign is that I keep abandoning my original ideas; my brain must be rusting."
[1323] "As Pope said, my child," the professor said, "half-knowledge is a killer."
[1324] "And," Ellery countered, "faciunt nae intelligendo, ut nihil intelligant...[21] Knowledge is too much that brings about complete ignorance. Of course, not intentionally to anyone—"
[1325] "Of course not," Adler said seriously, "Terence[22] didn't mean that, did he?... Anyway, I think you're trying to explain these crimes using Egyptology. You always tend to romanticize, I remember even in school. Once, we were discussing the origin of the Atlas[23] legend, because it was told by Plato, Herodotus[24] and—"
[1326] "Allow me to interrupt you," Ellery said impatiently, "I'm trying to find my way out of a quagmire, and you're muddying it with irrelevant classical knowledge. Sorry... If Crocker decapitated the victims and scattered T-shaped symbols near the crime scene, it couldn't have been the Ankh cross, but only the T cross. In ancient Egypt, the significance of the T cross was negligible, probably not in Crocker's mind, even though he was associated with a madman obsessed with Egyptian religious matters... Confirmed? Yes. Thomas Brad was hung on a totem pole, sorry, a totem pole. This is another religious symbol. Let's confirm further—if Crocker wanted to imply the Ankh cross, he would have left the head intact, not decapitated it... So we have our doubts about the Egyptian-based conjectures. We have no evidence for the American totem hypothesis, except for the single fortuitous fact that Brad was crucified in a place—it was chosen obviously because of its T-shaped meaning, not any religious meaning—we cannot persist in the hypothesis of the cross... The T cross in Christian faith... As far as I know, beheading has never been used in the execution of martyrs... Ergo, we abandon all religious conjectures—"
[1327] "Your creed," the professor said with a smile, "seems like Rabelais' [25] religion—a big 'maybe'."
[1328] "—and go back to the thing that was right in front of me all along." Ellery ended his speech with a regretful smile.
[1329] "What is it?"
[1330] "Just that T might just be T, nothing else that damn thing." T on its letter. T, T—" Suddenly he stopped speaking, and the professor curiously regarded him. Ellery stared at the pool, his eyes as if he had never seen such a simple thing as blue water and golden sunlight.
[1331] "What's wrong?" Yadley asked.
[1332] "Is it possible?" Ellery grumbled, "No... too coincidental. And it cannot be verified. I once thought -" He dragged out the sound, not even hearing Yadley's question. The professor sighed and picked up his pipe again. For a long time, the two men said nothing.
[1333] Two almost naked people sat quietly on the terrace, and suddenly an old black woman burst in, her shining black face filled with anger.
[1334] "Mr. Yadley," she said with a gentle complaint, "someone wants to break in."
[1335] "Is that so?" Professor Yadley was surprised and dropped his reverie, "Who is it?"
[1336] "That policeman. He seems drunk."
[1337] "Well, let him in."
[1338] Vance burst in a moment later, waving a piece of paper and flushed with excitement. "Quinn!" he exclaimed, "Big news!"
[1339] Ellery sat up, blinking. "Really? Oh, hello, officer. What news do you have?"
[1340] "Read this." The officer threw the paper onto the marble floor and sat down on the edge of the pool, panting.
[1341] Ellery and the professor exchanged glances before looking at the paper together. It was a telegram from Jamaica.
[1342] Arrived today, heard of Brad's death, going to New York immediately.
[1343] Signed: Stephen Megalas.

[1345] [1] Alors, French, "So be it."
[1346][2] ad nauseam, Latin, "to the point of nausea."
[1347][3] ergo, Latin, "therefore."
[1348][4] Also known as "reductio ad absurdum," a method of proving the truth of a proposition by proving that its opposite is false.
[1349][5] A half hitch, a simple knot that is easy to untie.
[1350][6] The chemical formula of a water molecule, composed of hydrogen and oxygen elements.
[1351] The Ganges River is famous for its turbidity, and Yad uses this as a metaphor for how he has been thoroughly confused by the case.
[1352] Located in the American Midwest, the capital is Springfield. Chicago is the largest city in Illinois.
[1353] "Manna," a food from the story of the Jewish and Christian Bibles. According to legend, when the Jews left Egypt to go to Canaan, they ran out of food in the wilderness, and heavenly food, white in color, was rained down. The Jews did not recognize it and asked each other, "What is it?" (meaning "What is this?"), hence the name.
[1354] A country established on the southern Balkan Peninsula after World War I, composed of six independent constituent republics: Bosnia and Herzegovina, Croatia, Macedonia, Montenegro, Serbia, and Slovenia. From 1992 to 2006, all nations except Serbs in Yugoslavia gradually gained independence, and the country began to disintegrate. Yugoslavia became a historical term. This book was written in 1932, at a time when Montenegro was part of Yugoslavia. In 2006, Montenegro officially declared independence.
[1355] Sarcophagus, a type of stone or clay coffin used in ancient Egypt, Greece, and Rome. Egyptian sarcophagi were used to protect mummies and often featured door and window patterns and head sculptures. In 1922, British Egyptologist Howard Carter discovered the intact tomb of Pharaoh Tutankhamun (1361-1352 BC), who ruled during the 18th dynasty of Egypt, containing a golden coffin, the mummified body of the pharaoh, and numerous valuable artifacts.
[1356][12] Located in the southeastern United States, the capital is Nashville.
[1357][13] Indirect evidence. In English and American law, a situation caused by the defendant must have a necessary connection to be used as evidence for conviction.
[1358][14] Count Dracula, the vampire king in Bram Stoker's (1847-1912) horror novel "Dracula."
[1359][15] In Western superstition, vampires are said to fear crosses, garlic, and sunlight, and can be destroyed by hitting them on the head with a stake or piercing their hearts.
[1360][16] Latin for "crucial sin."
[1361] Saint Anthony (251?-356?), an ancient Egyptian hermit, is believed to have founded the ancient Christian hermitage.
[1362] A T-shaped symbol with a circle on top, which symbolizes life in ancient Egypt.
[1363] "Tau-cross" in English, meaning a cross with a T shape.
[1364] Alexander Pope (1688-1744), an 18th-century British poet and a prominent Enlightenment figure, is considered one of the greatest poets in English literature.
[1365] "Ignorantiae Causa" in Latin, meaning "Ignorance is caused by too much knowledge."
[1366] Terence (186?-161 BC), a Roman comic writer, was a slave.
[1367] Atlas, a giant in Greek mythology who supported the sky on his shoulders. According to legend, his dwelling turned into the Atlas Mountains of Africa, which support the sky.
[1368] Herodotus (484?-430/420 BC), a Greek historian, is known as the "Father of History."
[1369] François Rabelais (1483-1553), a French satirist, is best known for his work "Gargantua and Pantagruel."

[1371] As an officer of the Brussels court, I have found that the mindset of criminals is often difficult for law-abiding citizens to understand. [1]
[1372] Felix Brounage [2]

[1374] [1] The original text is in French.
[1375] [2] Felix Brounage.
[1376] Chapter 13: The Secret of Neptune
[1377] Stephen Megalas' yacht, the Helen, sailed at full speed from Jamaica northward, passing through the Bahamas archipelago, but encountered serious engine trouble near New Providence Island, forcing Captain Swift to dock in Nassau for repairs. It took several days before it could set sail again.
[1378] Thus, it was not until July 1st, eight days after Inspector Vane received a telegram from Megalas, that the Helen approached the Long Island coast. Port authorities made arrangements for it to quickly pass through New York Harbor. The Helen, after a brief delay, was accompanied by a police boat and several small vessels as it entered Long Island Sound. These small vessels were hired by ambitious reporters who found it difficult to leave the Helen's deck, which had been polished with a grindstone [1].
[1379] Eight days... Eight days of calm and uneventful sailing. Even the funeral was a calm affair. Brad was buried in a rather unimpressive cemetery on Long Island, or rather, in an unsuitable environment. The press noticed that Mrs. Brad displayed remarkable fortitude in bearing this severe test. Even her daughter, who had no blood ties to the deceased, displayed more grief at the funeral than the widow herself.
[1380] The search for Werger Croscak was expanded into a nationwide manhunt. His physical description was sent to all police departments and county sheriffs' offices across the United States, as well as to all port officials; he was under surveillance by police in all forty-eight states, Canada, and Mexico. Despite the large net, no blackfish was caught; the man disappeared without a trace, as if he had flown into the sky from the ground. There was no trace of Clinton either.
[1381] The driver, Fox, remained under watch in the small house, despite not being formally arrested, and was effectively confined in a manner similar to that of being behind bars in a new-new prison. His investigation was proceeding quietly, but until Megalla arrived, his fingerprints were not found in any of the mugshots in the eastern criminal photo collection. The officer persistently sent fingerprint copies to the west. Fox remained silent. He did not complain about this informal confinement, but his eyes had a desperate gleam, which the officer closely monitored. Apart from strict surveillance, Vance paid him no attention, which was part of his skill. Fox was not interrogated, threatened, but strictly isolated alone. Although this was nerve-wracking, he did not collapse. He sat silently in his small house, day after day, rarely touching the food repeatedly sent from Mrs. Baxter's kitchen and hardly moving.
[1382] On July 1st, Friday, everything was ready. The Helen cut through Long Island Sound, passing through the narrow part west of Chatham Bay, and anchored in the deep water between Oyster Island and the mainland. The wharf at Bradwood was crowded with people - detectives, county police, state police, who watched the yacht slowly maneuvering. It was a gleaming white fast boat. The reflections of the bright brass fittings and the tiny moving figures on the deck were clearly visible in the clear morning air, as the small boats swayed around its long hull.
[1383] Vance, the district attorney Escham, Ellery, and Professor Yadley stood on the wharf, waiting quietly. A motorboat was lowered over the side and dropped into the small bay's waters with a thud. People saw several figures descend the iron ladder and board the motorboat. Soon, a police boat set sail, and the motorboat obediently followed behind, heading towards the wharf. The crowd stirred...
[1384] Stephen Megalla was a tall, burly man with tanned skin and a black beard, with a nose that looked as if it had been punched too hard in a boxing match. He was a lively and somehow ominous person. He jumped from the motorboat to the wharf with quick, steady, gentle, and nimble movements. Ellery studied him with great interest, feeling that he was a doer, unlike the bloated, overeating, prematurely aging man - Thomas Brad.
[1385] "I am Stephen Megalla," he suddenly spoke in English with an Eton College accent. "So many people to greet. Helen!" He singled her out from the crowd - she stood timidly behind her, along with her mother, Jonah, and Dr. Temple... Megalla took Helen's hand, ignoring everyone else, and looked tenderly into her eyes. She blushed and slowly withdrew her hand. Megalla raised his beard, smiled slightly, and murmured something in Brad's wife's ear, nodding briefly to Dr. Temple, and turned away. "So Tom was murdered? Who wants to introduce themselves, please."
[1386] The district attorney mutters, "Really?" and says, "I am Essem, the county district attorney. This is Detective Vorn of the Nassau County Police Department. Ellery Quine, special investigator. Professor Yadley, your new neighbor."
[1387] Megalas mechanically shakes hands with everyone. Then he turns to the elderly man in a blue uniform, with a stern and frosty face, who accompanied him on the motorboat. "Captain Swift, my captain," Megalas says. Captain Swift has a jaw that never stops chewing and eyes like telescope lenses - like the eyes of a wandering Jew, crystal clear, set in a weather-beaten face.
[1388] "Nice to meet you all," Captain Swift says without particularly addressing anyone, and puts his left hand on his hat. Ellery notices that the three fingers on that hand are missing. And when they all disembarked from the pier and walked towards the path leading to the house, Ellery found that the captain walked like a man walking in deep water.
[1389] "It's a shame I didn't get the news earlier," Megalas says quickly to Essem, walking alongside him. The Brad mother and daughter, Lincoln, and Dr. Temple follow without expression. "Tom's death is a blow. But as he speaks, he doesn't look like he's been hit; he talks about his partner's murder without emotion, as if he's talking about buying a new carpet.
[1390] "We've been waiting for you, Mr. Megalas," Detective Vorn says. "As far as you know, who had a motive to kill Mr. Brad?"
[1391] "Well," Meigal said, turning to look at Mrs. Bradley and Helen for a moment, "I'll hold off on answering that for now. Let me get the full story first."
[1392] Ellery was about to speak when he suddenly asked in a gentle voice, "Have you ever heard of a man named Andrew Van?"
[1393] Meigal's rhythmic stride was interrupted for a moment, but he continued on, his facial expression puzzling. "Andrew Van? What does he have to do with this?"
[1394] "So you know him!" Esam exclaimed.
[1395] "He was killed in a similar manner to your partner, Mr. Meigal," Ellery said.
[1396] "Van was murdered too!" The yacht owner's composure was shattered, his wild eyes flashing with unease.
[1397] "His head was severed, and his body was nailed into a T-shape," Ellery continued without emotion.
[1398] Megalas suddenly stopped in his tracks, and the entire procession behind him halted. His tanned face turned furious. "T!" he muttered under his breath, "Oh—let's go inside, gentlemen."
[1399] He spoke shakily, his shoulders slumping, his russet face turning a terrifyingly pale shade, and suddenly looking years older.
[1400] "Can you explain the T?" Ellery asked urgently.
[1401] "I have an idea…" Megala's teeth clicked together as he continued walking forward.
[1402] They walked the rest of the way to the house in silence.
[1403] Stollings opened the door, his welcoming smile immediately brightening. "Mr. Megala! I'm delighted to see you—"
[1404] Megala brushed past him without a glance, heading towards the living room where the others followed, striding purposefully across the floor. He seemed to be deep in thought. Mrs. Bradshaw hurried up to him, placing her plump hand on his arm.
[1405] "Stephen—if you could just clarify this terrible—"
[1406] "You know, Stephen!" Helen exclaimed.
[1407] "If you know, Megalas, please say it and end this dreadful suspense!" Lincoln said hoarsely. "It's a nightmare for all of us."
[1408] Megalas sighed, shoving his hands into his pockets. "Stay calm. Sit down, captain. I apologize for bringing you into such a despicable situation."
[1409] "You know, do you?" Wain asked calmly.
[1410] "Who?" Essem called out.
[1411] Meagal shrugged his broad shoulders. "A man named Wileg Croce. Croce... I have no doubt in my mind. T, you say? If its meaning is the same as what I think, then he is the only one who could have left it. T, is that right? In a way, it's a symbol of life... Tell me, what happened in the murder of Van and the murder of Brad."
[1412] Vance looked at Eames, who nodded. The officer began to summarize all the events that had happened in both cases, starting from when Old Pete and Michael O'Keefe found the school principal's body at the intersection of Arojo Street and the New Kowbran-Pewtown Road. When Vance mentioned the testimony of the garage owner Crocker, who had been hired to drive him to the intersection, Meagal nodded slowly, as if putting aside his last doubts. The narration ended, and Meagal smiled without humor.
[1413] "Now I understand," he regained his composure, his posture showing purpose and courage, "Now tell me, what did you find in the gazebo. What strange thing... "
[1414] "But Mr. Meagal, " Vance objected, "I don't see -"
[1415] "Take me there immediately," Meagal said briefly, and began to walk towards the door. Eames looked uncertain, but Ellery nodded at him. They all followed the yacht owner out the door.
[1416] As they walked along the path leading to the totem pole and gazebo, Professor Yardley whispered, "Well, Quine, it seems like the final act, doesn't it?"
[1417] Ellery shrugged. "I don't understand why. What I said about Crocker still applies. Where is the man? Unless Meagal can point him out in person -"
[1418] "That would be unreasonable, " the professor said. "How do you know he's even around here?"
[1419] "I don't know! But it must be possible."
[1420] The gazebo was covered with a tent, and a state trooper was on guard. Vance pulled back the tent, staring intently, and Meagal followed him inside. The gazebo looked exactly the same as when the investigators had discovered it the morning after the incident - the officer's careful planning seemed destined to have an effect.
[1421] Megala's gaze ignored the T-shaped symbol, the bloodstains, the signs of struggle, and the evidence of slaughter, focusing instead on one thing – the carved wooden pipe carved in the shape of a sea god's head and trident…
[1422] "That's exactly what I thought," he said calmly, bending down to pick up the pipe. "Detective Vance, when you mentioned the sea god pipe, I knew something was off."
[1423] "Off?" Vance was uneasy; Ellery's eyes sparkled with curiosity. "What's off, Mr. Megala?"
[1424] "Everything," Megala said with a look of painful resignation as he looked at the pipe. "You thought it was Tom's pipe? Oh, it's not!"
[1425] "You're not telling me," the officer exclaimed, "that this pipe belongs to Crocker!"
[1426] "I wish it did," Megalas said angrily, "No. It's mine."
[1427] They all chewed on this unexpected fact for a while, mulling it over in their minds as if trying to extract some meaning from it. Vorn clearly felt confused. "After all," he said, "even if it is -"
[1428] "Wait a minute, Vorn," the district attorney said quickly, "it's not as simple as it seems. Mr. Megalas, in our minds, this pipe belongs to Brad. Stollings convinced us it was. Although now that I think about it, it's easy to make such a mistake, but it has Brad's fingerprints, and on the night of the murder, he used his own brand of tobacco in it. Now you say it's yours. What I don't understand is -"
[1429] Megalas narrowed his eyes, his tone stubborn, "There's something wrong here, Mr. Escham. It's my pipe. If Stollings said it was Tom's, then he was either lying or he thought it was Tom's because he noticed it was in the room when I left last year. I carelessly left it there about a year ago."
[1430] "What's puzzling," Ellery whispered to Escham, "is why someone would smoke another person's pipe."
[1431] "Exactly."
[1432] "What a joke!" Megalos exclaimed angrily. "Tom wouldn't smoke my pipe, and nobody else would. He has many pipes of his own, and if you open the drawers in his study, you'll see them. No one would put another person's pipe stem in their mouth. Especially Tom, he has an aversion to dirt. " He turned the Neptune pipe in his fingers, "I lost the old Neptune. I've had it for fifteen years. Tom—he knew how proud I was of it."
[1433] No one laughed. Ellery quickly said, "We have an interesting situation here, gentlemen. The first ray of light. Can't you see the significance of the pipe belonging to Mr. Megalos?"
[1434] "There's no damn significance," Vance snorted. "It only means one thing—Crosetti tried to frame Mr. Megalos."
[1435] "Nonsense, officer," Ellery said genially. "It doesn't mean anything like that. Crosetti couldn't expect us to believe that Megalos killed Breda. Everyone knows that Megalos was out of town somewhere, thousands of miles away, on a sea voyage. Secondly—the T's, and the connection to Van's murder—it's a signature. Not to frame, but—" He turned to the yacht owner, who was still frowning over the pipe. "Where were you on June 22nd, sir—your yacht, yourself, your crew?"
[1436] Megalla turned to his captain. "Didn't we expect this, captain?" He grinned, his beard bristling, "Where were we?"
[1437] Captain Swift blushed, and he pulled out a piece of paper from one of his bulging blue pockets. "Here's a summary from my logbook," he said, "it should answer your question, sir."
[1438] They checked the summary: On June 22nd, the Helen was passing through Lake Gatun in the Panama Canal, heading to the West Indies. Attached to the summary was a formal receipt confirming the payment of the transit fee to the Canal Authority.
[1439] "All crew members were on board," Captain Swift said gruffly, "My logbook is open for inspection. We sailed eastward in the Pacific, as far west as Australia."
[1440] Vance nodded. "No one doubts you. But we still want to see your logbook."
[1441] Megalus stretched his legs, swaying back and forth; it was easy to imagine him sitting in the cockpit of a ship, rocking with the waves of the sea. "No one suspects us. Indeed! I'm not too fond of what you've done, you know... the closest we came to death during our voyage was when I had a pain in my groin as we left Suva."
[1442] Esham looked uneasy, and the policeman turned to Ellery, "Oh, Mr. Quin, what's on your mind? I can tell you have an idea."
[1443] "I'm afraid, officer," Ellery said, pointing to the summary and the note, "we can't really believe that Croce wanted us to think Megalus was the murderer of his partner." He took a puff of his cigarette, continuing, "This pipe... " He lightly tapped the ash of his cigarette into Megalus's strange heather pipe. "Croce must have known that Mr. Megalus had an impeccable alibi for the entire period of the murder. Therefore, our suspicions would be greatly diminished. But, from the fact that this is Mr. Megalus's pipe, and that Brad wouldn't smoke it, we can now establish a logical hypothesis."
[1444] "Splendid," Professor Yardley said, "if that's the case. How do we establish it?"
[1445] "Brad wouldn't smoke this pipe of Neptune's head, it was his partner's possession. However, it has been smoked - obviously, the victim touched it. But if Brad didn't smoke this pipe, and there's evidence that he did, what can we gain?"
[1446] "Clever," muttered the professor. "The pipe was made to look like Brad had smoked it. It's very easy to get a dead man's fingerprints on the pipe stem."
[1447] "Exactly!" cried Ellery. "It's very easy to make the pipe look like it's been smoked. Perhaps the murderer himself loaded and lit it, smoked a few puffs. Unfortunately, the Betatron personality test didn't take into account the different bacteria of each person; there's an ideal... oh, who would want to make it look like Brad had smoked this pipe? Only the murderer. Why? To strengthen the impression that Brad was wandering around outside - wearing a smoking suit, smoking a cigarette and walking towards the pavilion, where he was attacked and killed."
[1448] "Sounds about right," admitted Esham. "But why did Crocetta use Mei Ga's pipe for this? Why didn't he use one of Brad's own pipes?"
[1449] Ellery shrugged. "There's a simple answer if you think about it. Where did Crocetta get this pipe? From the drawer of the desk in the study. Is that right, Mei Ga?"
[1450] "Probably," said Mei Ga. "Tom kept all his pipes there. He found my pipe and must have put it in the same drawer for me to come back."
[1451] "Thank you. Oh, Crosetti walked over to the drawer and saw many pipes. He naturally assumed they all belonged to Brad. He wanted to leave one behind to make it look like Brad was smoking in the gazebo. So, according to that excellent theory: the most distinctive pipe is the easiest to identify, he chose the god of the sea. However, fortunately for us, the god of the sea belonged to Megala, not Brad."
[1452] "Ah," Ellery continued in a sharp voice, "but here we encounter an interesting inference. Did Crosetti encounter considerable difficulty in making it look like Brad was smoking in the gazebo and then being attacked and killed? Because, you see, if there had been no pipe, no evidence of smoking, we would have suspected Brad's presence in the gazebo, especially since he was wearing a smoking jacket; he might have been forced there. But when we know that someone was smoking in a certain place, it implies, at least, that he was there willingly... However, we now find that he wasn't smoking there, and we know that the killer wanted us to believe he was. The only logical inference is that the gazebo was not the crime scene, and the killer wanted us to believe it was."
[1453] Megala's gaze fixed on Ellery with a thoughtful, mocking look. The others remained silent.
[1454] Ellery threw his cigarette out of the doorway. "The next step is clear. Since this is not the crime scene, then somewhere else must be. We must find that place and examine it. I believe finding it will not be difficult – naturally, it's the study. Brad was last seen alive there, playing chess with himself. He was waiting for someone, as he had sent away all possible witnesses and intruders."
[1455] "Wait a minute," Megala's tone was firm, "you speak eloquently, but you're all wrong."
[1456] Ellery's smile faded. "Hmm? I don't understand. Where did this analysis go wrong?"
[1457] "It's wrong in assuming Crosetti didn't know the pipe was mine."
[1458] Ellery removed his spectacles and wiped them with a handkerchief – a reliable sign of his inner turmoil, satisfaction, or excitement. "An unusual statement, if true, Mr. Meagal. How did Crosetti know the pipe was yours?"
[1459] "Because the pipe was in a box. Did you find a box in the drawer?"
[1460] "No." Ellery's eyes gleamed. "Don't tell me, your initials were on the box!"
[1461] "Even clearer than that," Megalas growled, "my full name is engraved in gold leaf on the morocco surface. The last time I saw the pipe, it was in the box. The box, of course, had a peculiar shape, impossible to hold another pipe, except for a replica."
[1462] "Ah, excellent!" Ellery exclaimed, beaming. "I take back all my words. You've revived us, Mr. Megalas. It changes the whole picture, gives us more to do... So, Crosack knew it was your pipe. Yet, he deliberately left it at the pavilion. Since the box is missing, it's clear he took it. Why take the box? Because if he left it, we would find it, see the peculiar shape of the box of Stephen Megalas and the shape of the pipe that was supposed to be of Brad's, and immediately realize that the pipe was not Brad's. By taking the box, Crosack made us temporarily believe that the pipe was Brad's. Do you agree with this reasoning?"
[1463] "Why is it temporary?" Vane asked.
[1464] "Because," Ellery said smugly, "Mr. Megalas will return, and then recognize the pipe and tell us about the lost box! Crosack must have known that Megalas would do that. Conclusion – Crosack wants us to believe that the pipe belongs to Brad before Megalas returns, so that the pavilion becomes the crime scene. Megalas returns, and Crosack wants us to know that the pavilion is not the crime scene; and furthermore, wants us to search for the real crime scene, since it's inevitable. Why do I say 'wants'? Because Crosack could have avoided all this by choosing another method to make the pavilion look like a crime scene; actually, by choosing one of Brad's own pipes!"
[1465] "So, you think," the professor said slowly, "the killer deliberately wants us to return to the actual crime scene. I don't understand why."
[1466] "It seems too strange," Isam said, shaking his head.
[1467] "It's crystal clear," Ellery grinned. "Don't you see—Croscutt wants us to check the crime scene now, not a week ago, you notice, now! Why? Because Megala will return and recognize the pipe and tell us about the missing box! Croscutt must have known Megala would do that. Conclusion—Croscutt wants us to believe that the pipe belongs to Brad before Megala returns, so that the gazebo is the crime scene. Megala returns, and Croscutt wants us to know that the gazebo is not the crime scene; and, furthermore, wants us to search for the real crime scene, since that is inevitable. Why do I say 'wants us to know'? Because Croscutt could have avoided all this by choosing another method to make the gazebo look like a crime scene; actually, by choosing one of Brad's own pipes!"
[1468] "But why, old chap?" Megala asked impatiently. "It doesn't make sense."
[1469] Ellery shrugged. "I can't explain it to you exactly, but I'm sure it's of great importance, Mr. Megala. Croscutt needs us to discover something now—while you're at Bradwood—that he didn't want us to discover when you were in the Pacific."
[1470] "Rubbish," Inspector Wain said sternly.
[1471] "Whatever it is," said Escham, "I'm prepared to suspect it."
[1472] "I propose," said Ellery, "that we follow Crosack's plan. If he wants us to find it, let's oblige him. Shall we go to the library?"

[1476][3] A famous English public school founded in 1440.
