[1479] Chapter Fourteen: The Ivory Keyboard
[1480] The study had been closed since the morning after the discovery of the mutilated body of Breda. Escham, Vane, Megala, Professor Yadle, and Ellery entered the room; Captain Swift stumbled back to the wharf, Breda's mother and daughter, and Lincoln were in their own residence. Dr. Temple had disappeared.
[1481] Meagher watched as the search was conducted. This was not a cursory inspection, but a thorough search that left no stone unturned. Eames turned the desk into a slaughterhouse, piling crumpled papers on top of it. Vaughan meticulously examined each piece of furniture. Dr. Yadley, in his private capacity, retreated to the recess where the piano was placed and tinkered with it.
[1482] Almost immediately, a discovery was made—or at least one was made, whether it was part of Vilgai Croasdale's plan or not, it didn't matter. It was Ellery who made this crucial discovery. He was wandering around the officer when, by chance or thoroughness, he grabbed a corner of the sofa and pulled it away from the wall, placing it entirely on the Chinese carpet. Before, its back legs were resting on the bare floor. He pulled with force and called out, quickly bending down to check what was hidden under the part of the sofa that had been previously covered by the carpet. Eames, Vaughan, and Dr. Yadley rushed to his side, while Meagher stretched his neck but remained stationary.
[1483] "What is it?"
[1484] "Good heavens," the officer muttered, "there's a stain in an otherwise pristine place!"
[1485] "A bloodstain," Ellery whispered, "unless someone with my dear professor's inexperience wouldn't have noticed it."
[1486] This was a dried black bloodstain, standing out vividly next to a wax print on the golden carpet. A few inches away from the bloodstain, the fibers of the carpet had a square indentation, left by a chair or table leg that had been placed there for a long time. The indentation's shape was not that of the sofa's foot, which had a rounded bottom.
[1487] Ellery knelt down and looked around. His gaze wavered for a moment before he looked at the desk in front of the wall.
[1488] "There should be –" he said, pushing the sofa to the center of the room. He immediately nodded as he saw that there was a matching indentation three feet away from the first one on the carpet.
[1489] "But the bloodstain," Eisam frowned, "how did it get under the sofa? When I asked Stolins, he told me nothing had moved in the room."
[1490] "Isn't it obvious?" Ellery said coldly, standing up, "Nothing moved – except the carpet itself, you can hardly expect Stolins to notice that."
[1491] He looks around the room, his eyes shining. His idea about the desk is correct, and the only furniture legs in the room that could leave an indentation the same size and shape as the ones under the sofa are the legs of the desk. He walks around the room, lifting one of the desk's square-ended legs. Underneath it, clearly imprinted on the carpet, is a mark the same shape and size as the two on the other side of the room, only not as deep or clear.
[1492] "We could do a little experiment," Ellery says, straightening up. "Move the carpet so it's in the same position as it was before Crosetti changed it on Tuesday night."
[1493] "Move it?" Escham asks. "Why?"
[1494] "So we can see if the bloodstain appears under the sofa like it did then."
[1495] Vane's face lights up. "Good heavens," he exclaims. "I see now. He didn't want us to find the bloodstain, and he couldn't get rid of it!"
[1496] "That's only half of it, officer," Dr. Yadley said. "If I understand Quinn's meaning."
[1497] "You've understood it," Ellery said calmly. "It's just a matter of moving the desk out of the way. The rest is easy."
[1498] Esham looked. "Behind the chess chair!"
[1499] "Ah. The scene is beginning to take shape," Ellery said. The bloodstains were two feet behind the chess table's folding chair, which stood next to the desk.
[1500] "He was attacked from behind," Dr. Yadley said. "While he was trying to move one of the pieces. He probably knew that this obsession would one day get him into trouble."
[1501] "What do you think, Mr. Meagher?" Ellery suddenly asked, turning to the yacht owner.
[1502] Meagher shrugged. "That's your job, gentlemen."
[1503] "I think," said Ellery, settling into a low-backed armchair and lighting a cigarette, "let's quickly analyze this to save time. Any objections, officer?"
[1504] "I still don't see," complained Vance. "Why did he turn the carpet? Who was he trying to fool? As you pointed out, if he hadn't left the pipe as a clue to return to this room, we wouldn't have found it at all."
[1505] "Slow down, officer. Let me think... Now it's clear – there's no disagreement on this point – Crosetto never intended to permanently hide the fact that this room was a crime scene. He not only didn't want to permanently hide that fact, but he arranged to bring us back to this room at a time when a more thorough check would reveal the bloodstain. To permanently hide that fact, he wouldn't have left the pipe as a clue to return to the room, nor would he have left this bloodstain as it is. Because you see," Ellery pointed to the open desk drawer in the study, "right here, almost directly above the bloodstain, are two bottles of ink. If Crosetto had left the carpet in its original position and had intentionally knocked over one of the ink bottles, the police would have found the bottle and the stain and would have assumed that the surface fact – that the ink was spilled by Brad or someone else – and would never have thought to look for blood underneath... Crosetto didn't take the simple step of turning the carpet and making things so complicated, to make us miss it the first time we checked, and then to confirm with Mr. Meagher that the pipe was his, bringing us back here, and thus discovering it in the second check. The point is, in all these complicated strategies, Crosetto gained nothing but – time."
[1506] "Well," the professor said, annoyed, "if I can figure out why he wanted us to find it, let me be dismembered."
[1507] "Dear professor," Erlie said, "don't jump to conclusions. This is something I've memorized. Your ancient history is top-notch, but my specialty is logic, and within my area of expertise, I don't lose to anyone. Ha, ha! Let's move on."
[1508] He suppressed a smile. "Croscot didn't want to permanently hide the crime scene, but to delay its discovery. Why? Three possible reasons. Pay attention – especially Mr. Meagal; you may be able to help us in this regard."
[1509] Meagal nodded and sat down on the sofa, which had been put back in its original position in front of the wall.
[1510] "First, there's something in this room that's dangerous to Croscot, that he needs to take away later, but for some reason couldn't do it on the night of the murder... Second, there's something that Croscot wants to add or bring back to this room, but he couldn't do it on the night of the murder –"
[1511] "Hold on," the district attorney said, frowning. "Both assumptions sound reasonable, as in either case, making the gazebo look like a crime scene would divert attention from the study, where the killer might have entered during that period."
[1512] "It's a contradiction, Mr. Escham, wrong," Ellery said, drawing out the syllables. "Crossett would have anticipated that, even if the bloodstains were missed in the first search—as he planned—the gazebo would be considered the crime scene… He would have anticipated, I repeat, that the house would be guarded, and he would be hindered by the police's preventive measures from taking or bringing anything back later. But there's an even more important obstacle to achieving the first two possibilities, gentlemen.
[1513] "If Crossett wanted to come back here and make the gazebo look like a crime scene—of course, to his advantage—it would give him unlimited time and opportunity to sneak into the study. But he didn't—he left a clue to come back to this room, which, if my earlier inference is correct, would be the last thing he would want to do. So I say that neither of these hypotheses holds up."
[1514] "I don't get it," Wan said with disgust. "It's too much for me."
[1515] "Then shut up and listen," Escham said angrily. "This isn't a method that only a cop who knows how to bark can come up with, Wan. I admit it's an unconventional method of solving a case, but it sounds genuine. Go on, Mr. Quine. We're all ears."
[1516] "Be careful not to incriminate yourself, officer," Ellery said seriously, "The third possibility: there is something in the study now that was there on the night of the murder, it is not dangerous to the criminal, and he does not intend to take it away, but he wants the police to find it, but not before Megala returns."
[1517] "Well," Vance said, raising his hands, "Let me out of here."
[1518] "Ignore him, Quine," Escham said.
[1519] Megala calmly glanced at Ellery. "Go on, Mr. Quine."
[1520] "Since we are helpful people," Ellery continued, "we must find and discover what Croasdale planned for us to only find when you, Mr. Megala, are present... You know, I always find - I think you'll agree with me, officer - that a murderer becomes more prone to make mistakes as he becomes more involved. Let's get Stirling to come here for a moment."
[1521] The detective at the door called out, "Stollings!" The butler appeared in a state of haste that was not lacking in dignity.
[1522] "Stollings," Ellery said suddenly, "you're familiar with this room, aren't you?"
[1523] Stollings coughed. "I can say, sir, that I'm as familiar with it as Mr. Brad was."
[1524] "I'm glad to hear it," Ellery said. "Take a look around." Stollings looked around respectfully. "Is everything normal? Is there anything new here? Is there anything that shouldn't be here but is?"
[1525] Stollings smiled briefly and began to walk around the room with an air of assurance. He glanced into corners, opened drawers, and examined the inside of the desk... It took him ten minutes, but when he finished his inspection, he said, "This room is exactly the same as I saw it last, sir—I mean before Mr. Brad was killed... except, sir, that the table is gone." Everyone felt that there was nothing more to ask.
[1526] But Ellery persisted, "Nothing else has been disturbed or taken?"
[1527] The butler shook his head vigorously. "No, sir. The only real difference is that stain, sir," he said, pointing to the carpet, "it wasn't there when I left the room on Tuesday evening. The chess table..."
[1528] "What about the chess table?" Ellery asked eagerly.
[1529] Stollings shrugged elegantly. "The chess pieces. Of course, they would have been moved. Mr. Breda would have continued playing after I left."
[1530] "Oh," Ellery said reassuringly, "that's good, Stollings. You have a keen eye, a camera's eye... Well, you can leave now."
[1531] Stollings gave Vance a disapproving glance and left the room. Vance was now looking glumly at the wall, puffing on a West Indian cigar.
[1532] "Well," Ellery said cheerfully, "let's split up and search."
[1533] "But what are we looking for?" Vance grumbled.
[1534] "Well, I wish I knew! That's why we're looking for it!"
[1535] The scene that followed was ridiculous to any observer except Stephen Meagal, who looked as if he had lost his sense of humor. Four adult males were crawling around a room, trying to reach the top of the walls to tap on plastic and wood, carefully inspecting the stuffing in the sofa cushions, trying to unscrew the legs and arms of chairs, sofas, desks, and chess tables… The sight was like a scene from Alice's Adventures in Wonderland [4]. After fifteen minutes of fruitless searching, Ellery was covered in dust, hot, and frustrated, and sat down next to Meagal, lost in thought. His expression was more ominous than a daydream. As for the professor, nothing could discourage him, and he continued to work hard, finding great pleasure in this crawling movement, his tall figure bent over the carpet. Once, he straightened up and examined the old chandelier with care.
[1536] "That would be an unusual place to hide something," he muttered, standing on a chair and causing the crystal decorations of the chandelier to tinkle. Something was wrong with one of the wires or exposed, as he suddenly shouted and fell to the ground. Wain grumbled, bringing another sheet of paper close to the light; evidently, the detective was thinking that it might be a message written with invisible ink. Esame shook the curtains; he removed the blinds; he checked the interior of the table lamps. All of this was both ridiculous and unrealistic, and useless.
[1537] They had all glanced at the rows of books on the built-in shelves, but none of them had taken any action to check them. The task of going through all those volumes was daunting, and none of them had the courage to even start.
[1538] Ellery suddenly sat back and said in a long drawl, "We are a bunch of big fools! Chasing our own tails... Crosack wants us to come back to this room to find something, so he'll make sure we find it. He wouldn't put it in a place that would require a combination of Houdini and a bloodhound to find. Instead, he would hide it in a place that's not too obvious to find on the surface, but not so remote that it couldn't be found even in a thorough search. As for you, Professor, when you try to examine the chandelier again, remember that Crosack may not be familiar with this room to the point where he knows where there are holes in the furniture legs or in the lamp... no, it's in a clever but accessible hiding place."
[1539] Wain sneered, "But where?" He was tired and soaked with sweat. "Do you know of any hiding places, Meigal?"
[1540] When Meigal shook his head, Professor Yadley's beard bristled like the false whiskers of an Egyptian pharaoh.
[1541] Ellery said, "This reminds me of a search my father, District Attorney Crowning, and I recently conducted that was extremely similar. We were investigating the murder of the cunning lawyer Monty Field, who was poisoned. Do you remember? During the performance of 'Firearms' at the Roman Theater. We found it in—"
[1542] Professor Adler's eyes lit up, and he rushed across the room to the recess where the piano was. Ellery had checked it out a few minutes earlier. But Adler didn't bother to check the piano, the piano chair, or the music cabinet. He simply sat down on the chair, with the same solemnity that Ellery remembered from his university lectures, and began to play the keys from the first bass note to the highest note, one by one, slowly pressing each key.
[1543] "Excellent analysis, Quine," he said as he pressed each note in turn, "gave me a positive boost... Suppose I were Crowsax, and I wanted to hide something—small, say, flat. I only have limited time, and limited knowledge of the house. What would I do? Where would I—"
[1544] "Wise man's active victory, Professor!" Ellery exclaimed. "I couldn't have done better myself!"
[1545] Just as a concert always begins after the program introduction, Professor Adler made a discovery. The ripple of the scale was interrupted; he touched a key that would not go down. He pressed it several times, but it was clear that the key was out of tune, and he continued his in-depth exploration. "Crowsax needs a place that won't be discovered before he's ready—even accidentally. He searches—here is the piano. Now, pay attention to this: Brad is dead; this is Brad's room. Naturally, he concludes that no one would play the piano in a dead man's private room—for a long time. So…"
[1546] "Eureka!" Yadley said, his face full of disbelief. He looked like a man who had just discovered that his first attempt at a magic trick had succeeded.
[1547] Everyone gathered around him, Megalos just as eager as the others. The key, no matter how hard the professor tried, was stuck four inches. It was completely stuck, not even popping up.
[1548] Ereli exclaimed, "Wait a minute." He took out the small set of tools he always carried with him, ignoring his father's ridicule, and selected a long needle from the tools. He began to probe the immovable key and the adjacent key on the other side. After a while, a tiny edge of a piece of paper emerged between the two ivory boards.
[1549] Everyone straightened up, sighing. Ereli carefully pulled out the small piece of paper. They stood in silence, returning to the study. The paper was flattened and pressed, and Ereli carefully unfolded it and laid it on the table.
[1550] Megalos' face was mysterious. As for the others, including Ereli, no one could foresee the unusual information conveyed by the hastily written words on the paper.
[1551] To the police:
[1552] If I am murdered - I have good reason to believe that someone is trying to take my life - please immediately investigate the murder of Andrew Van, the headmaster of Arroyo College, who was found nailed to a cross and decapitated last Christmas.
[1553] At the same time, notify Stephen Megalos, wherever he may be, to return to Bradwood as soon as possible.
[1554] Tell him not to believe that Andrew Van is dead. Only Stephen Megalos knows where to find him.
[1555] If you value innocent lives, please keep this absolutely confidential. Do not take any action until Megalos suggests what to do. Van and Megalos need to be closely protected.
[1556] This is so important that I must repeat the advice to let Meagles lead the way. You are dealing with an unscrupulous fanatic.
[1557] Signed: Thomas Brad. The handwriting matches that of other samples taken from the study.

[1559] [1] The original term "draw and quarter" refers to the medieval European punishment of "four horses and parts". Here, the professor states that he cannot fathom the motive for the killer's actions.
[1560] [2] Referring to the famous detective Sherlock Holmes.
[1561][3] An American idiom meaning the ability to remember faces.
[1562][4] A work by Lewis Carroll, a nineteenth-century British author of fairy tales.
[1563][5] Houdini (1874-1926), an American magician born in Hungary, was known for his ability to escape from handcuffs, bindings, and various locked containers.
[1564][6] Refers to the content of the book "The Roman Hat Mystery."
[1565][7] Italian for "I have found it," a phrase attributed to Archimedes when he discovered the principle of buoyancy to test the purity of gold; now used as an exclamation of amazement due to a major discovery.
[1566] Chapter 15: Lazarus
[1567] Stephen Megalla's face was a classic example of agitation. The transformation of this lively, calm man was shocking. The pressure of the unknown finally tore away the mask of willpower from his face, and his eyes glinted coldly and nervously. He quickly looked around the room - at the windows, as if he sensed that Vilga Crozac was approaching him in his imagination; at the door where the detective was leaning indifferently. He took a short gun from his back pocket, quickly checked its mechanism, and then gathered his spirits, striding to the door and closing it in the policeman's face. He walked to the window, glaring outside, and stood there quietly for a moment, chuckling softly, and then put the gun in his coat pocket.
[1568] Isam roared, "Mr. Megalla!"
[1569] The yacht owner turned quickly, with a determined expression. "Tom is a weak man," he said briefly. "He couldn't kill me like that."
[1570] "Where is Van? How is he still alive? What does this mean? Why...?"
[1571] "Hold on a minute," Ellery drawled. "Don't get excited, Mr. Eisam. We have enough to chew on without adding another… It's obvious that Brad placed this note in a place where it could be seen immediately - on the desk or in one of the drawers of this round table, if he wanted it to be found after his death. But he didn't anticipate Crocecki's thoroughness. With each investigation, I've grown more and more impressed with Crocecki.
[1572] "When he murdered Brad, Crocecki didn't overlook a post-mortem search of the room. He may have sensed that there would be such a note or warning. Anyway, he found the note, and because he saw that it posed no danger to him personally—"
[1573] "How do you figure that?" Vane asked. "To me, any killer would want to avoid that—letting his victim's note be found!"
[1574] Ellery said coldly, "This amazing man makes a seemingly stupid move, and we don't need complex reasoning, officer. If Crocecki thought the note posed a danger to his safety, he would have destroyed it. Or at least, taken it with him. But instead, he—despite the surface reasons you've pointed out—left it at the crime scene, fulfilling the last wish of the victim."
[1575] "Why?" Eisam asked.
[1576] "Why?" Ellery's nostrils flared. "Because he believed that the police finding the note would not pose a danger to his safety, but, in fact, would be advantageous to him! Oh, but here we find the crux of the matter. What did the note say?"
[1577] Megala's shoulders jerked, his face twisted in a grimace. "The note said that Andrew Van was still alive and that only Stephen Megala knew where to find him!"
[1578] Professor Yardeley's eyes widened. "Extremely clever. He didn't know where Van was!"
[1579] "That's right. Now it's clear that somehow he killed the wrong man in Arroyo. He thought he killed Andrew Van; Thomas Brad was next on his list, and when he found and killed Brad, he discovered the note. The note told him that Van was still alive. But if he had a motive and desire to kill Van six months ago, he probably still does. If Van is alive - putting aside, for the moment, the poor unfortunate man Crosett killed by mistake - he must find and eliminate Van. But where is Van? It's clear, as soon as Crosett realized he was being pursued and had killed the wrong man, he disappeared."
[1580] Ellery waved his index finger. "Now consider the problem our clever Crosett faces. The note didn't say where Van was. It said only that one person, Megala, knew where Van was..."
[1581] "On the surface, that's a very good question. But actually, it's unnecessary." Eleanor tapped a cigarette with a trembling hand. "You didn't think, if there was no note, Megalas would return without any reason to suspect Van's death! Will you suspect it, Mr. Megalas?"
[1582] "I would suspect it. But Croscok couldn't know that."
[1583] Eleanor was startled. "I don't understand... Croscok couldn't know that? That proves my point. By leaving the note here for the police to find - I mean, as soon as the police found the body, they would know the study was the crime scene - the police would immediately begin searching for Van. Of course, a simultaneous police search would hinder his own investigation. But by delaying the discovery of the note, Croscok achieved two things: first, during the time between Brad's murder and Megalas's return, he could search for Van without being hindered by the police; the police hadn't found the note yet, so they didn't know Van was still alive. Second, if Croscok couldn't find Van during that time, he wouldn't lose anything; because when Megalas arrived at the scene, he would confirm that the pipe was his, which would open up a new investigation - just as it is now being conducted - ultimately leading to the discovery that the study was the actual crime scene, which would be thoroughly searched, and the note would be found, and Megalas would learn that Van is still alive, and would reveal Van's whereabouts...and Croscok would only have to follow us to find Van's exact hiding place!"
[1584] Megalas said angrily, "Perhaps it's all over!"
[1585] Eleanor turned around. "You mean, you think that Croscok has already found Van during that time?"
[1586] Meagal spread his hands and shrugged—a European gesture, totally out of place on the rugged American man. "It's possible. For that demon, anything is possible."
[1587] "Listen," the detective said sternly, "we could have gotten real information, but we're wasting valuable time talking nonsense. Wait a minute, Mr. Quinn; this isn't a coffee party, your speech is long enough... Spill it out, Mr. Meagal. What was the relationship between Brand, your partner Breed, and you?"
[1588] The yacht owner hesitated. "We were—we were—"
[1589] "What?" the district attorney called.
[1590] "Brothers."
[1591] "Brothers!"
[1592] Ellery's eyes fastened on the lips of the tall man. Eames exclaimed excitedly, "So you were right, Mr. Quinn! They weren't their real names. It couldn't be Brad, Meigalo, and Van. What —"
[1593] Meigalo suddenly sat down. "No, they weren't. When I tell you —" His eyes grew dim and he looked far away beyond the study.
[1594] "What?" the policeman asked slowly.
[1595] "When I tell you everything, you'll understand this whole thing that's been a giant puzzle to you until now. When you told me about the T — that crazy business about the T — the headless body and the arranged limbs, the blood-written T on the door and the pavilion, the crossroads, the totem —"
[1596] "You're not telling me," Erling said in a harsh voice, "that your real name starts with T!"
[1597] Megal nodded his head as if it weighed a ton. "Yes," he murmured, "our last name is Tvervel. T-v-a-r... you see, it starts with T."
[1598] They fell silent for a moment. Then the professor said: "Quinn, as usual, you're right. It's just as literal, nothing profound. Just a T - nothing to do with the cross, nothing to do with Egyptology, nothing to do with religious implications... strange. It's incredible."
[1599] A shadow of disappointment fell over Erling's face, staring intently at Megal.
[1600] "I don't believe it," Vain said with disgust. "I've never heard of such a thing."
[1601] "Dividing a man into his initials!" Escham muttered. "Oh, we'll be laughingstocks of the Orient, Wain, if this gets out."
[1602] Megalos jumped up, his whole body stretching out in rage. "You don't understand Central Europe!" he roared. "You fools, he's using those T—the symbols of our hateful names—to humiliate us! The man's mad, I tell you! It's so clear...," he said, sitting back down. "It's unbelievable," he muttered. "Yes, but not something that worries you. It's unbelievable, he's been chasing us for years. Like a movie. But he dissected the body—"
[1603] "Tver," Ellery said quietly. "Using three aliases for years. Obviously for some important reason. And Central Europe... I think it's revenge, Mr. Megalos."
[1604] Megalos nodded; his voice was weary. "Yes, that's it. But how did he find us? I don't understand. When Andrew, Tomislav, and I decided—God, how many years ago—to assume false identities, we also agreed that no one—no one, you understand—would know our original surnames. It was to be a secret, and it was kept, I swear. Not even Tom's wife Margaret and her daughter Helen knew our original surname was Tver."
[1605] "You mean," Ellery asked, "that Crocetta was the only one who knew?"
[1606] "Yes, that's why I can't imagine how he tracked us down. The names we chose...”
[1607] "Go on," Vorn roared, "Start, I need information. First, who is this Crocax? Why is he against you? Second -"
[1608] "Don't rush, Vorn," Esam said impatiently, "I want to think about the T thing. I can't quite figure it out yet. Why did he choose their initials?”
[1609] "To signify," Meigal grumbled, "that the Tverly family is doomed. Very stupid, isn't it?" His laughter was piercing.
[1610] "Can you recognize Crocax if you see him?" Elyre pondered aloud.
[1611] The yacht owner tightened his lips. "That's the problem. We three haven't seen Crocetta in twenty years. He was young then, and it's impossible to recognize him now. He could be anyone. We encountered a man – a nearly invisible man."
[1612] "He's got a limp on his left leg, doesn't he?"
[1613] "He had a slight limp when he was young."
[1614] "It may not be permanent," Professor Yadley muttered, "It could be a feigned defect to throw people off his trail. It's in line with Crocetta's cunning."
[1615] Vane suddenly stepped forward, his lips curling back from his teeth. "You may want to spend all day here chattering, but I want to know what's going on! Listen, Meagal – or Tewell – or whatever your name is – why doesn't Crocetta follow the rules? Why did he kill you? What's going on?"
[1616] "We can wait on that," Ellery said curtly. "There's something more important right now. Mr. Tewel, the note your brother left said you knew where to find Van. How could you know? You've been out of touch with the world for over a year, and the murder of Arroyo happened six months ago – last Christmas."
[1617] "It was all arranged, everything was arranged," Megalas murmured. "For a long time, years... I mean before that, even without the note, I would have known that Andrew Tewel was still alive. The reason is what you mentioned when you talked about the Arroyo incident."
[1618] Ellery narrowed his eyes. "Hmm?"
[1619] Megalas scanned the room again, as if to make sure the vanished Crosetti couldn't hear him. "I knew then. Because if old Pete – the mountain man you mentioned – was alive, then my brother, Andrew Tewel, was alive."
[1620] "I'm afraid it's me –" the district attorney said blankly.
[1621] "Oh, come on!" Ellery exclaimed, turning to Professor Yadley, "Can't you see it? Andrew Van is old Pete!"
[1622] Before the others could recover from their surprise, Megala nodded and continued, "That's right. He started impersonating a mountaineer every now and then to avoid such unforeseen events. He's probably hiding out in the mountains of West Virginia—if Croscos hasn't found him by now—hoping Croscos didn't notice his mistake. Croscos hasn't seen us three in twenty years, remember. I'm sure he hasn't."
[1623] "That's why he made the mistake," Ellery said, "He hadn't seen his victim in so long."
[1624] "You mean he killed Clinton?" Esham asked.
[1625] "Who else?" Ellery smiled. "You want to take action, Officer? We're going to have to do something. Because there's one thing that's certain: we must act before Croscos does, fool him. I think Croscos hasn't discovered Andrew yet. Old Pete's disguise was flawless; I sat in Weldon's courtroom, and I never suspected anything out of the ordinary. We must go to your brother's place, Mr. Megala, but secretly, so that Croscos—whatever he is, whatever he may pretend to be—remains unaware of the mountaineer's disguise."
[1626] "Right on the money," Wain said, grinning with irritation.
[1627] Megala stood up, his eyes narrowing to slits. "I'll do everything you ask, gentlemen – for Andrew's sake. As for me," he tapped his gun-pocket nervously, "if that devil causes trouble, he'll find my clip is full."

[1630] Chapter 16: The Messenger.
[1631] Mrs. Brad and her daughter could not persuade Stephen Megalus to stay overnight that night. For the rest of the day, he maintained his air of superiority and spent a calm evening with the Brad family and Lincoln; but as night fell, he became restless and set out for the yacht anchored offshore. The sharp light of the yacht's anchor lamp pierced the darkness of Oyster Island. For Mrs. Brad, the return of her husband's "partner" was a comfort and relief. She followed the yacht owner along the path leading to the dock, pleading with him to stay.
[1632] "No," he said, "I'll sleep on the Helen tonight, Margaret. I've lived here so long, it's become my home… Thank you for your kind invitation, but Lincoln will stay with you," his tone was unpleasant, "and I'll be no safer here than I am there. Goodnight, Margaret, don't worry."
[1633] The two detectives accompanying them to the bay curiously watched the two of them. Mrs. Brad's tear-stained face lifted to the sky, and she turned to walk back. Surprisingly, this tragedy seemed to have no effect on her courage, and she almost indifferently walked past the motionless totem pole with the meditative eagle.
[1634] Everyone quickly agreed that the Tewell brothers' matter must be kept secret from anyone.
[1635] Stephen Megalus, under the watchful gaze of Captain Swift and the crew, slept under supervision that night. Detectives patrolled the deck. Megalus locked his cabin door, and the person on duty outside heard the gurgling sound of liquid and the steady clinking of cups for two hours. Later, the lights went out. Despite his confidence, Megalus seemed to like to drink to calm his nerves. But he slept quietly, and the detectives did not hear a sound all night.
[1636] The next day was Saturday, and early in the morning, two police cars raced up the driveway to the colonial house and waited there. Officer Vaughn, like Conqueror Caesar, got out of the car and strode towards the dock with his uniformed escort. The motor of a police boat roared on the dock. Officer Vaughn's face was flushed and stern as he jumped into the boat and was taken to the yacht.
[1637] The operation was open and without disguise. As the boat made its way, the small figures on Oyster Island could be distinguished in the foreground of the spreading green. Dr. Temple, with a pipe in his mouth, stood on his small dock watching. The Rein couple watched from their boat, pretending to paddle.
[1638] Officer Vaughn disappeared up the ladder of the Helen.
[1639] Five minutes later, he reappeared, accompanied by Stephen Megal. Megal was dressed in casual clothes, looking pale and smelling of alcohol. He said nothing to the captain and followed Officer Vaughn down the ladder, surprising in his steady step. They descended into the boat, which immediately headed for the shore.
[1640] At the Breedwood dock, they whispered for a moment; guards waited nearby. Then the uniformed men closed in, and the two were surrounded by police as they strode along the path towards the house. It was almost like a procession.
[1641] At the front of the house, a man in plain clothes watched them approach, jumping down from the back seat of the first police car and saluting, waiting. Soon, Vane and Meagher were seated in the first car, and the police filled the second. With the electric siren blaring, the two cars sped out of the driveway and onto the road leading past Bradwood.
[1642] At the gate, four state troopers on motorcycles immediately started their engines. Two motorcycles rode ahead of the first car, two on either side, and the police car followed… It was a surprising sight, but with the departure of the two cars, no state troopers, county police, or detectives remained on the ground or nearby in Bradwood.
[1643] The group sped along the road, forcing passing vehicles to the side, and making it clear they were heading to New York…
[1644] Back in Bradwood, the departure of the inspector and Meagher's group brought everything back to normal. The Reins rowed home. Dr. Temple wandered off, smoking and disappearing into the woods. The figures on Oyster Island disappeared. Kekem the old man rowed his dilapidated boat into the bay, heading towards the mainland. Jonathan Lincoln quietly drove a car out of the garage and onto the driveway.
[1645] Professor Yale's house was a fair distance from the road, and from the outside, it looked lifeless.
[1646] However, by looking at the two ends of the road that separated Broadwood and Yale's property, it was clear that Vaughn had not lost his mind... because at both ends of the road - two stations (each of which was the only way for any vehicle or pedestrian to leave Broadwood by land) - there were two large police cars loaded with detectives parked unobtrusively.
[1647] And across the channel, on the other side of Oyster Island, from the mainland, a large boat was seen drifting, with the engine turned off, and people sitting on the deck fishing, their eyes alertly watching the two corners of Kagim Bay, any boat that wanted to leave here by water had to pass through one of these corners or the other.

[1650] Chapter 17: The Mountain Elder
[1651] Saturday morning, there was no sign of activity in Professor Yardley's house, and there was a reason for it. Professor Yardley, like the officials, had received orders; so had his black old maid. It would have been unwise for him to make a public appearance when Inspector Vance and Stephen Magellan were making a fuss and leaving. It was known that Professor Yardley was entertaining a guest - Eliot Quine, a special investigator from New York. If Professor Yardley had appeared alone, it would have aroused suspicion in those who were on guard.
[1652] It was a clever plan. Late Friday night, in the darkness that concealed Bradwood, Ellery had quietly driven his Duesenberg car away from Yardley. It was only when he reached the main road that he began to drive like a demon. He then rushed to Minola, where he picked up District Attorney Isam, and they drove to New York.
[1653] Saturday morning at 4:00, the old Duesenberg arrived in Harrisburg, Pennsylvania. The city had already fallen asleep; both were exhausted and too tired to talk, so they checked into the Sennett Hotel and went to their rooms. Ellery instructed the front desk to call them at 9:00 a.m., and then they collapsed on the bed like corpses.
[1654] Saturday morning at 9:30, they were already several miles outside Harrisburg, heading towards Pittsburgh. They didn't stop for lunch. The car was covered in dust, and Ellery and Isam were both exhausted from this tedious journey... The Duesenberg had been performing excellently for years. On two occasions, when Ellery drove the old car at 70 miles per hour, he was pulled over by motorcycle police for questioning. After Isam showed his credentials, they were allowed to continue... At 3:00 p.m., they were crossing Pittsburgh.
[1655] Isam complained, "Let all this go to hell. Can't we stop for a moment? I don't know about you, but I'm hungry. Let's eat something."
[1656] Although District Attorney Escham had eaten, they had wasted valuable time. Ellery was inexplicably excited, fidgeting with his food; although he looked tired, his eyes were bright and shone with some unspoken thoughts.
[1657] At a few minutes to five, the Duesenberg stopped in front of a building where the prominent figures of Arroyo were. They got out of the car, their joints cracking. Escham stretched his arms wide, ignoring the curious eyes of a fat German old man - Bernheim, the respected owner of Arroyo's grocery store - and a country bumpkin dressed in blue denim, who seemed to be sweeping the sidewalk in front of the town hall all year round. Escham yawned and said, "Well, let's get it done quickly. Where is the county sheriff, Mr. Queen?"
[1658] Ellery led the way into the back of the house, where the sheriff's office was located. He knocked on the door, and a gravelly male voice said, "Come in, you son of a bitch!"
[1659] They went inside. Sheriff Rudden sat there, his massive body drenched in sweat, as if he hadn't moved since Ellery's last visit six months ago. As he opened his mouth, his fangs protruded from his fat, red face.
[1660]
[1661] "If it isn't Mr. Quin," Redden yelled, putting his big feet down with a bang, "I'm a son of a bitch! Come in, come in. Still chasing the guy who killed our principal?"
[1662] "Still on the case, Sheriff," Ellery smiled. "Meet the man who upholds the law. This is New York County District Attorney Escham. Sheriff Redden - Escham, sir."
[1663] Escham grumbled, not extending his hand. The sheriff grinned. "We had some bigwigs come to our town last year, sir, so don't be so arrogant."
[1664] Escham gasped in surprise. "You heard what I said... what do you care about, Mr. Quin?"
[1665] Ellery quickly said, "Can we sit down? We've been driving for a long time."
[1666] They sat down. Ellery said, "Sheriff, have you seen that crazy hillbilly, old Pete lately?”
[1667] "Old Pete? Oh, that's odd,” Rudden said, glancing slyly at Asheem, "I haven't seen him for several weeks. He doesn't come into town often, old Pete, I mean, not often; but this time - I haven't seen him for two months! Must have brought a lot of supplies down the mountain last time; you can ask Bernheim.”
[1668] "Do you know where his shack is?” Asheem asked.
[1669] "I think I know... what do you want with old Pete? Planning to arrest him? The old coot hasn't done anything wrong... no,” the sheriff hastily added as Asheem frowned, "it's none of my business... I've never been to old Pete's shack - hardly anyone has. It's in the cave area - the old folks, they're all so wise - people are a bit afraid. Old Pete's shack is in a very desolate place in the mountains, you won't find it.”
[1670] "Can you show us the way, Sheriff?” Ellery asked.
[1671] "Of course! I think I can find the place," said Redden, getting up, wagging his tail like an old, fat, well-trained dog. "You don't want the news to spread, do you?" he asked casually.
[1672] "No!" said Escham. "Don't tell your wife."
[1673] The sheriff snorted. "Don't worry about that. I don't have a wife, thank God... Amen."
[1674] He didn't take them to the street in front of the town hall where they had parked the car, but through a back door and onto a little-used street. Redden and Escham waited, and Ellery quickly circled the town hall and jumped into the Duesenberg. Two minutes later, the car was on the little street, and the three men drove away from the town hall, Redden stamping hard on the accelerator.
[1675] Sheriff Redden led them up a winding path that seemed to lead into the nearby mountains. "Different road," he said. "You park the car here, and we'll walk up the mountain."
[1676] "Walk?" Isam asked skeptically, eyeing the steep slope.
[1677] "Well," Redden said cheerfully, stretching out the syllables. "I could carry you, Mr. Isam."
[1678] They parked the car in a thicket. The district attorney looked around, then bent over the side of the Duesenberg and pulled out a large bundle from the undercarriage. Redden watched curiously, but neither man said anything.
[1679] The sheriff lowered his big head and laboriously pushed through the underbrush, searching for the target with an indifferent air. Finally, he pointed out a hidden path. Ellery and Isam followed silently, struggling to keep up. It was a real climb, requiring them to traverse a wilderness of dense jungle; the trees were so thick that they blocked out the sun. The air was hot, and the three men were soaked with sweat after only fifty feet.
[1680] Fifteen minutes of painful uphill climbing, the forest became denser, and the path narrower; the sheriff suddenly stopped.
[1681] "Matt Hollis once told me," he grumbled, pointing, "that there's a roof. That's it."
[1682] They approached closer, with Rueden leading the way. As the sheriff had said, it was there...in a small clearing in the woods, under a huge outcropping of rock on the hillside, squatting a crude shack. The trees on either side and in front of the shack had been cut back thirty feet, and its rear was protected by an outcropping of granite. Erlie stared, seeing a high protective fence of thirty feet on either side and in front, made of tangled barbed wire that looked dangerous. "Look at that!" Escham whispered. "Not even a door!"
[1683] There was no opening in the barbed wire fence. The crude shack stood there, cold and uninviting, like a fortress. Even the smoke coming from the chimney hole was menacing.
[1684] "Well," Rueden grumbled, "he's like he's locked himself up in a castle. Crazy, just like I told you."
[1685] "A hard-to-find place in the dark," Erlie whispered, "Sheriff, District Attorney, and I have a favor to ask of you."
[1686] "A hard place to find in the dark," Ellery murmured. "Constable, District Attorney, and I have a favor to ask of you."
[1687] Rudding Constable, anticipating another generous gesture from Ellery, immediately took interest. "Oh, that," he exclaimed. "I mind my own business. I've got to. There's plenty of bootlegging in the hills, but you don't hear me talking. No, sir – what is it?"
[1688] "Forget the whole thing," Eisam said sharply. "We've never been here. Understand? Don't report this to Arroyo or any other county officials. You know nothing about old Pete."
[1689] Rudding Constable's large hand closed around the object Ellery had taken from his wallet. "Mr. Eisam," he said eagerly, "I'm deaf, dumb, and blind... Is that all right?"
[1690] "Yes."
[1691] Judge Rudd turned away, showing no concern, and walked quietly through the woods, not once looking back.
[1692] Escham and Ellery exchanged glances and then shrugged their shoulders, turning to walk to the barbed wire fence.
[1693] As soon as their feet touched the ground in front of the fence – actually, Escham was about to throw the package he was holding over the top of the barbed wire fence – a harsh, hoarse voice called from inside the shed, "Stop! Go back!"
[1694] They stopped abruptly, dropping the package to the ground. They noticed that the muzzle of a rifle poked out from the only window of the cabin, which was protected by barbed wire. The ugly weapon did not waver; it was real, beyond doubt.
[1695] Ellery held his breath, and the district attorney stood still. "That's old Pete's voice," Ellery whispered. He raised his head and shouted, "Wait! Move your finger off the trigger. We're friends."
[1696] They stood motionless, being slowly scrutinized by the owner of the rifle.
[1697] Then the shrill voice echoed in their ears again: "I don't believe you! Go out. If you don't leave in five seconds, I'll shoot."
[1698] Isaac called out, "We're police, you fool! Here's a letter for you - a letter from Megalas. Hurry up! We don't want to be seen here."
[1699] The muzzle did not move, but the old man's thick-bearded head was dimly visible behind the wire curtain, and his bright eyes suspiciously watched them. They could feel his suspicion.
[1700] The old man disappeared, and the rifle disappeared. A moment later, the heavy, nail-studded door creaked open, and Old Pete himself stood there - with gray beard, unshaven, and in ragged clothes. The gun lowered, but the muzzle still pointed at them.
[1701] "Climb the fence, buddy. There's no other entrance."
[1702] They looked at the fence despondently. Eleanor sighed and elegantly lifted one leg over the lowest wire. She carefully tried to find a safe grip.
[1703] "Hurry up," Old Pete said impatiently, "neither of you is going to pull a fast one on me."
[1704] Isaac found a stick on the ground and propped it between the two lowest wires. Eleanor climbed in, but her shoulder was torn when she climbed. The district attorney clumsily followed behind; neither of them spoke, and the gun's barrel never left them.
[1705] They rushed towards the man, and the old mountain man retreated into the hut. When they entered, Isaac pushed the heavy door shut and locked it. It was a very simple dwelling, but a careful hand had made it neat. The floor was made of stone, clean, and covered with mats. In one corner was a well-stocked pantry, and near the fireplace was a neatly stacked pile of firewood. A basin-like device leaned against the back wall, opposite the door, which was evidently the mountain man's bathroom; above it hung a shelf for storing medicine. Above the basin was a small hand pump; a well must be under the house.
[1706] "Letter," Old Pete croaked.
[1707] Esam took out the note. The old, cataract-stricken mountain man still did not lower his rifle; he read the letter haltingly, never taking his eyes off his guests. However, as he read, his attitude changed. It was still the same beard, still the same ragged clothes, but Old Pete himself was different. He slowly leaned the rifle against the table and sat down, fingering the letter.
[1708] "So Tomasov is dead," he said, his voice shocking them. It was not Old Pete's hoarse noise; it was humble, educated, the voice of a well-educated man.
[1709] "Yes, he was murdered," Esam replied. "He left a short note - would you like to see it?"
[1710] "Please show it to me." The man took the note from Esam's hand and read it quickly and emotionlessly. He nodded. "I understand... Well, gentlemen, I am here, Andrew Van - formerly Andrew Gaetzel. I am still alive, and Tom, the stubborn fool -"
[1711] His bright eyes glazed over, and he impatiently stood up and walked to the iron sink. Ellery and Isam exchanged glances. What an odd man, this fellow! Van removed the thick beard, took off the heavy white wig. He washed off the face paint. ... When he turned around, he was a completely different person from the one who had challenged them from the window. Tall, erect, with short black hair, a sharp ascetic face, worn by hardship. The ragged clothes hung on his muscular frame, and Ellery thought of Rabelais' words, "grotesque, out of tune, out of place."
[1712] "I'm sorry, I don't have chairs for you, gentlemen. You must be the district attorney Isam, I think, and you... I think I've seen you, Mr. Quin, sitting in the first row of the Wilt Court during the trial."
[1713] "Yes," Ellery said.
[1714] This man was remarkable. Of course, an oddity. He apologized for having only one chair and sat down on it, leaving the two guests standing. "This is my hideout. A nice place, isn't it?" His tone was pained, "I think it was Krossak who did it?"
[1715] "It seems so," Isam whispered. He and Ellery were surprised by the resemblance between the man and Stephen Magall. They had a great family resemblance. "Stephen's letter said he -" Van trembled, "he used T."
[1716] "Yes, beheading. Terribly frightening. So you're Andrew Tewell!"
[1717] The school principal grimaced. "In my original country, I was Andrew Tewell, my brothers were Stephen and Tomislav. When we came here, we hoped –" He shrugged and sat stiffly, gripping the rough chair's seat. His eyes darted towards the heavy door and the barred window. "Are you sure," he rasped, "you haven't been followed?"
[1718] Eisam tried to appear reassuring. "Absolutely. We've been careful, Mr. Tewell. Your brother Stephen was publicly protected by Detective Wain of the Nassau County Police Department when he traveled from Long Island to New York."
[1719] Andrew Tewell bit his thin upper lip. "It's come, it's come… it – I can't tell you how terrifying it is. After years of useless worry and fear, to see a terrible ghost take shape… Do you want to hear my story?"
[1720] "In this situation," Ellery said coldly, "don't you think we have the right to know?"
[1721] "Yes," the headmaster said solemnly, "Stephen and I will need all the protection we can get... What did he tell you?"
[1722] "Only that you and Brad were his brothers," Isam said. "Now we want to know -"
[1723] Andrew Tewell stood up, his gaze suddenly icy. "Not a word until I see Stephen. No!"
[1724] His sudden change in demeanor left both men staring at him. "But why, man?" Isam called. "We've traveled hundreds of miles to get here -"
[1725] The man picked up his gun, and Isam took a step back. "I didn't say anyone was posing as someone else. The letters are Stephen's and Tom's. But these things can be arranged. I've taken so many precautions, I can't let myself be duped. Where's Stephen?"
[1726] "In Bradwood," Ellery drawled, "Don't act like a child, man; put down the gun. As for you not saying anything until you see your brother—well, Megala anticipated this, and we're prepared. Your skepticism is understandable, and we'll grant any reasonable request. Right, Esam?"
[1727] "Yes," the district attorney growled. He picked up the package he had brought up the mountain. "How about this, Mr. Tewell? What do you think?"
[1728] The man hesitated, looking at the package; it was clear he was uncertain. Finally, he said, "Open it."
[1729] Esam tore open the brown paper. Inside was a complete set of police uniforms, shoes, and a gun.
[1730] "Nothing to raise suspicion," Ellery said. "Once we get to Bradwood, you'll be a police officer. There are plenty of them around that area. Anyone in uniform looks like any other in uniform, Mr. Tewell."
[1731] The principal paced back and forth on the slate floor. "Leave the shed…" he grumbled, "I've been safe here for months. I—"
[1732] "The gun is loaded," Esam said coldly, "and you have plenty of ammunition in your belt. With a loaded weapon and two strong escorts, what could happen?"
[1733] He blushed. "I guess I look like a coward to you two… well."
[1734] He began to remove his ragged clothes and put on clean, decent undergarments. At this point, they noticed another discord. He began to awkwardly put on the police uniform.
[1735] "It fits," Elyot said, "Megalas's measurements were spot on."
[1736] The principal said nothing… After he put on his uniform, the gun was inserted into the thick leather holster on his side, and he presented a formidable image: tall, strong, and to some extent, handsome. His hand unconsciously reached for the weapon, caressing it, as if drawing strength from it.
[1737] "I'm ready," he said, his voice firm.
[1738] "All right!" Isam approached the door; Ellery glanced at the window with the barbed wire. "Is it all right, Mr. Quin?"
[1739] "It seems so."
[1740] Isam unlocked the door, and they quickly stepped out… The clearing in the woods was deserted; the sun was setting, and the trees were already shrouded in twilight. Ellery struggled over the barbed wire fence at the low point, followed closely by Isam. They both stood, watching the man in uniform who followed them, climbing over the barbed wire fence with ease – to Ellery's envy.
[1741] Andrew Ga Tveril deliberately closed the door. Smoke still rose from the chimney. To anyone lingering near the forest edge, the hut looked inhabited and impregnable.
[1742] The three men rushed towards the forest, the trees providing cover for their heads. They cautiously followed the faintly visible path, leading them to the clump of bushes where DuSchenberg was waiting for them. There was no sign of anyone else in the mountains or on the road.

[1748] Chapter XVIII: Fox's Speech
[1749] Ellery and Isam left on Friday night and were not around all Saturday, but this did not make Bradwood peaceful. The mysterious journey of Vawn and Stephen seemed to attract the attention of the entire community and became a topic of conversation among everyone. Even Oyster Island felt the impact; Hester Lincoln struggled through the thicket of the road, crossing the "temple" of Harakat and the woods between the island's eastern end, inquiring about what had happened to Kekem the old man.
[1750] However, until Vawn and Megal returned, Bradwood enjoyed peace. Professor Yadley kept his promise and stayed in his amazing shelter.
[1751] Around noon - when Ellery and Escham rushed through South Pennsylvania between Harrisburg and Pittsburgh, heading for Arroyo - that striking procession of people returned to Bredwood. Motorcyclists led the way on both sides, with police cars bringing up the rear. The vehicles sped down the lane and came to a sudden stop. The car door opened, and Inspector Vance jumped out. Meagal followed slowly, his face grim and his eyes darting around alertly. Meagal was immediately surrounded by his escorts, who led him around the house to the bay dock. His yacht was waiting for him. With the police boat trailing behind, he returned to the Helen, disappearing down the ladder. The police boat circled the yacht repeatedly.
[1752] In the doorway of the colonial-era house, a detective who had been sitting comfortably in a chair throughout, stood up and handed the inspector a large envelope. Vance, who had felt particularly helpless this morning, snatched it up as if it were a lifeline. As he read the letter, the look of helplessness disappeared from his face, and his expression became stern.
[1753] "It was just delivered by courier about half an hour ago," the detective explained.
[1754] Helen Bredwood appeared in the doorway, and the inspector hastily put the letter in his pocket.
[1755] "What's going on here?" Helen asked. "Where is Stephen? I think you should explain all of this to us, Inspector!"
[1756] "Megala has returned to his yacht," Vane replied. "No, Miss Bradshaw, I'm not obligated to explain. If you'll excuse me –"
[1757] "I won't excuse you," Helen said angrily, her eyes flashing. "I think you people are acting barbaric. Where were you and Stephen this morning?"
[1758] "I'm sorry," Vane said. "No comment. Miss Bradshaw, please –"
[1759] "But Stephen looks sick. You wouldn't be torturing him, would you?"
[1760] Vane grinned. "Oh, those are just rumors in the papers. There's no truth to it. Does he look sick? I suppose he might be feeling unwell. He did say his groin hurt quite a bit."
[1761] Helen stamped her foot. "You're all heartless! I'm going to get Dr. Temple to come up to the yacht and see him."
[1762] "Go ahead," the officer said eagerly. "I have no objection." As she strode out of the veranda and up the path between the totem poles, he breathed a sigh of relief. But Vance clenched his jaw again. "Come on, Johnny. There's something to do."
[1763] With the detective accompanying him, the officer descended from the veranda and made his way through the trees along the western path. The small house where the gardener and chauffeur Fox was being held came into view through the trees. A plainclothesman lounged on the steps.
[1764] "Quiet?"
[1765] "Not a peep."
[1766] Vance opened the door roughly and stepped into the cottage, followed by his men. Fox's pale, bearded face was drawn and his violet eyes were dark with fatigue. He turned eagerly toward the visitors, but when he saw who they were, he clamped his lips together and began pacing.
[1767] "I'm giving you one last chance," the officer said abruptly. "Are you willing to talk?"
[1768] Fox's heavy footsteps sounded with an unbroken rhythm.
[1769] "Still won't tell me why you went to see Patsy Malone?"
[1770] There was no answer.
[1771] "Well," Vane said, lazily sitting down, "the consequences are on you, Pendleton."
[1772] The man's step stopped for a moment and then resumed. His face remained expressionless.
[1773] "Good lad," Vane sneered, "you're really bold. And thick-skinned. You're in a dead end, Pendleton. Because we know all about you."
[1774] Fox muttered, "I don't know what you're talking about."
[1775] "You've been in trouble with the law."
[1776] "I don't know what you mean."
[1777] "You've done time, haven't you? Don't you know what it means to be in trouble with the law? Alright, alright," the officer smiled, "but I'll tell you, Pendleton, you're a damn fool. I don't hold it against you for having been in jail... his smile faded. "I'm telling you the truth, Pendleton. Denying it won't help you. You're in a tight spot, you know? You have a record, and in this situation, it's best to come clean."
[1778] The man's eyes showed a look of distress. "I have nothing to defend myself with."
[1779] "Nothing? Alright, let's talk. Imagine I come across a thief in New York. A jewelry store's safe has been cracked... do you think the person I come across needs to explain anything? Think about it."
[1780] The tall man stopped and leaned forward, resting his elbows on the table, his knuckles white against the dark surface. "For God's sake, officer," he said, "give me a chance! Yes, I'm Pendleton. But I tell you, I'm innocent in this case! I want to go straight—"
[1781] "Oh," the officer said, "that's good. Now we know where the problem is. You're Phil Pendleton, who served five years in the Vandervliet State Prison in Illinois for theft. Last year, you heroically saved the warden's life during the prison break. The governor of Illinois reduced your sentence. You have a record - in California for assault, in Michigan for breaking and entering. Serving time for both... Now, if you're honest, we don't want to harm you. If you don't come clean and admit it, I can easily take you down. Did you kill Thomas Brad?"
[1782] The man named Fox in Breedwood collapsed into a chair, weakly. "No," he whispered, "God forbid, officer."
[1783] "How did you get your last job - from the person who gave you the reference?"
[1784] He said without looking up, "I want to start over. He - he had no problems. Business was bad, and he fired me. That's all."
[1785] "Is there any particular reason you took on this dual role as gardener and driver?"
[1786] "No, it's outdoor work, pays well..."
[1787] "Good. If you expect us to consider your case, you need to clarify your visit to Malone. If you want to go straight, why would you go to someone like Malone, a bunch of crooks?"
[1788] Fox remained silent for a long time. He then stood up, his face darkening. "I have the right to live my own life..."
[1789] "You certainly do, Pendleton," said the officer kindly. "That's the way it should be. We'll help you."
[1790] Fox spoke quickly, looking at the detectives in the doorway but not really seeing them. "An old - an old prison buddy tracked me down here. I first heard about it on Tuesday morning. He insisted on seeing me, and I refused - I cut him off. He threatened, 'You don't want me to tell your boss, do you?' So I went."
[1791] Vance nodded, listening intently. "Go on, child, go on."
[1792] "He told me where to meet—no name, just an address in New York. On Tuesday night, after I let Stollings and Mrs. Baxter off at the Rockside Theater, I drove there and parked in the next block. A thug let me in. I met—someone. He made me an offer. I said no, I wanted to cut ties with my past and not get involved in any more criminal activities. He told me to consider it, and if I didn't, he'd tell Mr. Brad about who I am. I fell off the wagon—the rest you know."
[1793] "When he heard about the murder, he naturally put it on hold," Vance grumbled, "That was Patsy Marlon, wasn't it?"
[1794] "I—well, I can't say."
[1795] Vance gave him a sharp look. "Not wanting to betray, I see. What was the offer?"
[1796] Fox shook his head. "I won't say any more, Officer. It's good of you to want to help, but if I spill the beans, it'll be a black mark on me."
[1797] The officer stood up. "I understand. Well, off the record, I can't fault you. It sounds like the truth… by the way—Fox…"
[1798] "In New York, Officer. Looking for work. I saw Mr. Brad's ad and came to apply, and he hired me on New Year's Day."
[1799] "Okay." The officer sighed. "Well, Fox, for your sake, I hope what you say is true. In this situation, I'm at a loss. You'll have to stay around here. No guards, no detainment, but you'll be under police surveillance, and I don't want you trying to escape."
[1800] "I won't, Officer!" Fox called out, a new glimmer of hope in his eyes.
[1801] "Keep living as if nothing happened. If you're innocent, I won't mention this to Mrs. Brad, and I won't reveal your past to her."
[1802] Faced with this generosity, Fox stood speechless. The officer gestured to his men and left the shack.
[1803] Fox slowly followed to the doorway, watching as the officer and two detectives walked down the path into the woods. His chest rose, and he took in the warm air deeply.
[1804] Vorn found Helen Brad on the porch of the big house.
[1805] "Tormenting poor Fox again." She snorted with her nose.
[1806] "Fox is fine," the officer said briefly, weariness and helplessness evident on his face, "found Temple?"
[1807] "Dr. Temple is out, sailing on his steamboat somewhere. I left him a note to see Stephen as soon as he returns."
[1808] "Out, huh?"
[1809] Vance nodded wearily in the general direction of Oyster Island.

[1812] Chapter 19: T
[1813] On Sunday morning at 9:15, Officer Vance, who had spent the night at Bradwood, was called by Stollings to answer the phone. [1848] The third policeman stepped forward and licked his lips. The person on the bed grabbed the blanket.
[1849] They stared at each other silently, without shaking hands.
[1850] "Stephen," the headmaster said.
[1851] "Andrew Jagg."
[1852] Ellery felt a surprising urge to giggle; there was something ridiculous about the whole scene, despite its tragic undertones. These two stalwart men with foreign names—the yacht, the suffering in bed, the drab uniforms... He had never encountered such a scene in all his experiences.
[1853] "Croisac. Andrew Jagg," the patient said, "Croisac found us, just as you predicted."
[1854] Andrew Jagg Tewell said harshly, "If Tom had listened to me... I wrote him a letter last December warning him. He didn't contact you?"
[1855] Stephen slowly shook his head. "No. He didn't know where to find me. I was cruising in the Pacific... How are you, Andrew?"
[1856] "Fine. How long has it been?"
[1857] "Years… five? six?"

Mr. Van…" he indicated the principal, "Mr. Van must leave Bradwood as soon as possible. Every moment he lingers here increases the danger. Crozak, whoever he is, is very sharp. He may easily see through our little charade, and we don't want him to follow Mr. Van back to West Virginia."
[1859] "Yes," Van said gravely. "That's the truth. Stephen, tell them."
[1860] The yacht owner sat up straighter on the bed—or the pain left him, or in his excitement he forgot the pain—and gazed at the low ceiling of the cabin. "Where do I begin? It happened so long ago. Tomislav, Andrew Ga, and I were the last of the Tverils. A proud, wealthy clan in the Black Mountains."
[1861] "It has already disappeared." The principal spoke in a cold voice.
[1862] The patient waved his hand, as if it didn't matter. "You have to understand, we come from the hottest Balkan blood. Hot – hot enough to hiss.” Megalha chuckled briefly, "The Tver family has a feud with the Croats – another clan. For generations –"
[1863] "Inter-tribal feuds! No doubt about it," the professor exclaimed. "Not exactly Italian style, but definitely a blood feud, just like the old families in the Kentucky hills."
[1864] "Yes," Megal interrupted, "for some reason, there is still such a thing as a blood feud - the original cause is stained with blood, and by the time it reaches our generation, we can no longer understand why. But we have been taught from a young age -"
[1865] "Kill the Croscius family." The headmaster called out.
[1866] "We are the attackers," Mejgal continued, frowning, "Twenty years ago, due to the cruelty of our grandfather and father, only one Croscos man was left alive – Vilga, the one you're after – he was just a child then. He and his mother were the only survivors of the Croscos clan."
[1867] "How distant it seems," Rang mumbled, "How savage you, Tomislav, and I were, setting a trap for the Croscos' father and two uncles to avenge the kin."
[1868] "Completely unbelievable," Ellery whispered to the professor, "Unbelievable that we are dealing with civilized people."
[1869] "What about the youngest Croscos?" Eisam asked.
[1870] "His mother took him to Montenegro and fled. They went to Italy, hid there, and soon his mother died."
[1871] "So, it's only the youngest Crowsack left to avenge you," Wan said thoughtfully, "I think his mother must have filled him with madness before she died. Have you managed to track down the boy's whereabouts?"
[1872] "Yes, we must do so for our own protection. We know that when he grows up, he will try to kill us. We have hired agents to track him across Europe, but he disappeared without a trace before he turned seventeen, and we haven't heard from him since - until now."
[1873] "Did your people ever see him?"
[1874] "No, we haven't seen him since he left our mountains, which was around the time he was eleven or twelve years old."
[1875] "Wait a moment," Ellery said, furrowing his brow, "How can you be so sure that the Crowsack wants to kill you? After all, he's just a child..."
[1876] "How can we be sure?" Andrew Van smiled bitterly. "When he was under surveillance, one of our agents managed to coax his true feelings out of him. He swore to wipe us all out, no matter where we were."
[1877] "So you mean to say," Esham asked, "that because of a child's ranting, you really left your country, changed your names?"
[1878] The two men blushed. "You don't understand the Croatian blood feud," the yacht owner avoided looking at the others. "A Crozak once tracked a Tivoli into the heart of South Arabia – generations ago..."
[1879] "So, you're sure: even if you met Crozak face to face, you wouldn't recognize him, right?" Ellery suddenly asked.
[1880] "How could we recognize him?... We are the only three left in the world. Our parents are dead. We decided to leave Montenegro for America. There is no bond between us – Andrew and I are not married, and Tom, although married, has no children.
[1881] "We were a wealthy family, with valuable property. We sold everything, used fake names, and came to this country separately, arranging to meet in New York. In the end, we decided to take different nationalities."
[1882] "I warned you about Croate," the headmaster said grimly.
[1883] "Tom and I - we were both well-educated - engaged in our current business. Andrew was always restless, and he preferred to work alone, studying English, and eventually became a principal of a small school. Of course, we all became American citizens. Gradually, as the years passed, with no news of Croate or his letters, we almost forgot him. He became - at least for Tom and me - a legend, a myth. We thought he was dead or had given up on his quest for revenge."
[1884] "If you had listened to my advice," Van said sternly, "this would not have happened, and Tom would be alive today. I repeatedly told you that Croate would come back for revenge!"
[1885] "Enough, Andrew," Megala said sternly; but there was a look of pity in his eyes when he looked at his brother. "I know. And you don't see us often. You must realize that it is your fault. If you had shown more brotherly affection, maybe...
[1886] "Why would I want to stay in that hole with you and Tom, where Crocetta could have taken us all out at once?" the man from Arroyo called out. "I love life, Stephen! But I'm smart, and you –"
[1887] "And I'm not so smart, Andrew," the yacht owner said. "After all, Crocetta found you first. And –"
[1888] "Yes," the officer said, "he found Mr. Range first. I want to clarify the murder in Arroyo, Mr. Range, if you don't mind."
[1889] The headmaster felt uncomfortable with the sad memories. "Arroyo," he said in a hoarse voice, "a terrible place. My fear caused me to start pretending to be Old Pete. I felt that having a double personality would be useful, in case Crocetta found me –" He growled, "He found me, but –" He paused and then quickly continued, "I kept the shack for years. It was an abandoned shack I discovered while exploring some caves in the mountains. I built a barbed wire fence and bought disguise supplies in Pittsburgh. During my spare time at the school, I would sneak up the mountain and appear in town as Old Pete, giving the people of Arroyo the impression that Old Pete was real. Tom and Stephen – they always mocked this cunning trick. They said it was childish. Is it childish, Stephen? Do you still think so? Don't you think that in his grave, Tom regrets not following my example?"
[1890] "Yes, yes," Megalos spoke quickly, "tell us your story, Andrew."
[1891] The eccentric principal paced around the cabin, his hands behind his borrowed uniform, his eyes wandering. Then, everyone heard a surprising story.
[1892] As Christmas approached – he spoke in a distinctive tense tone – he realized it had been two months since he had appeared as Old Mountain Man in Arroyo. He hadn't shown up in so long that some people in town – maybe Sheriff Redden – might come looking for the old man in the mountains, investigating his cabin… He pointed out that it would be a disaster for his carefully maintained deception. There was still a week between Christmas and the end of his school's holiday, so he still had a few days to safely play the recluse Old Pete. He had always impersonated the ragged man during school breaks or weekends.
[1893] "How did you explain your absence to Kline?" Ellery asked. "Or did your servant participate in the secret?"
[1894] "No!" Van exclaimed. "He's a fool, an idiot. I only told him I was going on vacation in Wheeling or Pittsburgh."
[1895] So, on Christmas Eve, he told Kline he was going to Pittsburgh to celebrate Christmas. That night, he left for the mountain shack – naturally, all his mountain man clothes were stored there. There, he became Old Pete again. The next morning – Christmas morning – he got up early, set out on foot to town, as he needed supplies. Although it was Christmas, most shops were closed, but he knew he could get what he needed from the grocer Bernheim. He arrived at the intersection of the main road and Arroyo Street at 6:30 am, where he was alone, and discovered the terrible corpse nailed to the cross. The twisted T-shape meaning terrified him. He quickly rushed to his home a hundred yards away on Arroyo Road. Later, when others saw the slaughterhouse-like scene, it had a painful meaning for him; he immediately realized that, purely by chance, Croasco had come to kill poor Kline the night before (thinking he was Andrew), cut off his head, and nailed his body to the landmark.
[1896] He had to think quickly. What should he do? Since fate had been kind, Crosack now believed he had avenged Andrew Gage; why not let him continue to believe? By permanently impersonating Old Pete, he could not only fool Crosack but also the small West Virginia world he lived in... Fortunately, the clothes Kin was wearing when he was killed were a set of old clothes that Van had given him a few days earlier. He knew that people in Arooy would consider anyone wearing those clothes to be Andrew Van - their principal; and if he put a few more items in those pockets that could identify him as Andrew Van, the identity of the deceased would not be suspected.
[1897] After getting letters and keys from his old clothes, the principal sneaked back to the intersection, took all the items from the mutilated body that belonged to Kin - a gruesome task that still sends shivers down Van's spine - put his own things in their place, and then deliberately walked away from the road to a nearby forest. There, he built a small fire and burned Kin's personal items, then waited for someone to pass by.
[1898] "Why?" Wain asked. "Why didn't you run away and hide in your hut?"
[1899] "Because," Van said simply, "I had to go to town immediately to warn my brothers that Crosack had appeared. If I went to town without mentioning the body at the intersection, I would be suspected because I had to pass by that intersection to enter town. If I went to town alone and told my story, I would still be suspected. But if I waited for someone to pass by, say a nearby innocent person, I would have a 'discoverer' of the body and go to town to buy food and give my brothers a warning."
[1900] Michael O'Keefe, the farmer, arrived at the crime scene about an hour later. Van, or Old Pete, cleverly pretended to slowly walk along the road towards the intersection. He greeted O'Keefe, who invited him to get in the car, and then they discovered the corpse... As for the rest, Van calmly said, "Mr. Quin attended the interrogation, and you all know about it."
[1901] "Did you notify your brothers?"
[1902] "Yes. After finding Clinton's body at the crossroads, I hastily wrote a brief letter to Thomas—the one you call Thomas Brad—at my home. When we excitedly arrived in town, I managed to slip the letter through the mail slot—the post office was still closed. In the letter, I simply told Tom what happened and warned him that Crowsley was probably bent on revenge. I wrote that from then on, I would be Old Pitt, and neither he nor Stephen should mention it. I, at least, wanted to protect myself from Crowsley's wrath—because I am dead."
[1903] "You're lucky," Megala says bitterly, "When Tom received your letter, because he couldn't reach me, he must have written that note to the police—as a final warning to me before anything happened to me before I got back to Bradwood."
[1904] The two brothers look pale and tense; they are under immense pressure, both physically and mentally. Megala even becomes disoriented. They are startled when a hoarse laugh comes from outside the deck, only to realize that it is one of the crew members of the Helen mocking a policeman.
[1905] "Well," Esam finally says hopelessly, "Knowing the truth is good, but what good does it do us? It's still impossible to catch Crowsley."
[1906] "This is too pessimistic," Ellery said. "This is an excuse for one's own incompetence. Gentlemen, does anyone now or in the past know about the feud between the Tewell and Crosby families? A little investigation along that line may help us narrow down the suspects."
[1907] "No one but ourselves knows," the headmaster said gloomily. "I haven't told anyone."
[1908] "No record of this feud?"
[1909] "No."
[1910] "Good," Ellery said thoughtfully. "So the disseminator of this history can only be Crosby. Although it can be imagined that he might have told someone, but the possibility is small. Why would he tell anyone? Crosby is now an adult—and a madman obsessed with revenge. He would feel that his revenge must be done by himself; those things cannot be entrusted to agents or accomplices, can they, Mr. Megala?"
[1911] "That's true in Black Mountain," the yacht owner said solemnly.
[1912] "Of course, it's obvious to anyone familiar with feud philosophy," Professor Yadley said, "In the ancient Balkan feuds, only family members can wash away the stain. Feuds more bloody than our own mountain feuds."
[1913] Ellery nodded. "Would Croscott tell anyone in this country? Almost impossible. It would leave him at the mercy of others, or leave a trail. And Croscott, from his cunning actions, is a careful villain, albeit an obsessive one. What could he offer a co-conspirator?"
[1914] "Good question," Isam admitted.
[1915] "His theft of the money from Mr. Van's iron box—"
[1916] "There was one hundred and forty dollars in the box," Van murmured.
[1917] "—indicates that Croscius is cash-strapped and takes what he can get, unable to maintain a co-conspirator. But your brother Tomislav's house was not robbed. There was certainly no co-conspirator then, for if there had been, he would not have missed the chance to steal what he could (Croscius has no money to offer in return). These two murders were revenge, not robbery... are there any other signs of no co-conspirator? Yes, in the murder of Klein, only one person was seen at the crossroads, that person being Vilga Croscius."
[1918] "What are you trying to prove?" Wain roared.
[1919] "I'm just trying to prove that Croscius is acting alone, telling no one of his desire for revenge—from his personal motives, the terrible method, and the behavior of a recluse. These things he has not tried to hide. Remember, Croscius has actually signed his crime in both instances by leaving T at the scene. Whether he's crazy or not, he must have realized this. And it's incredible that a co-conspirator would be willing to ally himself—especially after the first murder—with such a fallen, shameless maniac."
[1920] "And all this means nothing to you," the officer interrupted angrily. "Why bother about an imaginary co-conspirator? We've made no progress in finding the principal suspect, Mr. Queen!"
[1921] Ellery shrugged; evidently, for him, excluding a possible co-conspirator or confidant in Crostach's secrets was of utmost importance.
[1922] The district attorney, Esham, paced between the two brothers. "Listen," he finally said. "We can't let this get us all mixed up. One person can't just disappear without a trace. We need to learn more about his appearance. Assuming you two don't know what Crostach looks like today, can't you tell us more about him—other characteristics that wouldn't change from childhood?"
[1923] The two brothers looked at each other. "Limp," Van said, shrugging.
[1924] "I told you that," Meagal said. "When Crostach was a child, he contracted a mild case of hip joint disease—it didn't ruin his appearance, but it made his left leg lame."
[1925] "Permanent?" Ellery asked.
[1926] The Tewell brothers looked blank.
