He remembered you knew he was lame as a child, and he might have been pretending to be lame – of course, assuming he healed in the meantime."
[1928] "On the other hand," the officer said sternly, "the limp might be real. Why are you so determined to undermine every piece of evidence we have, Mr. Quin –"
[1929] "Very well," Ellery said coldly. "Crocker has a limp. Are you satisfied, officer?"
[1930] "We've wasted enough time," Vorn complained. "One thing is certain. From now on, you two must be fully protected. I suggest you return to Arroyo, Mr. Van, and avoid any public appearances. I'll send five or six police officers with you back to West Virginia and have them stay there."
[1931] "Oh, dear Lord," Ellery groaned. "Officer, do you realize what you're saying? You're giving Crocker an opportunity. We can assume that our strategy has been successful, and Crocker still doesn't know where Andrew Tewell is, although he knows he's alive. Focusing all our concern on Andrew Tewell would certainly draw Crocker's attention if he's watching us—which he undoubtedly is."
[1932] "What would you do?" Vance challenged.
[1933] "Mr. Van should be discreetly escorted back to his mountain cabin—by one person, not five or six, officer. Why not send a whole army? And then not make a big show of protecting him. As Old Pete, he's safe. The less fuss we make, the better for him."
[1934] "What about Mr. Megalas?" Escham asked. He seemed to be having difficulty choosing names for the brothers. "Just leave him alone?"
[1935] "Certainly not!" Ellery exclaimed. "Crocker expected to be protected, and he must be protected. You can publicize the fact that he's being protected any way you like."
[1936] As their fate was discussed by outsiders, the two brothers remained silent; they exchanged furtive glances, and Meagal's face grew even more severe, while the rector blinked uneasily and fidgeted.

[1940] "I think—" Van moistened his lips. "I'm not a wealthy man, Mr. Queen. You may think I'm a coward, but I live in the shadow of that devil—" His eyes blazed with an odd fire. "My brother Tomislav's will leaves me the money. I renounce the inheritance. I just want to get away—" His words were contradictory and fragmented, making everyone feel uncomfortable.
[1941] "No, Andy," Megala said gravely, "If you want to leave - well, you know best. But money… I'll advance it to you. Whatever you go, money is needed."
[1942] "How much?" Vawn asked suspiciously.
[1943] "It's not much," Megala's cold eyes grew colder, "Five thousand dollars. Tom could have provided…but Andrew is the youngest, and in our country, the concept of inheritance is strictly adhered to. I will -"

[1947] The headmaster paled. "If you think he doesn't know—"
[1948] "How could he know?" Vane snapped. "If it makes you feel any better, Mr. Megala, I'll arrange for you to get the money that's yours, and you can take it with you. That way, if you have to run away, you won't be penniless. But that's all we can do."
[1949] "With my own savings in my humble house," Van grumbled, "that'll be quite a bit. I'll be more than all right wherever I go…well. I'll go back to Arroyo. Thanks, Steve."
[1950] "Perhaps," the yacht owner said casually, "you'll need more money. How about I give you ten thousand instead of five—"
[1951] "No," the headmaster said firmly, straightening his shoulders. "I'll take what's mine. As you know, Stephen, I've always been able to make my own way."
[1952] Megalas shrank back from the bed and went to a desk, where he sat down and began writing a check. Andrew G. Tewell paced around. Now that his fate was decided, he seemed in a hurry to leave. The yacht owner stood up, waving the check.

[1957] "You too."
[1958] Their gazes met, an invisible barrier between them swaying, almost collapsing. But the barrier did not disappear. Megalas turned away, the principal slumped his shoulders, and walked towards the door.

[1963] "It's very clear! Why would three adults, as Escham said, abandon their homeland, change their names, and flee to a foreign land? Hmm?"
[1964] "Police!" Wain whispered.
[1965] "Quite right. I assure you they left because they had to leave, and they were being pursued by a danger more direct than that of the boy Crocok. If I were you, I would conduct a transatlantic investigation."

[2100] "No, Mrs. Brad," Wain said, "that's the truth. It's consistent with the fact that you and Miss Brad went to the Park Theater, but only Lincoln and your daughter came back in a taxi to this house. And it's also consistent with the fact that the Park Theater's doorman saw a woman like you leave during the first act... Roman said you had a date, and you met him near the docks."
[2101] She covered her ears. "Please don't say any more," she moaned, "I'm crazy. I don't know how it happened. I'm a fool... " They exchanged glances. "Hester hates me. She wants him too, she thinks—she thinks he's respectable..."
[2102] "He can't do that anymore, Mrs. Brad," Officer Vance said harshly, "no one is judging you or trying to judge you. It's your own life. If you're stupid enough to get involved with that scoundrel, I think you've had enough of it. Now we're only concerned with: how did you get home? What happened that night?"
[2103] Mrs. Brad's fingers twisted on her knees, and a choking sob almost choked her. "I—I slipped out of the theater during the performance; I told Helen I felt unwell and insisted she stay and wait for Jonah... I went to Pennsylvania Station and took the first train back—fortunately there was one leaving almost immediately. I—I got off a station early and hired a taxi to a location near Bradwood, and walked the rest of the way. It seemed that no one was around, so—so..."
[2104] "Of course," Isam said, "you didn't want Mr. Brad to know you were back. We understand."
[2105] "Yes," she murmured, her face flushing with an unhealthy red, "I met him—at the docks."
[2106] "What time was that?"
[2107] "Around ten thirty."
[2108] "You're sure you didn't see, hear, or encounter anyone?"
[2109] "Absolutely." She lifted her head, pain in her eyes. "Oh, you think, if I saw something or someone, I wouldn't say? And when—when I came back, I sneaked in and went straight to my room."
[2110] Escham was about to ask another question when the door opened silently, and Helen Brad appeared. She stood still, looking from her mother's sad face to the men's faces. "What's going on, Mom?" she asked firmly.
[2111] Mrs. Breed buried her head in her hands and sobbed.
[2112] "So, you said it all," Helen whispered, slowly closing the door, "you're too weak to keep it to yourself? You're weak." She looked disdainfully from Vane to Easom, then to the sobbing woman. "Don't cry, mom. It's said; other women have tried to relive their romances and failed. God knows..."
[2113] "Don't say that," Vane said, "it's unpleasant for everyone. How did you and Lincoln know where your mother was that night, Miss Breed?"
[2114] Helen sat next to her mother, patting her hunched broad back. "Well, mom... when mom left me that night—uh, I knew. But she didn't know I knew. I was weak too." She gazed at the floor, "I decided to wait for Jonah; we both noticed—uh, certain things. When he came, I told him. We came back home. I looked in this room, mom was lying in bed, asleep... but when you found that—the body..."
[2115] "Did she admit it to you?"
[2116] "Yes."
[2117] "Please allow me to ask two questions," Ellery said earnestly. The woman's eyes, very much like her mother's, turned to him. "When did you first suspect something was going to happen, Miss Bradley?"
[2118] "Oh!" She shakes her head, looking distressed, "A few weeks, a few weeks ago."
[2119] "Do you think your stepfather knows?"
[2120] Mrs. Bradshaw suddenly lifted her head, her face smeared with tears and powder. "No!" she cried, "No!"
[2121] Helen murmured, "I'm sure he didn't know."
[2122] District Attorney Isaam abruptly said, "I think that's enough, let's go." He walked towards the door and stepped into the hall.
[2123] Inspector Vance, Professor Yardley, and Ellery followed him meekly.

[2131] Chapter 21: A Spat Between Lovers
[2132] "Nothing to show for it." The next evening, Ellery said, as he and Dr. Avery sat on Avery's lawn, gazing up at the stars over Long Island.
[2133] "Hmm." Dr. Avery said, with a sigh. "To be honest, I've been waiting for the fireworks to start, Ellery."
[2134] "Patience, my friend. It's our Independence Day, after all. There will be fireworks. Look! There's one now!"
[2135] They sat in silence, watching as a long, bright streak of light shot up into the dark sky and exploded into a shower of falling sparks. The single firework seemed like a signal, and soon the entire Long Island coastline was lit up with fireworks, and they sat there, watching the festivities on the North Shore. In the distance, they could barely make out the tiny, firefly-like flashes in the sky above the New York coastline.
[2136] "I'm disappointed," the professor grumbled. "I've heard so much about your exceptional detective skills, Quine, and yet—I'm sorry if I'm blasphemous—it's been a letdown. When are you going to start, Quine? I mean—when is Sherlock going to show his hand and cuff the cowardly murderer?"
[2137] Ellery looked glumly at the patterns of light swirling around the North Star. "I'm wondering if there won't be an end—or a beginning…"
[2138] "Doesn't look like it," said Professor Yardley, removing his pipe from his mouth. "Don't you think it was a mistake to pull the police out? Temple told me this morning; he said the county sheriff issued a withdrawal order. I don't understand why."
[2139] Ellery shrugged. "Why not? Clearly, Crosetti is only after two people—Stephen Megalos and Andrew Van, or the Tewell brothers, as you like. Megalos has the waterways and Wain's squad for protection; Van has his disguise, which is good enough.
[2140] "There are many factors in the second crime, Professor, that we need to discuss; they have important implications on their own; but they don't point to anything specific yet."
[2141] "I can't think of anything."
[2142] "Really?" Ellery stopped and looked at a sizzling Roman firework tube [3]. "You mean you haven't read the whole and very interesting story of the chess game?"
[2143] "Chess, is it?" Yardley's short beard was dimly visible in front of the glow of his pipe. "I admit, there's nothing I find meaningful about the last supper of Bread."
[2144] "So I regain my lost self-respect," Ellery muttered. "The story is very clear. But, damn it, it's clearer than Vance and Esam's simple guesses…" He stood up and stuck his hands in his pockets. "Can I leave for a while? I need to go out for a walk and let my foggy mind clear up."
[2145] "Of course." The professor leaned back, puffing on his pipe, curiously watching Ellery's retreating figure.
[2146] Ellery strolled under the stars and fireworks. The night was dark and heavy, the kind of black that is typical of the countryside. He crossed the road between Adler and Breadwood, feeling his way blindly ahead, breathing the night air, and listening to the distant sound of the water festival boat. Breadwood was cold and lonely, except for a night lamp on the porch. Ellery could see the porch because he accidentally drove up the driveway and two detectives were smoking there.
[2147] As he passed the house, one of the detectives stood up and called out, "Who's that over there?"
[2148] Ellery raised a hand to shield the blinding beam of the flashlight.
[2149] "Oh," the detective said, "Sorry, Mr. Quinn."
[2150] The flashlight went out. "So alert," Ellery murmured, continuing to walk around the house.
[2151] At this point, he wondered why his feet had led him in this direction. He was approaching the path that led to the ominous totem pole and gazebo. The path and its destination exuded a terror - perhaps his subconscious sensitivity to terrifying scenes - that he hastened to pass.
[2152] Suddenly, he stopped. Not far to the right, near the tennis court, someone was talking.
[2153] Although Ellery was usually a gentleman, he had learned one thing from his father, the excellent detective - besides his affinity for crime, the detective was a gentleman in everything - that is, "Always listen to people talking." This old man often said, "The only valuable evidence, child, is what people say when they think no one is listening. Sometimes listening once will give you more than a hundred times of questioning."
[2154] So Ellery, a dutiful son, stayed where he was and listened.
[2155] The voices came from a man and a woman. The tones of both voices were familiar to his ears, but he couldn't make out the words. He bent down as low as he could, like an Indian, and silently jumped from the gravel path onto the grass at the side of the road, carefully approaching the source of the voices.
[2156] Finally, he realized who the speakers were - Jonathan Lincoln and Helen Brad.
[2157] It seemed they were sitting at a garden table on the west side of the tennis court - Ellery vaguely remembered the terrain. He crawled to within five feet of them, just coming to a tree behind them.
[2158] "Denying this won't do you any good, Jonathan Lincoln," he heard Helen say coldly.
[2159] "But, Helen," Jonathan said, "I've told you countless times, Roman -"
[2160] "Nonsense! He wouldn't be so careless. Only - only you, with your ridiculous ideas, your - your despicable cowardice..."
[2161] "Helen!" Jonah was greatly hurt. "How can you say that? Yes, like Sir Galahad, I tried to punch him a few times, and he knocked me out, but -"
[2162] "Well, maybe I was unfair to you, Jonah." There was a silence. Ellery knew she was trying to hold back tears. "Of course, I can't say you haven't tried. But you always - oh, interfere."
[2163] Ellery could imagine the scene. He was sure the young man was stunned. "Is that so?" Jonah said painfully. "Well, that's all I wanted to know. Interfere, right? I'm just an outsider, not entitled. Well, Helen. I'll stop interfering. I'll -"
[2164] "Jonah!" Now her voice was panicked. "What do you mean? I didn't -"
[2165] "I mean what I say," Jonah complained indignantly. "All these years I've been doing good, being a horse for the man who's out at sea all year round and the other one who's sitting at home playing chess. Well, that was a mistake! That damn salary isn't worth it. I'm leaving with Hester, by God, I've told you precious Melchior! I told him this afternoon on the yacht. Let him manage his own business, switch things up; I'm sick of working for him."
[2166] There was a tense silence during which neither of the two spoke. Elyria sighed in relief from his hiding place behind the tree. He could imagine what was coming.
[2167] He could hear Helen take a deep breath and feel Jonah's defensive stiffness. "After all, Jonah," she whispered. "It's not like - like you don't owe your father anything. He - he did a lot for you, didn't he?"
[2168] "I'm not being cruel," Jonah said solemnly.
[2169] "You are! Ah, Jonah..." Another silence, during which Elyria imagined that the young woman neither moved her chair closer to her ventrator nor leaned back against it. She was like Calypso [6]. "I'll tell you something I've never told you before!"
[2170] "Is that so?" Jonah was surprised, and then he hastily said, "You don't have to tell me, Helen. I'm not interested - I mean, if those words are about Megalas."
[2171] "Really?" Jonah was startled, and then he hastily said, "You don't have to tell me, Helen. I'm not interested—I mean, if those words are about Megalas."
[2172] "Don't be silly, Jonah. You know why Stephen's recent trip lasted an entire year outside?"
[2173] "I have no idea. Probably found a hula girl to his liking in Hawaii."
[2174] "Jonah! That's not fair. Stephen isn't that kind of person, you know that... I'll tell you, it's because he wanted me to marry him. There! That's why."
[2175] "Oh, is it? Well, that's a splendid reception for your fiancé—leaving for a year! I wish you both good luck."
[2176] Ellery sighed again, turning back down the path. For him, the night was still cold and silent. For Lincoln and Miss Bradley... quiet. Ellery thought he knew what was happening.

[2184] Chapter XXII: News from Abroad
[2185] "All the signs point to justice being served," Ellery said to Professor Yadley two days later, on Tuesday. "It's taking a break."
[2186] "What signs?"
[2187] "The frustrated police have some general indications. You know, I've spent my whole life with a cop[1]... Officer Vane, to put it politely, is at a loss. He can't find anything concrete, so he becomes a belligerent defender of the law. He chases people around, pushes his men into senseless, useless activities, yells at his friends, ignores his colleagues, and acts like a grumpy little Rollo[2] all the time.
[2188] The professor chuckled. "If I were you, I'd forget this case altogether. Take it easy, read 'The Iliad'[3], or some other epic like it. You're on the same boat as Vane, only you're dealing with it sinking a little more gracefully."
[2189] Ellery grunted, tossing his cigarette butt into the grass.
[2190] He felt regret; worse, he felt worried. His mind hadn't provided a logical solution to the case, which made him uneasy, but what truly troubled him was that the case seemed to be winding down without a resolution. Where was Crosetto? What was he waiting for?
[2191] Mrs. Bradley weeps for her sins in her bedroom. Jonathan Lincoln, forgetting his threatening words, returns to the Bradley-Megala office and continues to distribute carpets to the American market. Helen Bradley walks around, flushed with excitement, almost walking on air. Hester Lincoln packs up her belongings and heads to New York after a heated argument with Dr. Temple. Dr. Temple wanders around the Braidwoods, chewing on his pipe, with his dark eyes darker than ever.
[2192] Andrew Van hides in the mountains of West Virginia. Stephen Megala remains on his yacht; the crew, except for Captain Swift, has been paid and dismissed by Detective Wainwright. Megala's bodyguards – two detectives – lazily lounge on the deck, drinking and smoking, playing cards. Megala insists on dismissing them, saying he can take care of himself. However, the harbor police continue to patrol the channel.
[2193] An urgent telegram from Scotland Yard finally breaks the monotony:
[2194] Further investigation of Percy and Elizabeth Rein in England has yielded no results. Suggest coordination with European law enforcement.
[2195] So Detective Wainwright, as Ellery says, acts like a grumpy little Rolo. District Attorney Isam adopts a simple method of staying in his office and cleverly withdraws from the case. Ellery cools off in Professor Yadley's pool, reading the professor's excellent books, and thanks his various deities for having a holiday – both physical and mental – while still keeping a watchful eye on the big house across the street.
[2196] On Thursday morning, Ellery strolled to Bradwood and found Officer Vane sitting on the porch, fanning himself with a handkerchief tucked into his tanned neck and wrinkled collar, cursing the heat, the police, Bradwood, the case, and himself.
[2197] "Any progress, officer?"
[2198] "Nothing damn it!"
[2199] Helen Bradley emerged from the house, wearing a white cotton dress and looking as beautiful as a spring cloud. She murmured a good morning and descended the steps, turning towards the western path.
[2200] "I've been telling reporters all the old clichés," Vane complained. "Progress and such. This case will end up in a dead end because of these progresses, Mr. Quine. Where is Crosack?"
[2201] "A rhetorical question," Ellery frowned at his cigarette. "Frankly, I'm baffled. Has he given up? It doesn't seem likely. Madmen never give up. So why is he dragging his feet? Is he waiting until we think the case is hopeless and abandon it before acting?"
[2202] "You're joking," Vance muttered under his breath, then added, "I'll stay here until the end of the world."
[2203] They fell into silence. The tall figure of Fox in his corduroy trousers and shirt moved about in the garden, accompanied by the sound of a lawn mower.
[2204] The inspector suddenly stood up, surprising Ellery, who was half-closing his eyes and smoking. The sound stopped. Fox stood still, like a brave scout, looking west. He then dropped the lawn mower, took off running, leaping over a flower bed, and ran west.
[2205] The two men jumped up, and the inspector shouted, "Fox! What's going on?"
[2206] Fox didn't stop. He gestured toward the trees, shouting something they couldn't hear.
[2207] Later, they heard a faint scream, coming from the direction of the Rein residence.

[2209] When they rushed to the open space in front of Rein's house, they found Fox in front of them, kneeling on the grass, with his knees on the head of a person lying down. Helen's face was as pale as her blouse, her hands clutched at her chest, leaning over them.
[2210] "What happened?" Vance panted, "Oh, it's Temple!"
[2211] "He - I thought he was dead," Helen stuttered.
[2212] Dr. Temple lay there, limp, with his eyes closed, his dark face ashen, and a deep, red gash on his forehead.

[2215] Fox jumped up and rushed up the stone steps to Reine's house. Ellery and Vance gently lifted the still body and followed behind.

As they placed the unconscious man on a long couch, Helen hurried away and returned with a basin of water.

[2238] Temple's eyes filled with fear. "The head," he repeated slowly, "I never thought of that... no, I thought of something else."
[2239] Ellery quickly asked, "What?"
[2240] "It was a few years after the war, when I was released from the Austrian prisoner-of-war camp and roaming Europe, enjoying my freedom. In Budapest... well, I met a couple. We were staying at the same hotel. A guest, a German jeweler named Bondrein, was found tied up in his room, and a valuable batch of gems he was supposed to bring back to Berlin was missing. He accused the couple; but they disappeared... When I saw the Rein's here, I was almost certain they were the couple. They were then known as Traceton - Mr. Percy Traceton and his wife... Good heavens, my head. As soon as Rein appeared in front of me, disguised, I was certain it was him, just as easy as finding a first-class star with a telescope!"
[2241] "I can't believe it," Helen whispered, "such nice people! They were so good to me in Rome. Cultured, obviously wealthy, and charming..."
[2242] "If that's true," Ellery mused, "and the Reinke couple are the ones Temple is accusing, then they certainly had a good reason to be nice to you, Miss Bradley. For them, discovering you're the daughter of a millionaire from America would have been a piece of cake. Besides, if they committed the robbery in Europe..."
[2243] "Entertainment and business combined," the policeman said angrily, "I think you're right, doctor. They must have buried something. What happened this morning?"
[2244] Temple smiled faintly. "This morning? For the past two weeks, I've been quietly looking around. I've been looking for it... This morning I came over and finally confirmed where it was. I walked straight to the hiding place and started digging, when suddenly I looked up and found Reinke right in front of me. Then, like the sky falling, I knew nothing. I think Reinke, or Tracdon, or whatever name he goes by, has been watching me all along. As soon as he realized I was onto him, he knocked me out, dug up the loot, and ran off with his wife."
[2245] Temple insisted that he could walk, with Fox's help, and stumbled out of the house and into the woods. The others followed. They found that, only thirty feet into the woods, a hole with a gaping mouth, about a foot square, lay on the grass.

[2247] "Because I'm a fool," the doctor said gloomily, "I wanted to expose the whole thing. But I couldn't be sure then – I didn't want to accuse innocent people. I didn't want them to get away."
[2248] "Don't worry about that. I'll have them arrested tonight."
[2249] But it turned out that Inspector Vane was too optimistic. At nightfall, the Rein couple was still at large. The police had found no trace of them, nor of any couple resembling them.
[2250] "They must have split up and disguised themselves," Vane roared. He sent telegrams to Paris, Berlin, Budapest, and Vienna.
[2251] Friday came and went, with no news from the search for the missing British couple. Their descriptions, along with copies of their passport photos, were posted on thousands of bulletin boards across the country. The Canadian and Mexican borders were closely monitored. But the Reins proved once again how difficult it is to remove two ants from a nest as large as America.
[2252] "They must have had a well-prepared hideout to deal with such emergencies," Detective Inspector Vance said, looking glum, "but eventually we'll catch them. They can't hide forever."
[2253] Saturday morning, three telegrams arrived from abroad. One was from the Chief of Police in Paris:
[2254] A couple matching the description was wanted by the Paris police in 1925 for assault and robbery, named Mr. Percy Strong and his wife.
[2255] The second was from Budapest:
[2256] Traklund and his wife had been wanted by the Budapest police since 1920 for stealing jewelry and matching the description you provided.
[2257] The third, and most informative, was from Vienna:
[2258] The couple matching the description is named Percy and Beth Anlexter in Vienna, wanted for defrauding a French tourist of 5,000 francs and stealing precious jewelry last spring.
[2259] If the couple is apprehended by the American authorities, please extradite the unrecaptured stolen goods. Attached is a detailed description of the stolen jewelry.
[2260] The end.

[2275] Due to the formal dissolution of Montenegro as an independent state, archives are difficult to find, especially those from twenty years ago. The reports on the Twerly brothers and Vilga Krosak have been delayed. The authenticity of the two is without question, but the existence of the blood feud is being investigated by agents, and the outcome will be reported within two weeks.

[2283] Chapter 23: Emergency Meeting
[2284] On Sunday, Monday... It was obvious that progress was minimal, and they had very little concrete evidence from the murder. [2337] [1] A Rosetta Stone, discovered near the town of Rosetta in Egypt in 1799, has an inscription in three scripts: the ancient Egyptian hieroglyphs, demotic script, and Greek. The discovery of the Rosetta Stone provided a key to deciphering hieroglyphs.
[2338] [2] Jean-François Champollion (1790-1832), a French archaeologist, is considered the founder of Egyptology. He deciphered the hieroglyphs using the Rosetta Stone.
[2339] [3] The Ptolemaic dynasty, established by Ptolemy I in 305 BC, was the last dynasty of the Hellenistic Ptolemaic Kingdom of Egypt. It ended with the death of Ptolemy XV, in 30 BC, when it was conquered by Rome.

[2348] Chapter Twenty-Four: T Again
[2349] That morning, the group traveled silently from the mainland to the Helene. The silence was the result of the sudden and horrifying murder, a stunned silence. Ellery, with a face as white as his linen clothes, stood nervously at the railing of the patrol boat, staring at the yacht. Even before the boat rocked on the waves, he felt nauseous, his stomach tingling and quivering, and his mouth tasting bitter. Standing quietly beside him, the professor muttered over and over again, "Incredible. Terrible." Even the detectives accompanying them were depressed; they had been staring at the yacht's neat lines, as if they had never seen it before.
[2350] People were moving around on the deck. The center of activity seemed to be around the upper part of the ship's midsection; a small group of people stood there, and as the patrol boat approached and the county police and detectives climbed aboard, the whirlpool of people grew larger and larger.
[2351]Against the calm morning sky, the ghostly symbol was clearly outlined, the body dressed in bloody pajamas. It was stiffly tied to the first mast, not looking human at all, especially not like the lively, flesh-and-blood person who had spoken to them only twelve hours earlier. It mocked them from its elevated position; its legs, bound tightly to the pillars, were out of proportion to the human form; the terrible image of the body made it seem as if it had been exaggerated in size.
[2352]"The Christ of Calvary," Professor Yardley said mournfully, "Lord, it's unbelievable, unbelievable. His lips turned gray.
[2353]"I'm not religious," Ellery said slowly, "but for God's sake, Professor, don't blaspheme. Yes, it's unbelievable. You've read many ancient historical stories—about Caligula's, the Vandals', Moloch's, the Assassins', the Inquisition's. Mutilation, impaling, skinning... blood, blood-stained pages. You read... but it doesn't give you that intense, smoldering terror. Most of us can't understand the terrible fickleness of those madmen who enjoyed destroying the human body... in this world, in the twentieth century, despite wars and civil strife, Europe is still plagued by organized killings, but we don't have a clear concept of creating terror by murder."
[2354]"It's just a slogan," the professor said stiffly, "you don't understand, I don't understand. But I've heard stories about veterans..."
[2355]"It's something distant," Ellery murmured, "impersonal. Collective madness can never be as directly repulsive as personal madness like Satanism. Oh, damn, let's stop. I feel terrible."
[2356] The two men said nothing to each other until the police boat and the Helen were side by side, and they climbed up the ladder to the deck.
[2357] That morning, among all the people busy controlling the deck of the Helen, Officer Vane looked the least touched by the whimsical nature of the crime. For him, it was business - of course, terrible business, strange and bloody business, but within the scope of duty; if his eyes flickered and his mouth spat out venom, it was not because Stephen Meagher - whom he had stared into the eyes the night before - hung like a gruesome, incomplete wax figure from the mast, but because he was shocked by the extreme incompetence of his subordinates.

[2442] The place where Jesus Christ was crucified is located north of Jerusalem.
[2443] Caligula (12-41), Roman Emperor (37-41), was tyrannical and cruel, executed the Praetorian prefect who had raised him to power, massacred Jews, and was assassinated.
[2444] Molech, an ancient Phoenician deity, was worshipped by believers who offered children as burnt sacrifices.
[2445] The Assassins, a secret sect of Islam, was founded in Persia in 1090, regarded assassination as a means of eliminating opponents and a sacred duty. It was destroyed by the Mongol army in 1256.
[2446][5] Satan, a name in the Christian and Jewish Bibles for the devil who is an enemy of God and humanity. "Satanism" refers to the worship of Satan or other devils. The worship may include the misuse of religious rituals (such as mocking the Mass), the use of magic, and other various activities related to occultism. Satanism experienced a resurgence in the 19th century and has not completely disappeared to this day.
[2447][6] A secret society that was formed in Naples, Italy around 1820, but was later banned (1911) due to its involvement in terrorist activities. The term is now often used to refer to organizations of a similar nature.
[2448][7] Here it refers to 11:30. "Eight bell" is a method of telling time on a ship. After the first strike at 4:30, 8:30, and 12:30, each half-hour is struck with an additional strike, and at 4 o'clock, 8 o'clock, and 12 o'clock, it is struck eight times.
[2449] Chapter 25: The Lame Man
[2450] A detective came aboard the ship with a familiar envelope.

[2452] "Telegram, just arrived,"

[2621] Chapter 27: The Flight
[2622] Dusenberry labored through New York City, finally clearing the business district, rushing through the Holland Tunnel [1], dodging through traffic in Jersey City, racing through the labyrinthine towns of New Jersey, then shooting straight up the road to Harrisburg, speeding like an arrow. There were few vehicles on the road, and the storm showed no signs of abating; Elyot prayed to God for good luck while flagrantly violating speed limits. He was lucky, and he didn't get chased by motorcycle cops as he flew through town after town in Pennsylvania.
[2623] The old car, without any rain protection, was soaked; he was sitting inside, his shoes soaked, water dripping from his hat. He found a pair of racing goggles somewhere in the car. His linen clothes were covered in a raincoat, a light felt hat hung limply over his ears, amber-tinted goggles covered his bifocals, creating a ridiculous image. He shrugged and sat in the huge steering wheel, serious, driving like a bullet through the countryside of Pennsylvania.
[2624] At about seven o'clock that evening, the rain was still pouring down - as if following the storm - he drove into Harrisburg.
[2625] He hadn't eaten lunch, and his empty stomach gnawed at him. He parked the Dusenberry at a garage, gave specific instructions to the mechanic, and then strode off to find a restaurant. An hour later, he returned to the garage, checked the oil, gas, and tires, and continued driving out of town. He remembered the route well. Sitting behind the wheel, he was wet and cold and uncomfortable. In six miles, he drove through Rockville, flying straight ahead, crossed the Susquehanna River, and continued speeding. Two hours later, he drove through Lincoln Road, stubbornly continuing on the road. The rain was still pouring down relentlessly.
[2626] At midnight, he was cold and exhausted, unable to keep his eyes open, and drove into Hollidaysburg. The car repair shop was his first stop; after a lively conversation with a smiling mechanic, he walked to a hotel. Rain pelted his wet legs.
[2627] "I need three things," he said stiffly with his lips in the small hotel, "a room, to dry my clothes, and to be called at seven in the morning. Can you do that?".
[2628] "Mr. Quine," the receptionist said, looking at Ellery's signature in the register, "You can count on it."
[2629] The next morning, Ellery was feeling quite lively, wearing dry clothes and his stomach full of bacon and eggs, and drove the Dorsenbeg forward with a roar, embarking on his final journey. The signs of the storm raged before his eyes: uprooted trees, swollen rivers, damaged vehicles left by the roadside. But the storm raged all night, and in the morning it suddenly subsided, despite the leaden sky still hanging low.
[2630] At 10:15 a.m., Ellery drove the rumbling Dorsenbeg through Pittsburgh. At 11:30 a.m., under a bright sky, the sun made a valiant effort, illuminating the peaks of Allegheny Mountains, and Ellery stopped the Dorsenbeg in front of the town hall in Aroo Village, West Virginia.
[2631] A man in a blue gingham shirt was sweeping the sidewalk in front of the town hall, and Ellery dimly remembered him.
[2632] "Hey, buddy," the man dropped his broom and tried to grab Ellery's arm as he ran past, "where are you going? Who are you going to see?"
[2633] Ellery didn't answer. He rushed through the dim hall to the rear office of Judge Lutten. The door to the judge's office was closed; as he saw, the fortress of Aroon residents was devoid of life. He tried the door, and it was unlocked.
[2634] The man in the blue gingham shirt, with a rough face and a stubborn look, slowly followed him.
[2635] The office of Judge Lutten was empty.
[2636] "Where is the judge?" Ellery asks.

[2638] "Ah!" Ellery says, nodding sharply. So, Ludden went into the mountains. "When did the judge leave?"
[2639] "What!?" Ellery's voice is filled with surprise, sadness, and despair. "Oh, no, he didn't get my -" He rushes to Judge Ludden's desk. There is a pile of messy papers on the desk. Ellery begins to toss through the judge's official letters – if they are official letters, the man in blue tries to stop him, but it's to no avail. So, as he feared, it lies there, a yellow envelope of a telegram.

[2641] West Virginia, Aroon, Sheriff Redden

[2643] Ellery Queen
[2644] A panoramic vision unfolded before Ellery's eyes. Due to a terrible oversight, the wheel of fate had turned, and his telegram to Redden was as good as unsent. The man in the checkered shirt patiently explained that the sheriff and the town's mayor, Matt Hollis, had left two days ago for their annual fishing trip; they would be gone for a week, camping out in the wilderness, fishing in the Ohio River and its tributaries. They wouldn't be back until Sunday. The telegram had arrived the day before, a few minutes past three; the man in the checkered shirt—who claimed to be the caretaker, housekeeper, and handyman—had received the telegram, signed it, and placed it on the sheriff's desk, where it might have sat for a week had Ellery not arrived. The caretaker seemed to have something on his mind, launching into a rambling discourse, but Ellery pushed him aside, his eyes filled with a vague fear, and returned to Aroon Street, jumping into his Duesenberg car.
[2645] He revved the engine, turned the corner, and drove along the route he remembered from his previous adventure with Esam and Sheriff Redden. There was no time to contact the county prosecutor, Krutem, or the state police's Captain Pike. If the thing he feared hadn't happened yet, he was sure he could handle any situation that might arise; he had a loaded semi-automatic pistol in the pocket of his Duesenberg. If it had already happened…
[2646] He parked the car in a clump of bushes - despite the rain, the faint traces of his previous visit remained on the ground covered with small shrubs and grass - holding the gun in his hand, he began the arduous ascent along the barely visible path that Royo had led before. He moved cautiously and quickly forward; he didn't know what he might encounter, determined to respond to any sudden attack. The dense forest was silent. He crept forward silently, praying that he would arrive in time, but the faint alarm bell in his head made him realize that it was already too late.
[2647] He crouched behind a tree, peering out at the clearing in the woods. There were no signs of tampering with the fence. Although the front door was closed, this gave Ellery a glimmer of hope, but he didn't dare take the risk. He opened the safety on his gun and silently emerged from behind the tree. Was that familiar old Pitt's bushy-bearded face at the window with the wire mesh? No, it was just his imagination. He clumsily vaulted over the fence, still holding his weapon.
[2648] He noticed the footprints on the ground. He stood there for three minutes, silently contemplating the story told by the footprints in the damp earth. Then, avoiding these revealing traces, he circled around carefully, stepping lightly, and came to the door.
[2649] He now noticed that, unlike what he had initially thought, the door was not fully closed, and he could see a narrow slit.
[2650] He held his gun in his right hand, bent down, and put his ear to the crack in the door, listening for any sounds within. There were none. He straightened up, hit the door with his left hand, and it opened quickly, revealing the interior…
[2651] For a moment, he stood there, his left hand raised halfway, his weapon aimed at the terrible sight inside.
[2652] He then jumped over the threshold and firmly closed the heavy door behind him.
[2653] At 12:50, Dusenberry made a sharp sound and stopped in front of the town hall again, and Ellery got off the sidewalk. The doorman must have thought the young man was strange, with his hair disheveled and his eyes sparkling with madness. He grabbed the doorman as if to hit him.

[2676] Chapter Twenty-Eight: The Man Who Died Twice
[2677] At eight o'clock on Tuesday morning, Officer Wain and District Attorney Esam drove into Bradwood, tired but pleased. With them was a man from the U.S. Attorney's office. Sitting in the back were Percy and Elizabeth Rein, both looking grim and defiant.
[2678] These two British thieves were being escorted to Minola. As Officer Wain's deputy, Bill, ran up and began talking rapidly, the officer's relaxed expression faded and was replaced by one of worry. After listening to Professor Adler's account of the whole affair, Esam cursed impatiently.
[2679] "What the hell are we going to do now?"

[2691] Q.
[2692] "It's happened," Escham sighed.

[2698] Esam and Vance struggled over the barbed wire fence. "Don't walk on those footprints," the officer said immediately, "Let's see... those winding footprints, I think they're Quin's. The others -"
[2699] They stood still, following the footprints that Ellery had noticed an hour ago. There were two sets of footprints made by the same pair of shoes; there were no others except Ellery's. These two sets of footprints were clearly distinguishable: one set led from the fence to the door of the shack; the other presented an arc. On the other side of the wire fence, the rocky ground did not allow for footprints to be left.
[2700] "Limping footprints," Vance grumbled, "The first set of footprints - strange." He walked forward, opening the door, with Esam following behind.
[2701] The two men stared in horror at the scene before them.
[2702] On the wall opposite the door, a man's body hung like a trophy on a rough-hewn log. It had no head and its legs were nailed together. From the bloodstained, tattered clothes it wore – the garb of the fake mountaineer – it was the body of the unfortunate schoolmaster.
[2703] Blood dripped onto the stone floor, splashing onto the wall. The shabby hut, which had been so clean and comfortable when Easom had visited before, now looked like a slaughterhouse. Stains of varying shades of red dotted the straw mats, and the floor was covered in red streaks and blotches. All the items that had been on the table were gone, and the sturdy old table had been turned into a makeshift writing board, with a large letter – the familiar mark of Crosetto's revenge – a capital T, written in blood.
[2704] "Good God," Vaughn muttered, "it's disgusting. If I caught him, I'd strangle the cannibal with my bare hands, regardless of whether it's legal or not."
[2705] "I'm going outside," Easom said hoarsely. "I feel – dizzy. He stumbled through the doorway, leaning against the outside wall and retching.
[2706] Officer Vain blinked and straightened his shoulders. He avoided the drying pool of blood, walking across the room. He touched the body, which was stiff, and a small red stream seeped from the heads of the nails on his palms and feet.
[2707] "Dead for about fifteen hours," Vain thought, clenching his fist. As he looked up at the body nailed to the cross, his face paled. Where the head had been was a gaping, bloody hole, the arms stretched stiffly, the legs drawn together—a grotesque, mad work of art.
[2708] Vain shook his head, shaking off the dizziness, and turned back. He sluggishly thought that there must have been a struggle, as several items lay on the ground near the table, telling a terrifying story. The first was a heavy axe, its handle and blade stained with dried blood, clearly the weapon used to sever Andrew Tewell's head. The second was a roll of bandage, like a flat doughnut, frayed and dirty, one side soaked in brownish liquid, now dried. The officer bent down carefully to pick up the roll of bandage; as he did, it unfurled, revealing that it had been cut by a sharp object. Scissors, Vain guessed; he looked around and found, a few steps away, a heavy pair of shears lying on the ground, as if hastily thrown there.
[2709] Vain approached the door; Esam, though pale and weary, had recovered somewhat. "What does this look like to you?" Vain asked, holding up the broken roll of bandage. "Good heavens, you picked a bad time to get sick, Esam!"
[2710] The district attorney wrinkled his nose, looking pitiful. "A bandage on the wrist," he stammered. "From the bloodstains and iodine, it was a serious wound."
[2711] "You're right," Vain says sternly, "from the way it's wrapped, it must have been around the wrist. There's no other part of the body that fits that size, not even the ankles. I'm afraid Crocq has a little scratch on his wrist!"
[2712] "Or there was a struggle, or he hurt himself while—while butchering the body," Escham ventures, shivering, "but why did he leave the bandage for us to find?"
[2713] "It's not hard to explain. With so much blood on it, the wound must have been left earlier in the struggle or whatever it was. So he cut off the first bandage and put on a new one... As for why he left it behind—he left in a hurry when he left the shack, Escham. I believe he was in no real danger. The fact that he left the bandage behind indicates that the wound was covered up somewhere. The sleeve could hide it. Let's go back inside."
[2714] Escham takes a deep breath and, bravely, follows the officer back into the shack. Vain points to the axe and scissors, then to a large opaque bottle lying on the ground near the spot where he found the bandage. The bottle is deep blue glass, unlabeled, nearly empty, and the liquid inside has stained the ground brown. The cork has landed a few feet away. It lies next to a ball of bandage, part of which is not wrapped.
[2715] "Iodine," Vain says, "that explains everything. After being injured, he took it from the medicine cabinet over there and put it on the table, and then accidentally knocked it over, or perhaps just threw it to the ground in anger. The bottle is thick glass and didn't break."
[2716] They walked to the wall where the body was hanging; a few feet away from the edge, above the basin-like device and the pump rod, was the shelf that Esham had noticed during his last visit. [2717] "That's clear enough," the officer said grimly, "he used Ran's drugs. The bandage and the large bottle of iodine came from Ran's shelf, and he didn't bother to put them back."
[2718] "Wait a minute," Esham frowned and said, "you've been assuming that the one who was cut was Crosetti. But what if the one with the wrist wound is the poor guy hanging on the wall. Can't you see it, Vance? If the one with the wrist wound isn't Crosetti, but Ran, then we're making a mistake, because we're looking for Crosetti based on the feature of the wrist wound."
[2719] "You're not so stupid," Vance shouted, "I never thought of that. Okay! There's only one thing to do - see the body."
[2720] "Oh, dear," Esham sighed, shivering, "I - I don't want to, Vance."
[2721] "Listen," Wain roared, "I don't like this job any more than you do. But someone has to do it. Come on."
[2722] Ten minutes later, the headless corpse lay on the ground. They had pulled out the nails from the palms and feet. Wain had cut off the tattered clothes from the corpse, leaving it naked and pale, a mockery of God's creation. Escham leaned against the wall, pressing his hands against his stomach. It was only Wain who was struggling to examine the grotesque, naked corpse, searching for wounds.
[2723] "No," he said, standing up, "no wounds, except for the nail holes in the palms and feet. So it was Croce who was injured."
[2724] "Let's get out of here, Wain. Come on."
[2725] They returned to Arroyo in silence, breathing deeply the unpolluted air. Wain found a phone in town and called the county seat of Wiltton. He talked to the district attorney, Krümmit, for five minutes, hung up the phone, and returned to Escham.
[2726] "Krummite will keep it secret," he said sternly. "He's shocked! But it won't leak, that's all I care about. He's bringing Captain Pickert here, as well as the coroner. I told him we made some unauthorized handling of the latest corpse in Nacogdoches County."
[2727] Escham said nothing, still feeling nauseous. They rented a fast car - an hour and a half late - and raised the same dust cloud, heading towards the Ohio River, the Ohio Bridge, and Stowberville.
[2728] Challenge to the reader
[2729] Who is the murderer?
[2730] By the time you read this passage, you should have all the necessary facts to solve the case correctly. It is my habit to challenge the reader's intellect at this point in the novel. The Egyptian Cross Mystery is no exception: Using strict logic and reasoning, you should now be able to not only guess but also prove the identity of the criminal.
[2731] There is nothing "if" and "but" in the only appropriate solution, which you will discover when reading the chapter on explanation. Although logical reasoning does not require the help of luck, I wish you good reasoning and good luck!
